THE RED PAGE.
Eve Triumphant .
THE year in which the Australian worn
have won equal franchise rights with m
in the Federal and most of the State Leg
latures is a fitting occasion for a review o
the progress (or retrogression) of woman in H
t A°, ry : Triumphant ” (put into English
Aljs Hal lard), and published this year, gi\
another hint to look back upon Woman and 1
works from the Flood to the Franchise, and see
amid a great confusion of facts, some thread o
purpose.
Generalising broadly, and asking leave to ignt
occasional isolated circumstances which may set
to conflict with the theory, it would appear tha
woman's progress and achievement up to t
19th century were on the lines suggested by ma
ideal of woman ; that the 20th century may see t
experiment of Woman developing along womai
idea of herself. Literature is the one faith I
mirror in which the facts as to past ages can
seen reflected through the centuries. Lookin
therein, it is plain enough that at no period o
which mankind has any certain cognisance die
woman fail to win, with the advance of civili.
tion, certain very valuable “ rights ” or, rathe
‘‘privileges.” The Homeric gallerv contai
many pictures of women—Penelope, Helen, A
dromache, the nurse Eurycleia, Nausicaa— wl
in exalted or humble stations, received a hig
respect and a marked deference ; whose opinio
were, often sought and followed ; whose positic
m life was one of dignity and power. T
Hebrew chronicles are no less rich in instances o:
women being allowed to take an active and
spectable part in the affairs of the nation. T
more dim and uncertain Asiatic and German]
records disclose here and there a worna
or women, sharing to a very larg
extent in the control of social and nation
matters; and the position of women in t
Egyptian civilisation was a very high one. In tl
more modern Grecian and Roman times, woman’s
“ progress ” continued, and the occurrence of a
Aspasia shows that there was no objection on th
part of man to permitting an occasional woma
even to share his political power.
But it will be seen on close examination that
all these cases woman developed and progressec
as man’s woman, not as herself. Helen is glorioi
because she is the acme of man’s desire ; Andro
mache, as mother of the hero’s child ; Penelope
because of her faithfulness to her husband’s in
terests ; Nausicaa, because of her maiden care fo
household duties and her frank aspiration fo
marriage with a hero; Aspasia, as the consor
whose mental gifts enable her to aid the ambition
of Pericles. Every privilege or right that woma
vas conceded was given by man as the admirin
reward of her fidelity to him, of her good servic
to him, of her recognition that her mission was t
minister to him. Even when a woman mountec
the throne it was because she had been daughte
or consort to some great man, and promised to
continue his policy better than any male available
Wherever woman sought to depart from wha
man regarded as the proper aim (and that was “to
play for his hand ”), her presumption was as
sternly punished as was the misdoing of th
maids of Ithaca, who were “strung up to th
rafters, like so many sparrows, to dry.”
Christianity, it has been often argued, ohangec
all that, and woman and man, with equal sou
value, came to be considered as with equal earth
rights. But, notwithstanding any such soul
value theory, there is no proof that, either in
teaching or in practice, the new religion o
civilisation made the slightest difference ir
woman’s position. What there is of positive evi
dence seems to suggest that Christianity accentu
atsd the idea that woman was for man and not for
herself. From the Adam-and-Eve incident to the
direct injunction of Paul, “wives be subject to
your husbands ” thefe is much in the Bible to dis-
courage and prohibit the idea of sex-equality.
Nor did the Age of Chivalry, which produced
“the Romance of the Rose,” and represented
humble love of a woman as the chief glory of a
man, make any real difference in woman’s posi-
tion. She was set up for adoration certainly, anc
knights did most curious penances for her favor,
but man’s idol was of the man’s own making, wor-
shipped with much mystic sensuousness merely to
give a sharper, finer zest to his love affairs, and
most unceremoniously dethroned if she departed
one whit from the rules of the game as man wanted
it played.
It would have been not natural if it had been
otherwise. Man evolved from lower types mainly
through the subjection of the female to the
fostering protection of the male. If in any of the
pre-Homo types the females tried to plav a lone
hand, those types certainly disappeared ; the
mother could not survive in the struggle for
existence without some protection from the male.
Those types flourished best wherein the male was
most careful of the female during motherhood,
until Man was evolved with his chivalry, the very
essence of which was the idea of female subjection
and helplessness. The Middle Ages was certainly
too early a date to expect the instinct implanted
by evolution to be nullified by any arguments of
abstract reasoning.
Very recently, indeed, was the claim for sex-
equality made—that claim which marks an
entirely new step in the history of the human
race. Almost as soon as made it has been con-
ceded in Australia, by the granting of the franchise
to women, a concession which will probably mean
nothing practical for years to come, but yet which
has a startling racial significance. Woman
now is given the right to shape her own destinies.
Hitherto she has only had the right to consider
the means by which she could make herself most
pleasant to man. Now, with full political indepen-
dence, she has the right (whether she exercises it
or not) to make herself unpleasant to man, not
only as an individual but as a class. This marks a
complete revolution in human thought on the sex
question, and a sublime confidence that the ten-
dencies implanted by thousands of centuries of
evolution have at last been destroyed. Whether
it is to have any real effect on the world’s social
relations is a point which one may be permitted
to strongly doubt. Probably the Suffrage will not
be used by woman (as it might be used) to assert
in a practical way her independence of man.
Woman will at the polling booth, as elsewhere, be
man s woman still, and remain faithful to the
instincts which have been graved into her by so
many centuries of time. If not—if woman votes
and legislates as woman's woman—there will be
-some interesting developments for the next few
generations to watch. The overlordship of man
is not an essential condition of community exist-
ence (though it seems to be an essential condition
of present civilised existence); among the bees
and the ants, for instance, the male is but a very
poor creature : Humanity may start to work on
bee-lines.
Meanwhile, in “Eve Triumphant,” a story
which describes the experiences of a young
American matron and an American maid in
European society, woman may learn how very
lit le she has to gain (from her own present stand-
point) by ceasing to be man’s woman and being
tier own woman. In U.S.A. the privileges and
rig,its of woman (man-conceded, man-protected)
are greater than anywhere else in the world.
Apparently she rules the land (though she has not
the franchise), and man is but her slave. Really,
of course, the Yankee is only practising in a
rn°i\ ern orni le chivalry of Arthur’s Round
I able, and bows so deeply and humbly to his idol
because it adds to his sensuous happiness to do so.
lie glories and exults in his show of humility, and
is an abject in the house of the Lord he creates
from choice. The practical situation, however, is
that the American woman gets without question
ail that her woman’s mind suggests to her that she
wants. For her the Yankee trusts and combines
aud wrecks and floats. For her the luxuries of
two hemispheres are piled up. But all the
time she is Man’s woman, the real subject of her
apparent worshipper. Yet, as present woman
ideas go, the American woman enjoys the acme of
well-being. There will need to be a wonderful
change, not in man’s but in woman’s mind, before
anything that the franchise can give, that the full
recognition of sex-equality can give, will make her
as happy as she is, with her triumphant feminity,
in that land where man does the work that woman
may enjoy the pleasure.
F.R.
Rudyard Kipling.
THERE could not be imagined a more plain
advertisement of the decline of literary
criticism in England than the appearance
of a third edition of “Rudyard Kipling
an appreciation, ” by G. F. Monkshood and George
Gamble, in “the English Writers of To-day”
series. Not so very long ago it was the fashion to
criticise a writer as a writer. In this book, which
is typical of current British criticism of Kipling,
he is glorified as a great writer because of his
achievements as a politician and as a preacher
Kiplmg is “ one of the greatest friends of the
British. Empire that have ( sic) stepped to the
ln t “ s our da y ” (again sic, very sic).
Who can rouse us with greater force into sharp,
clear, useful discussion of the vital things of
our Empire?” And much to the same effect,
arguing seemingly, that the great literary
y alu e ofKiphng is that he urges England
to double the Death Duties and double the
Royal INavy. There is one bit of genuine humor
m the book—a preface embodying a letter by Mr.
Kiplmg, sent in response to an advance copy and
a request for “a prefaeial note” (again sic).
Rudyard Kiplmg wrote :
I have read your type-written book with a good deal of
interest, and I confess that I greatly admire your enthu-
siasm. But does it not seem to you that a work of this
kind would be best published after the subject were
dead i
There are so many ways in which a living man can fall
from grace that, were I you, I should be afraid to put so
much enthusiasm into the abidingness of print until 1
was very sure of my man.
Which is a very neat snub indeed to these
enthusiastic and ungrammatical adorers, not so
m V ch of the literary value as of the Imperial
value of Mr. Kipling.
The time has not yet come for an accurate
appraisement of Kipling’s work. The one thing
certain is that the last few years have been years
of steady deterioration. With the one flash (not
sovery great a flash) of merit in the “Recessional,”
Kipling’s writing, since he became politician and
preacher, is absolutely worthless as literature.
The earlier work—“ Plain Tales from the Hills,”
“Departmental Ditties,” “Barrack-roomßallads,”
“The Jungle Book,” and various collections of
short stories—is on another plane altogether. But
perhaps the final judgment will be that Kipling
was a supremely able journalist, an intensely vital
observer and recorder of the surfaces of things—
and no more.

Carlyle’s view of a Coronation ceremony:
“ Often, when I read of pompous ceremonials, drawing-
room levees, and Coronations, on a sudden the clothes fly
off the whole party in my fancy, and they stand there
straddling m a half-ludicrous, half-horrid condition.”

Andrew Lang in Longman’s Magazine :
“It is women who write ‘daring’ books, and it is
women who read them. When once the fair do take off
their bonnets, they are not content with that, but
‘throw them over the windmills,’ as the French say
Men do not like this sort of thing. It not only disgusts
but bores them. It is a particular kind of woman, usually
earnest and stupid, who enjoys these exercises. Our sex
if this continues, must provide itself with fans to blush
behind. We must leave the ladies before the coffee and
cigarettes begin to circulate.”
The wrangle is finally
settled at last! Somebody, entering quietly into
the raging cipher-theory controversy in London,
says the plays were written neither by the crimi-
nal William, nor by the judicial Erancis, but by
one Bernard Shaw, and calmly points to the
fourth last letter in the titles of eleven of the
plays in support and proof of his statement.

In an English mag., The Reformer (edited by
Hypatia Brad laugh Bonner, daughter of Charles
Brad laugh) appears an interesting article by
Edward Carpenter on “Walt Whitman’s Chil-
dren.” It tells something new about the old
poet, not to be found in his own writings or any
of the books that have been written about him.
Whitman in his later life lived at Camden City,
United States—a lonely old man, with no closer
visthle ties than those of a brother and that
brother’s wife. There is slight clue in Whitman's
two published volumes to personal relations of any
kind—the most direct:
I loved a certain person ardently, and my love was not
returned ;
Yet out of that love I have written my songs.
That ooints more to a life-long bachelorhood than
anything else. Still, in some parts of “ Children of
Adam ” one can hardlv avoid theconclusion that he
is describing an actual bit of his own history as in
the lines: —
Once I passed through a populous city imprinting
my brain for future use with its shows, architecture, cus-
toms, traditions.
Yet now of all that city I remember only a woman I
casually met there, who detained me for love of me.
Day by day and night by night we were together—all
else has long been forgotten by me.
I remember only that woman who passionately clung
to me.
Again we wander, we love, we separate again.
Again she holds me hv the hand, f must not go.
T see her close beside me with silent lips, sad and
tremulous.
Edward Carpenter says that “in a life so full and
rich as Whitman’s there must have been many
such personal experiences of which the world
knows nothing and will know nothing.” But it
was not until a few months before his death, in
a letter to W. ,T. Addington Symons, that Whit-
man made any definite statement, although he
appears to have told his friends that he had
children. This letter bears date August 16, 1890,
and Symons quotes the part relating to the
children, in a letter to Edward Carpenter, written
in 1893, as follows :
My life, young manhood, mid-age, times South, &c.,
have been jolly bodily, and doubtless open to criticism.
Though unmarried, I have had six children—two are
dead—one living Southern grandchild, fine boy, writes
to me occasionally--circumstances (connected with their
fortune and benefit) have separated me from intimate
relations.
Whitman died shortly afterwards. That was his
death-bed confession.
' * The late Alexander Sutherland, in collaboration
with his brother, was the first to write a school
history of Australia. The book is written in a
good, plain style, and, from an Australian stand-
point, will be ranked of more importance than his
“Origin and Growth of the Moral Instinct,”
which is rather a contribution to Scottish philo-
sophy. At the present time there is practically
no instruction given in our schools of the history
of our land. Children usually begin history with
the accession of William the Conqueror and work
downwards, the majority of them knowing more
about the Norman Conquest than about Eureka.
Clearly it would be of more value, both from the
standpoint of national ideals and of mere useful
knowledge, to begin to teach them the history of
the land of their birth. Then knowledge of Aus-
tralian history would enter from the first into the
texture of the national mind, and in time influence
the national literature. The average young Aus-
tralian learns the history first of England, then of
Rome and Greece, perhaps (in an R.C. school)
that of Ireland, and last, if at all, that of Aus-
tralia. It should be Australia first, and the rest
as time permits.
The New Idea is the latest Australian publi-
cation, advertised as “ a home journal for women,”
and run by Mr. T. Shaw Fitchett, the son of an
energetic and reverend father. It’s not a new
idea at all, only the old one of reprinting snip
matter. There’s hardly an original par. in the
whole publication ; but it gives a big budget for
threepence, and may compete successfully with the
imported Home Notes, Home Chat, and the like.
“In the Bush Shade,” by Henry Lambert
Thompson (Geo. Robertson and Co., publishers),
prints in neat, pretty binding some neat, pretty
verses, no one of which can claim any reason for
surviving the fugitive publicity of a newspaper.
“The poet sings, as the nightingale, because he
must sing,” writes a critic ; and certainly that is
when the poet should sing, and only then—when
he wishes to express a thought too lofty or too
dainty for prose. Those who depart from that
rule must always face the question, “ But why ? ”
If, then, they can plead some exceptional grace of
versification, combined with some flavor of
original thought, a license is allowed to sing—as
minor poets. But Mr. Thompson can hardly
claim even that sort of a license. He rhymes anil
scans irreproachably, but never writes anything
approaching to “poetry,” never ventures on a
daring image. This, as a fair specimen, about
Tasmania:
Isle most blessed of all the Southern world,
For God hath blessed thee with benignant hand,
And sent his favor wide throughout the land ;
In thy fair orchards apples close are curled
In heaps luxurious on the branches swirled.
In spreading wealth and rosy by the wand
Of fairy sun, all berries too expand,
And in thy fields the ripened grain is furled.
Pretty? Yes, pretty-pretty.
“Travels in West Australia,” by May Vivienne
(from George Robertson and Co., Sydney), is the
fruit of an evidently most industrious and a dis-
tinctly intelligent observation of life in the great
Western colony. The lady has collected her
material with great diligence, has enriched it
with sortie very interesting photographs, and the
publishers have done their part well, with clear
print, good paper, and admirable etching. The
net result is a “ chatty ” impression of the gold-
fields and coastal settlements of W.A., the abo-
rigines, the flora and fauna, and so on. There is
no attempt at criticism—no attempt to dive
beneath the fair surface show to see what is grim
beneath. The writer looks only on the rosy side
of things; and her work, therefore, probably gives
an inaccurately optimistic impression of the State
of W.A. But, allowing for that, the information
it gives with pictures and letterpress is undoubtedly
Valuable. There is now and again a useful hint—
for instance, that the boronia flower should be
utilised for perfume-making. What is said of
that might also be said of the pittosporum of the
East Coast and many of the wattles. But Aus-
tralia learns very slowly how to utilise her
resources.

“ Henslowe’s Annual ” for 1902 shows a distinct
improvement on previous numbers, though there
is still within the-covers much that is very crude,
both in pictures and reading matter. As the
“ Annual ” has reached its third year, it seems to
have good promise of permanence.

An English publishing house, after printing a
harmless domestic book (title of which might be
twisted into an obscenity), was perplexed by a
curiously persistent demand from one source. It
was finally discovered that this buyer advertised
the book extensively in sporting and other jour-
nals. His method was simplicity itself. The title
given, he would add: “Posted secure for 25.,
safely sealed.”

Some Correspondents—
A.l’eto : Re “A. M.’s ” article on Democracy, Whitman
" as done more than amplify Aristophanes’ ideas. Aristo-
phanes, Heine, Ibsen hack off the leaves, twigs and
branches ; Whitman digs below the ground, and hews up
the roots of the tree of decadent democracy. In effect,
Whitman says: “Of what use are your social reforms
unless the protoplasm which goes to build up the heart
and brain of each individual democrat be healthy and
sound t ” Browning, in “ Aristophanes’ Apology,” aptly
sums up the reason why Aristophanes failed, and why
Athens, who accepted his teaching in place of that of the
Sophists, fell. Browning’s criticism of the comedian
might be a warning to those modern journals which fol-
low Aristophanes’ methods, satirise ideals, and scoff at
religion, while they themselves have no higher ideals or
better religion to put before Democracy. Religion (in
the true sense—l do not mean anthropomorphic Deities,
Popes, Bishops, and such curiously dressed dolls of .
humanity) is an essential note of all great literature ;
and yet it is the lacking note in Australian literature.
i, ?Y;?‘ Writing on “The Critics of Democracy,”
A.M. (Red Page, 19/7/’O2), says of the comedies of
Aristophanes: “It is not encouraging to democrats of
to-day to read those old satires of the faults and follies of
centuries ago, in view of the fact that the satires failed.
Now, the same conditions recurring, bring up new'
satirists. Will they, too, speak and write in vain?”’
Assuredly they will, unless somebody does something
more than satirise, prophesy, and exhort. The decay
of democracies, old and new, must have a cause, to
discover and remove which must be the aim of every
wise democrat. “A.M.” makes a good guess at it:
“ A democracy seems to flourish only so long as simplicity
of manners and customs prevails. When it becomes
wealthy it begins to degenerate.” And yet one of Kleon’a
baits for degenerate Demos is this: “I feed you and
clothe you, and contrive, by fair means or foul, that you
have your 3-obol piece a day.” Which suggests that the
poverty, and not the wealth, of Demos is the cause of his
corruption. The fact is, of course, that in all democra-
cies up to date increase of wealth has been accompanied
by greater and greater inequality in its distribution,
bringing the proletariat into a condition in which they
have nothing to sell but their votes—price a 3-obol piece
a day, with employment as a detail of the bargain. Thence
Demos’ loss of self-respect. Thence his sensitiveness to
flattery, his carelessness of public morality, and his ignor-
ance, which is the security of the Kleons, Seddons and
John Sees. Demos is not‘to be blamed. “All that a
man hath will he give for his life,” said Job’s Adversary,
who had a wide, if not deep, knowledge of human nature.
The problem of Democracy is, therefore, to find for Demos
something he can sell besides his vote—his labor.

Long and Weary Suffering.
Rheumatic Fever and a Torpid Liver.
An Eight Years Permanent Cure.
The Case of Mrs. H. R. LAWLER.
(BY A SYDNEY REPORTER).
Every scientific discovery means the
introduction of some commodity for the
purpose of rendering a service to the
multitude of its users, and as the public’s
ready appreciation of everything possessing
indisputable merit is observable on all sides,
as will be noticed by the remarks of M rs.
Hester Rose Lawler, of No. 4 Broadstairs-
street, Balmain, it is considered necessary
to place the following narrative before the
general reader.
Mrs. Lawler’s introductory statement
was:—“Several years before I married I
was taken ill with rheumatic fever. All my
joints swelled up and were most painful, my
knees being particularly bad. My head was
also terribly swollen, besides being so
afflicted with severe pains that I scarcely
knew what I was doing half my time. The
doctor gave me some medicine which caused
a burning sensation in my chest, and I felt
so distressed after taking each dose that I
asked him to change it, but he said it was
the best medicine he could give me.”
Reporter : “Was there no ease whatever
derivable from the treatment he gave you ?”
Mrs. Lawler: “ Not the slightest.
Previous to my attack I was an unusually
strong woman, capable of working hard all
day without showing any signs of fatigue,
but for four years afterwards I was subject
to such sudden seizures of intense pains and
faintness that in the middle of any light
task I was often compelled to leave the
work undone and lie down for the rest of
the day. With my rheumatism came a
complete collapse of the system, everything
seeming to go wrong at once. The terrific
agonies running through my body kept me
awake at nights, so that when I ought to
have been getting up I felt so dead beat aud
depressed that I had no ability or desire to
do anything. Thus the days and months
dragged wearily on, and my condition was
getting worse all the time. My appetite
was in a very bad way too, fori seldom wanted
any food, and if I took a little it did not do
me any good because it would not digest.”
Reporter : “ How could you tell that ? ”
Mrs. Lawler: “ Because I felt almost
stifled through having a heavy pain in the
chest for one thing, and my stomach felt as
unsettled as if I had drunk a cupful of
yeast. Sometimes the food turned positively
sour and came up again, but before it was
vomited I always suffered acutely from
internal pains. Biliousness gave my mouth
such a nasty taßte that retching used to
come on, and what an awful time I had with
sick headaches ! They made my eyes feel
swollen, and my sight was bad. The
swollen state of my ankles and feet often
prevented me from putting my shoes on,
fet, in a way, this did not matter much, as
was generally too weak to walk about.
There was also something radically wrong
with my shoulders, as I could feel a sharp
pain between them which gave me more
annoyance than enough, and the aches in my
loins were even worse. Suffering so long
and seeing myself going down hill year after
year at last made me utterably miserable,
and fears about the future were constantly
thrusting themselves upon my mind.”
Reporter: “ Are you naturally of a
nervous temperament ? ”
Mrs. Lawler : “ Not by any means ; but
when I was ill my nervous system was
greatly weakened, and if anything startled
mo I could feel them jumping as though
they had been cut loose. People say that
life is sweet under any circumstances, but I
used to think I would rather die than live
thisp unbearably painful existence any
longer. Up to this time I could never
understand why people should look at the
black side of everything, and allow them-
selves to think of ail sorts of things that are
going to happen; but that is exactly the
frame of mind I was in, so it must be ill-
health which accounts for such unhappiness.
Anyway, I was too weak and weary to
trouble much about anything, but, thank
God ! all that has passed, and for eight years
I have been free from all the tormenting
symptoms which were bearing me down to
the grave.”
Reporter : “ You had a change in your
treatment, I presume ? ”
Mrs. Lawler: “ You may depend I had,
or else I would not be able to speak to you
as I have done, and it was a friend who
gave the advice which resulted in the
obliteration of my maladies. He was a
friend of my husband’s, and he said he could
recommend something that would do me
good. *lt is Clements Tonic,’ he said, ‘ and
you cannot go wrong if you take it, because
it cured me when I was in the same state
as you are in now.’ Dubious as 1 was
through losing confidence in previous medi-
cines, I purchased some Clements Tonic,
and before going any further I would like
to state that it was the only medicine which
did me any good at all. The revivifying
effects of Clements Tonic were nothing short
of miraculous, as two or three bottles set
new vigour coursing through my weakened
system, and I passed from a long period of
melancholy hopelessness to a life of comfort
and cheerfulness. It took a little time, of
course, but the fact remains that Clements
Tonic gradually braced me up and started
to ease my pains very quickly. My people
got a surprise when I started to eat, because
it was so long since I had experienced the
sensation of being hungry, and my appetite
became more vigorous as the days went by.
I am positive that nothing in the world
can equal Clements Tonic for producing
refreshing and peaceful sleep, as it mado
me sleep shortly after retiring to bed,
dreams never troubling me, and when I
awoke there was no bitter taste to sicken
me, and the aching sensations had l®*t my
head and limbs.*
Reporter. “ Your rheumatic pains—did
Clements Tonic affect them ? ”
Mrs. Lawler : “Affect them ! Why, when
I could take plenty of food without vomit-
ing or retching, or feeling any pains in the
chest or stomach after meals, I was nearly
as well as ever, because by that time the
swellings in my limbs had gone down and
the rheumatic pains were hardly noticeable.
All I wanted was another bottle or two of
Clements Tonic, and you may be assured
that I got them. The end to my sufferings
was assured in the tone and steadiness which
Clements Tonic imparted to my nervous
system, and in due course there was
nothing in my appearance to indicate that
I had gone through the serious illness which
had set at defiance all other medicines
excepting Clements Tonic.”
Reporter: “ may I bring your statements
be r ore the public? ”
Mrs. Lawler: “You are at liberty to
publish them in any way you think best,
as I cannot speak too freely or too gratefully
about Clements Tonic for giving me the
splendid health I now enjoy.”

Languid
Condition
“ Doctor said there was one thing
which might do me good—Scott’s
Emulsion ”
Life has a poor lookout when you are too
languid to do anything—too languid even to
wish to do anything ! Such a condition is
usually a legacy from anaemia, and if not
corrected leads to disaster. Here is the
story of disaster averted :
“ 29, George Row, Dockhead,
“ London, England, June 10th, 1901.
“ Dear Sirs, —Last autumn I suffered very
badly from the effects of anaemia, which was
followed by lung trouble, and I got into a
very weak and languid condition. It was
positively too much trouble for me to do any-
thing, as I could not exert myself in the
least. I had to take to my bed ; yet I did
not make the slightest progress. In October
my doctor said there was one thing which
'
m
MISS N. BOOTH.
(From a photograph.)
might do me good, and that was Scott’s
Emulsion. I acted upon his advice, gave
the preparation a fair trial, and am heartily
glad to be able to say the results have been
most satisfactory, for Scott’s Emulsion was
successful where everything else had failed.
I can truly say that I never felt so well as
I do now, and I cannot help thinking Scott’s
Emulsion has been the means of saving my
life. I consider your preparation a wonder-
fully strengthening and invigorating remedy,
and would certainly advise all suffering from
chest and lung troubles to give it a trial.
“ Yours truly,
“ (Signed) (Miss) N. Booth."
Miss Booth’s story is a story often told by
those who write to us—that a doctor pre-
scribed Scott’s Emulsion. He could, of
course, had he thought wise, have prescribed
plain cod-liver oil, or any of the many sub-
stitutes and imitations of Scott’s Emulsion.
But he did not; he told Miss Booth that
there was one thing which might do her
good—Scott’s Emulsion.
The doctor, like doctors everywhere who
prescribe Scott’s Emulsion, had the best of
reasons for prescribing this remedy, because

“ON OUR SELECTION.”

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Devil-May-Care!
(AN AUSTRALIAN BALLAD.)
Well-known on the border, strong, handsome aiu
straight,
A reckless, wild liver, but true-hearted mate—
What his name was I’m not in position to swear,
But we called him —it suited him—Devil-may-
care !
Drink ! He drank Bullocky Jim out of breath !
lance! He could dance the red stars to their
death !
tide ! There was nothing in hide or in hair
Too rough to be ridden by Devil-may-care 1
Where did he come from ? Well! where are they
bred
With those laughing blue eyes, that proud lift of
the head ?
Oh ! it’s England, and England, and only up
there
Where they breed ’em light-hearted, like Devil-
may-care !
He was fond of the women ! Ay, that you might
think;
Love ’o women goes mostly with dancing and
drink;
He had eyes for the dark ones and lips for the
fair,
And a careless, gay gallant was Devil-may-care !
But there came to our hero, as mostly to those
Who expect to go scatheless, a wound of love’s
woes,
And an armful of roses and ruddy-gold hair
Set the love-stars a-reeling for Devil-may-care !
It was merely in touch with the fitness of things
And the maxim of “ true love” —which somebody
sings
That the gallant went droving all day in the sun,
And the maid was the heiress of Morrida run.
But love laughs at locksmiths, so, somewhere, they
say,
And true love tastes sweeter for bars in the way ;
And bars must be big ones to part a fond pair
When the one is a rebel like Devil-may-care !
So it chanced that on camp where the tired cattle
lay
Aik. .ue drovers kept double night-watch till the
day,
The boss took the road on his ambling bay mare
And a girl in the dim light m6t Devil-may-care.
When he reined the bay mare at the love-chosen
place
It was pure love that shone on his wild, haggard
face,
And the men that had drunk with this reckless
Lothair
Would 'hardly have known him as Devil-may-
care !
So thov met —so they parted—a kiss for a troth !
And the earth seemed a fairy land built for them
both ;
But some late laggard passing caught sight of the
mare,
And —True Heart no longer met Devil-may-care !
And they took her away—for her good—to the
South
With a rebel’s wild kiss on her rosy child-mouth ;
And ah ! for her good were they right to beware
That she sent no word Northward to Devil-may-
care!
And he curses her mern’ry on Merrida Run
Sinco she cared to make light of the love that she
won—
Yet he broods all alone o’er one lock of her hair
Who had wakened a pure love in Devil-may-care !
When the long trip is done, and the cattle safe
down,
Then he drinks —how he drinks ! down in Albury
Town ;
And “Why should I sorrow, it’s only my share
Who believed in a woman ! ” says Devil-may-
care !
WILL H. OGILVIE.
The Girls Who Wait.
By the gullies and creeks that lie lonely,
’Mid depths of the Silent Divide,
Or where tussock and mulga-bush only
Give cheer to the men as they ride;
By the gates of the yards in the city—•
The butts of a merciless Fate-
Pale faces that pray for God’s pity,
They peer up the roads as they wait.
Blue eyes and gold hair of the blonde !
Brown locks of the gleaming brunette !
True hearts that are faithful and fond
Cling close to the'far off loves yet.
To the men who are cutting out cattle,
Or sleeping ’neath storm-tossed belars,
Or racing where gold-stampers rattle,
Or lying stretched dead ’neath the stars.
9 m * *
Oh ! One waits for your coming home,
Another is waiting for me ;
God help them ! Strange tracks must we roam
As we delve from the Sea to the Sea.
So here’s to the lasses that love us,
In spite of our failings, old mate ;
And wherever Misfortune may shove us,
Good luck to the “ women who wait.”
VIC.
L. M. D.
Two or three fragments of a meteoric stone
which fell with a roar lately in broad day and
ploughed up the wilderness near Mt. Browne (far
north-west N.S.W.) have been picked up. The
biggest of the lumps—bluish-green, not unlike
tellurides —has been sent down to Sydney. This
is the nearest approach to a shower that that
district has had for 11 months.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 30, 1902.
The Sydney Gas Monopoly.
The proposal of Law, M.L.A., that the
Sydney gas works should be resumed by the
Government and handed over to Sydney
Municipal Council, was rejected in a thin
Assembly by only two votes—2B against 26.
In a fuller House it might have been carried ;
if the See Government hadn’t thrown its
dead weight on the other side it certainly
would have been carried. The failure of
Law’s motion was a grievous pity —yet not
quite such a pity as it might have been. It
is difficult to see how the one municipality
of Sydney could take over and work a
business which extends over about 39 other
municipalities. A Greater Sydney Council,
with control of the whole gas area, could and
should do it, but a single council would
do the work under great difficulties. Again,
State Socialism is under a cloud just now
by reason of the great Sydney Resumed Area
swindle. When the N.S.W. Government
took over the plague-stricken district it
began to receive the rents, and at the same
moment it became liable to pay interest to
the late owners on the value of their pro-
perty. Instead of paying the interest out of
the rents it used the latter as revenue, and
paid the interest out of loans. That fraud has
added, probably, about £12,000 or £15,000
a year to this country’s interest bill for ever,
and has saddled the Resumed Area with that
additional burden, and it may have thereby
changed a good spec, into a bad one. It
certainly created an impression that neither
the See Government, which was responsible
for the fraud, nor the present Labor party
(or else the Labor party’s present leaders),
that made no serious effort to prevent
it, can be trusted to carry out any
great work of State Socialism on an
honest basis, and it is almost better to let
things wait a while than have a noble prin-
ciple discredited by association with moral
stinks of the Resumed Area character. A
few more such transactions would set back
State Socialism for a generation.
*
Further, The Bulletin would not like to
swear that it isn’t better that a great pro-
perty should be privately owned by Aus-
tralians than nominally owned by the State,
and really owned by a gang of money-lenders
in London. The Resumed Area business
made this change—that (say) £3,000,000 or
£4,000,000 worth of property which used to
be owned principally by Australians is now
owned wholly by English money-lenders,
and the N.S.W. Government is their agent,
bailiff, and rent-collector. The position
wouldn’t matter if there was any reliable
arrangement for paying off the debt and
making the Resumed Area really N.S.W.
State property, but there is none. Nobody
even dreams of there being one. The tran-
saction has added something like £120,000
a year to the tribute N.S.W. has to pay to
England ; it has vastly increased the absen-
tees’ hold upon Australia ; it has made the
Australian less, to the extent of several
millions, the owner of his own country than
he was before. And boosting up the absen-
tee isn’t State Socialism —also, it never
will be.
• * • »
There was no proposal or assurance in the
motion recently rejected by the. N.S.W.
Assembly that the gas business would be
worked on any cleaner a basis. If the
motion had been carried, the usual loan
would doubtless have been floated in
England, and probably, by some juggle
like that practised in the matter
of the Resumed Area, some £lOO,OOO
or £200,000 of the gas revenue would have
been annexed by the Treasury while the
negotiation was pending, and that amount
extra needlessly added to the debt with
which the enterprise was burdened. Then
an enterprise which is now mostly Aus-
tralian would have become really English,
and be really owned by the absentee. The
man with the mortgage is the real owner in
the last resort —and always will be. Then
some £30,000 or £40,000 or £50,000 per
annum would be added to the drain on Aus-
tralia’s resources. It would be a perpetual
drain, for no politician in this country ever
dreams of paying off any part of our foreign
liabilities, and nothing of the kind was sug-
gested in Law’s motion. His proposition
was to apply the profits to reducing the price
of gas—the idea of applying any part of them
to paying off the debt and making the works
really State or municipal property didn’t
get a mention. And it is probably better for
Australia to pay 15 per cent, to our own
people who spend the money in Australia,
than per cent, to the absentee, for the
same reason that the writer finds it cheaper
to pay himself 15 per cent, than to pay any-
body else 3k per cent.
• * * *
The Australian Gas Light Co. is certainly
a monopolist—and a most objectionable one.
But there are English importing firms doing
a large business here, and earning 15 per
cent., and sending every penny of it for dis-
tribution in England, which are far more
objectionable. They don’t own any mon-
opoly, but the man who makes 15 per cent,
out of us with a monopoly is no more costly
than the one who makes 15 per cent, without
one. And if the 15 per cent, monopoly
employs solely Australian labor, and spends
its profits in Australia, it isn’t one-quarter
so disastrous as the concern which pays wages
chiefly to outside labor, and spends its 15
per cent, in another country. The worst
thing that ever struck Australia is the
foreign-owned monopoly, of which the new
Adelaide tramways may be quoted as a
specially hideous specimen. The next worst
is the great mass of foreign-owned enter-
prises of all sorts —the foreign-owned banks,
fire insurance offices, mines, importing and
trading concerns, pastoral companies, land
companies (the Australian Agricultural Co.
may be quoted as an extra-hideous specimen),
the absentee property-holders, shareholders,
mortgagees, &c. The foreign manufacturers
who supply us with goods that we should
and could manufacture for ourselves rank
along with these. The absentee person who
owns a local industry is bad, but at least he
pays wages to Australians; when the
owner, foreman, workmen, profits and wages
are all absentees, the case is 20 times
worse. The foreign bondholder who has a
£200,000,000 mortgage over our public
works, our country, our lands, our future, our
souls, our Australia in general, is extra objec-
tionable. And the purely local monopolist
and local Fat Man, who at least are Austra-
lian and spend their wealth in Australia, are
objectionable—but by comparison with the
other lot they are almost respectable. The
Bulletin is proud (within limitations) of the
Australian Gas Light Co.—the bloated,
nefarious, extortionate, arrogant, and dis-
gusting Gas Co.—inasmuch as its existence
is a proof that Australia has something of its
own, and makes something for itself, and that
the mortgage of Cohen, of London, isn’t
quite universal. The Gas Co. is at least ten
times more respectable than Henniker
Heaton, spending his profits in England,
and not even seriously pressed by the Labor
party for an absentee tax.
* * * *
The United States is often quoted as pre-
eminently the land of millionaires. So it is
—but after the millionaire has had his
stupendous steal, there is more left for the
poor man than there is in Australia. The
millionaire in Uncle Sam’s country spends at
least a great part of his ill-gotten income in
Uncle Sam’s territory. Australia’s million-
aires are more rapacious but less visible.
We send £8,000,000 a year to the great
aggregate Cohen, who holds our national
securities in England, and even if he isn’t
one Cohen, but a great, joint, multitudinous
Cohen, the result is the same. We send
about another £8,000,000 to miscellaneous
creditors in England. The United States,
which has practically no foreign debt, doesn’t
pay tribute to all its millionaires put to-
gether on the scale of £4 per inhabitant per
annum and the money its millionaires
absorb isn’t wholly lost to the country, as in
our case. It is a bad thing to have a bloated
and arrogant monopoly such as the Australian
Gas Light Co. camped like a nightmare on
our chest, but to buy it up with English
loan money and exchange it for more absentee
Cohen would be worse. This country can’t
breathe for the amount of absentee Cohen
that is on its chest already. When is the
N.S.W. Labor party going to announce that
the price of its support—the only price at
which it will do business—is a very heavy
absentee-tax ? That would be making war
on a monopolist who is worth fighting.
Retrenchment in N.S.W.
Treasurer Waddell reports that this year’s
revenue in New South Wales is not likely to be
equal to that of last year, and that trouble is
ahead. Therefore the usual easy devices in the
way of sweating the public service are likely to be
adopted. Stopping the public servant’s increments
for a few years longer (they have already been
stopped, in many cases, for quite a number of
years) is the easiest thing yet invented in the way
of retrenchment. In Britain there is a corres-
ponding device, though one of rather a different
character; the simple, handy, mechanical way
over there is to put a penny on the income-tax.
What might happen to a Treasurer who proposed
that sort of thing here is too horrible to be thought
of. The N.S.W. revenue this year is almost
certain to be less than that collected during
the year just closed, for various reasons. A
number of revenue duties have been struck off
the tariff. The Protective duties that are left
in the tariff are hardly likely to yield so
much as they did last year, seeing that local in-
dustries are beginning, in some degree, to take
the place of imports. It is not desirable, either,
that they should yield so much, for the money to
pay for last year’s lavish imports, and for the
duties on them, was only obtained by foreign bor-
rowing on an incredible scale, and the people who
should have been employed here in making the
goods that N.S.W. imported were being sup-
ported by a huge and disastrous system of relief
works paid for by loans. Moreover, the wicked
and disreputable swindle that was done last year
by annexing the rents of the resumed area of
Sydney as revenue, while paying the correspond-
ing interest liabilities out of loans, can’t be
done over again on anything like the old
scale; it is practically an exhausted swindle.
The amount at the disposal of the See Govern-
ment last year was nearly £400,000 more than any
N.S.W. Government ever received before, and in
addition about £1,000,000 of expenditure, which
previous N.S.W. Governments had to meet, was
defrayed by the Federal authority, which left the
See Ministry with about £1,400,000 more to spend
than any of its predecessors.
m m * *
Under these circumstances it might have been
supposed that the See Government would be able
to pay off a million or so of the old deficits, and
reduce the interest bill, and make some prepara-
tion, in that one year of exceptional fatness, for
the more ordinary year that must follow.
Curiously enough it did nothing of the kind.
It got through all the extra revenue, and that
without paying any more than the usual amount
of the public works expenditure out of revenue.
It even had a new deficit, and it was
reduced to doing a most disreputable public
fraud with the resumed area rents to disguise that
deficit. It borrowed more than N.S.W. ever bor-
rowed in one year before. Even if the money
raised for the repurchase of the plague-stricken
quarter of Sydney is not included, it borrowed
more than was ever borrowed before except in one
awful year when Parkes and M‘Millan were
running amuck. It added £150,000 or more to
the annual interest bill in one large, vigorous act,
and it seems as if it may add another £120,000 or
£150,000 this year. And now that there is a
prospect of a return to something like normal con-
ditions—that is to say, to the old state of things
under which N.S.W. had, in proportion to popu-
lation, very nearly the largest revenue in the
world—there is trouble. Also, there is talk of
cutting down the pay, or at least stopping the
increments, of almost the only class of public
servants whose wages weren’t raised in the
Great Drunk Year just ended. And the
Treasurer is represented as saying that the See
Government is resolved that N.S.W. “shall live
within its means” —which reads like the most
impudent or the most profoundly silly joke of the
generation. The idea of a Government which has
just put on the N.S.W. record the 15th deficit
which the State has had in 23 years, which did the
resumed area swindle, and which uses over
£1,000,000 a year of loan money for works that are
said to be permanent and reproductive but which
it knows to be neither —the idea of such a Govern-
ment professing to care a straw whether N.S.W.
lives within its means or not is a humorous epi-
sode. It is a good many years since N.S.W. had
a Government which cared for anything except
making the accounts balance on paper by any
shabby fraud or trick that came to hand.
* * * •
The financial situation of N.S.W. may be
roughly summed up thus :
It uses up about £1,400,000 worth of its public lands
every year for revenue purposes—that is the amount it
receives from the absolute sale of lands ; and it can’t keep
square.
It uses about £1,000,000 a year of loan money as revenue
—that is to say, it uses that amount for works, patches
and repairs which are not in any sense productive of
income ; and it can’t make its accounts look solvent even
after that.
It has an income-tax (first collected in 1896) which,
though far below the British figure, is still a reasonably
large source of income, and it seems more bankrupt than
ever.
It has a reasonably heavy land-tax (first collected in
1897;, and the deficit arrives just the same.
It has a respectable, though not exorbitant, scale of
death-duties, which were greatly increased in 1899, and
it hasn’t made ends meet any better since the increase
than before. .
It has a very large customs revenue (greatly increased
in 1901), and the shortage comes just as when it had a
small customs revenue.
N.S.W. only makes ends meet even on paper in
one year out of three, and the question naturally
arises : Under what possible set of circumstanoes
does it hope or expect to make ends meet ? The
State is still only lightly taxed ; but taxation is
steadily becoming heavier, and each increase
seems to leave the finances a little worse than
they were before. There is no satisfaction in
saying that the public lands are a great asset and
good security for the public creditor; it ap-
parently requires every penny that N.S.W. can
scrape out of its public lands to pay its ordinary
daily expenses, and there is no margin left as
security for the public creditor. The railway
revenue doesn’t cover much more than half the
interest bill. Every few years a little extra
taxation may be put on, or a little retrenchment
attempted, but it is always done too late. The
idea is never to raise or save £1,000,000 a year
now, voluntarily, for public works, but to pay
everything out of loans, and later on to raise or
save the same £1,000,000 a year under com-
pulsion for ever to pay interest on the
money borrowed for these works, which might
as well have been constructed out of revenue in
the first place. It is never a case of putting money
in the bank, but always of scraping up enough to
get the coat out of the pawnshop. It is taking
time by the heels instead of the forelock. The
average Australian Treasurer, in his economical
mood —the mood that Treasurer Waddell says
he is going to get into—is, in his political deal-
ings, a swindler, a dodger, a persistent deceiver,
a person who gets money by false pretences, and
a faker of the public accounts ; in his extravagant
moments, he is worse than that. Treasurer
Waddell, by way of a great reform and a
general straightening up of things, proposes to
use, as usual, about £1,400,000 worth of the coun-
try’s land assets and about £1,000,000 of loans as
revenue, and if he makes the ledger balance after
that, his pride and inflation will probably en-
danger the front buttons of his waistcoat.
■ft • * *
A question will be raised some day whether the
Australian people have proved themselves fit for
the exercise of self-governing powers, and unless
there is a great reform in this country’s financial
methods the reply will probably be in the nega-
tive. It is one of the boasts of the British people
that they are, at present, the only really success-
ful colonising race on earth, but between the
French or German colony that lives on subsidies
from the mother country, and the British one
which lives on loans from the mother country, the
difference isn’t so very great after all. About the
worst shock Britain has had since Washington’s
time will be the great Australian bankruptcy that
must needs come some day, supposing the present
methods last. If Treasurer Waddell finds him-
self personally a great deal deeper in debt every
month than he was the month before he will go
bankrupt someday, and Waddell’s country will do
the same. The process always ends that way. And
then Australia will be a good country for any
man to be out it and to have his money out of
—a fact of which the A.M.P. Society, which is
seeking authority to invest its funds in Europe
and elsewhere, appears to have a glimmering.
Meanwhile there is a universal explanation to the
effect that the drought is responsible, which might
be a very good explanation if it were not that
Australian finance is just in as bad a muddle in
wet years as in dry.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Wobbling 1 Philpocracy.
The Philp Government is, apparently, ill at ease.
Already this session four virtual motions of want
of confidence have been submitted (two from
Govt, supporters), and the Continuous Govt, is
commencing to look very rocky on its pedestal.
The Income-Tax Bill has driven a wedge into the
hitherto solid Govt, phalanx—and it is only the
intense dislike of the rebellious Philpites to an alli-
ance with the Labor party that saves the present
Cabinet. And it needs some salvation. Two
past deficits of £528,000 and £431,000 (pro-
portionately to population, the highest in
Australia), and a coming one of possibly
£200,000, ought to be sufficient to damn any
Government, and an early damnation would be
the inevitable fate of any such Government in any
part of Australasia save Queensland. The Q.
Democrats have at least one satisfaction. They
see the Philpites driven, dead against their will,
to adopt the hated Labor programme—lncome-tax
now, Land-tax shortly, Shops and Factories Act
already, Arbitration and Conciliation in another
session or two —and they have the satisfaction of
knowing that if they don’t ride the old Govern-
ment horse themselves, they are at least a spur to
prod it into progress.
The Tatters of the Golden Fleece.
“F.M.” writes on the grimmest joke in Australia:
The great “ natural ” industry of Australia culminated
so far as N.S.VV. is concerned in 1891. Nobody believed
it at the time, no publicist save Coghlan has noticed it
since. Projects and comments have gone on as if the
bulk of the land was yet virgin and the supreme natural
industry only on the fringe of development. But ten
years of varying seasons have made it plain enough that
the 61,000,000 sheep of that year will never again be seen
within the borders of N.S.W. They were crammed on
the food-reserves which Nature had prepared from the
beginning of things, and, when these reserves were con-
sumed, normal years began to make facts plain, and bad
years to burn them in. If 50,000,000 were put on the
country again it would be in the certain knowledge
that 10,000,000 of them with their progeny must perish
miserably in the recurring cycles of drought. They
would die out faster than they have done in the seven
years just past, for the reason that natural reserves are
all gone ; trees lopped, scrub gnawed out, earth made a
desert over thousands of square miles ; and, there are the
rabbits, as difficult to reckon with, or against, as the
locusts. Apart from them, it is safe to assert that the
carrying capacity of the State is less by 25 per cent, than
in 1891, while its holding power against a drought is
practically destroyed.
That brings us to an estimate of 45,000,000 as the full
sheep stock of the State. With careful management and
a'considerable annual expenditure in growing and storing
fodder an average of 45,000,000 might be maintained;
beyond that number no reasonable forecast can go.
-How far will the return from 45,000,000 sheep go
towards making a nation and supporting a people ?
The “natural” industry prophets, whose Australian
ideas are on the grand scale, ought to face such questions
as those, but they do not. Their calculations are made,
and their forecasts based, on the supposition that we
have only to keep on squatting to keep on prospering,
multiplying, increasing all round. The plain and appall-
ing truth is that we can’t go on another inch. The limit
was reached and overshot in 1891. VVe can only hope to
get back as near to it as is Sife, and to keep thereby
such effort and expenditure as is hardly yet contem-
plated. What shall we find in that position in the way of
annual returns?
The terrible overstocking of 1891 produced £11,000,000
worth of wool. We might expect £10,000,000 worth
annually from 45,000,000 well-managed sheep ; for wool
was low in 1891, and statistics, which, however, need a
bit of explanation, show that the fleeces have mar-
vellously increased in weight during the past ten years.
But £10,000,000 a year would be a liberal estimate,
an amount not likely to be exceeded in any series of years
through half a century. It represents fairly the value of
the Golden Fleece of N.S.W., grown, shorn, packed and
delivered. It is not much to keep a people ; 100,000 at
£lOO a year eat it up. But N.S.W. has not, and cannot
have more than a fraction of it. Mortgagees and absen-
tees claim the bulk. A million a year, perhaps, out of
the land which grows it, and how much in wages ? Some


figures recently quoted by squatters and shearers in con-
ference enable'us to answer that question as regards the
main items of outlay. It was stated that through the
year one man to 8,000 sheep would be a fair estimate.
We will allow one to 5,000. It was also stated that 4,000
shearers would manage 30,000,000 sheep. They would need
to, if useful cheques were to be taken home. Thus we
arrive at 9,000 hands regularly employed to work the sheep
stock of the State and 6000 for the shearing. These will
be the bulk contributions of the Golden Fleece to the
“ nation of N.S.W.” —the employment of 9000 men as a
regular thing and the casual employment of 6000 more.
We don’t need to go out amongst all peoples and tongues
to make up those. There are three times the number
required now ready for the work within the State. Allow
6000 rouseabouts for the shearing season, and then not
more than half the available supply would be used up—
and it has not been used up for years past. Under a proper
system of organisation half the shearers employed could
have done the work very well; while there have always
been two rouseabout3 for one wanted, and from 10,000 to
20,000 of them living dingoes’ lives nine months of the
year.
Nor do we find many purple patches about the cul-
mination which has been reached and passed when we
look at its higher phases. How many of the “lordly
squatter ” tribe inhabit Toorak and Potts and Darling
Points now ? Are not many of the mansions turned into
boarding-houses, and most of those privately held, in the
occupation of softgoods men, mining speculators, and
usurers ?
The horrors resultant from the culmination reached
and passed have, perhaps, received sufficient publicity.
We will hope, for the credit of our common humanity,
they may never be repeated ; but the plain facts of the
strictly limited prospect do merit, and indeed demand,
more serious attention than they have yet received.
Now, if we take the fleece from the shears to the loom,
does the dream of our modern Jasons seem likely to be
realised ? The United Kingdom spins 510,000,0001b5. of
wool to turn out £61,000,000 worth of woollens. The
N.S.W. annual clip could not be estimated at more
than half that quantity. To produce the finished
voods the old country employs 302,000 hands.
There we have the limits of possibility set of the
mother State of Australia in the full development of
her “greatest natural industry,” the employment of—
say 200,000 hands —and the employment of 180,000 of
these depends on N.S.W. establishing what the Free-
traders call “ artificial ” manufacturing industries. The
Carnegie Steel Trust goes right over it, both in value of
output and hands employed. The realisation of that one
concern goes beyond ail the possibilities of the Golden
Fleece of N.S.W. Even the divine far-off event of Aus-
tralia, weaving as well as growing the Golden Fleece of
the world, does not flatter big hopes of nationhood. And
it is part of our madness to imagine that Australia has
any monopoly in wool, or the United Kingdom in
woollens. In 1897 the United States used 601 million lbs.
of wool against the United Kingdom’s 510 millions, and
grew the bulk of it herself.
Another N.S.W. culmination may possibly be noted in
wheat, for it is by nb means certain that the drought is
solely responsible for the curtailment of acreage. But
even in good years the Australian wheat crop is miserably
poor compared with that of most other countries in the
same line of business. That outlook is also rather ghastly.
And what remains in a “ natural ” way that is promising?
Butter and fru’t —yes. But only when we approach
them by “ unnatural ” means : by Protection and bonus
and effective State interference in the way of instruction
and control. There is abundant natural opportunity, but
our “ natural” philosophy people regard it all as one of
their ancestors might have regarded the natural oppor-
tunities of the sea, saying, “ Since iron will not swim it
is clearly absurd to talk about building iron ships.”
The present condition of the mother State of Aus-
tralia, too, shows what may be expected if the policy of
Freetrade and non-interference is worked out free of any
restriction or impediment. It is a terrible outlook. The
old State sickens for a storm of fiscal revolution. Its scari-
fied, denuded, hunger-stricken body is more like leprous
senility than budding adolescence. Under the “ natural ”
system we cannot in any conceivable future imagine it
rising to the level of Illinois or Pennsylvania. Our Golden
Fleece culmination is a fiasco. There are even in the one
old State inexhaustible and almost infinitely varied re-
sources which, in the essentials of wealth and prosperity,
do not compare meanly with those of the Mississippi
Valley. But mankind knows what policy has made the
great valley and its adjacent States the world’s wonder
which now appears, and Cobdenism, in face of it all, is
like tin-pot Atheism looking up at the stars and babbling
about Oome-by-Chance.

Reform a la Cowley.
Ex-Speaker Cowley (Q. Assembly) is about the
most stubborn reactionary in Q. Parliament. The
other night he moved an amendment in favor of
the withdrawal of the income-tax proposals and a
substitution therefore of a scheme for selling pub-
lic lands wholesale. In the course of a remark-
ably frank speech (for Cowley always lias the
courage of his opinions) the ex-Speaker gave as
his remedies for Q. depression and deficit—the sell-
ing of State lands, the selling of State railways to
anyone who would buy, and the charging of fees to
schoolchildren. With a little provocation, Cowley
would probably revert to the old methods of forced
loans and benevolences—in fact, there is no telling
where he would halt in his retrogressive career.
The Opposition showed its appreciation of
Cowley’s principles and amendments by voting
en masse with the Govt. —and the Govt, didn’t
seem any too flattered thereby. The Philp Govt,
is bad enough, but the Cowley regime would evi-
dently be ten times worse, and would be adapted
only for a country of sugar-kings, monopolists,
mean whites, and meaner Kanakas.

The Imported Fodder Farce.
An exceptionally bitter yell of rage on the subject
of the fodder duties was blown off last week by
Premiers See and Philp— especially Philp. It
began with this telegram from See to the adjacent
Premiers:
I would strongly urge that you invite your Parliament
to pass a resolution, to be presented to the Acting-Prime
Minister of the Commonwealth, asking the Federal Par-
liament to remit the duties on fodder until the break-up
of the drought. The loss of stock in N.S. Wales and
Queensland has been enormous. Every day adds a con-
siderably increasing number to the deaths, and the con-
dition of many parts of these two States is deplorable.
This Government is already carrying fodder for starving
stock over its railways at a merely nominal rate. I am
also communicating with the Premiers of the other
States, and should be glad of an early reply.
Irvine, of Victoria, replied, declining to move in
the matter. So did Jenkins and Lewis, the
latter pointing out that N.S.W. ought simply to
refund the duties itself on its own imported pro-
duce. Philp, of Queensland, replied with a yell
of indignation, cut after this pattern :
Re fodder duties, I think it quite useless to ask Federal
Government for any concession. They have already
refused, and seem quite callous about what happens to
any of the States.
It seems to be really time these two very inferior
mummers, _ Philp and See, dropped this silly
farce, which they have enacted so long.
Everybody who knows how the financial
arrangements of the Commonwealth are
worked is quite aware that Philp and See
could allow their people to import all the fodder
they want duty free, if it was really fodder that
Philp and See wanted, and not rather a chance
to snarl at the Federal Government for its anti-
Kanaka legislation and other progressive measui-es.
Philp can tell his allegedly distracted pastoralists
to import 50,000 tons of fodder any day, and,
when it comes to hand, instead of the distracted
pastoralists paying the duty, the Philp Govern-
ment can pay it by giving its own State cheque to
the Federal Treasurer on the 31st of the month.
And on the Ist or 2nd of next month it will get
back its share of the surplus, which will be larger
by just the amount of the cheque than it other-
wise would be, and Queensland won’t have paid
any duty after all. It will have got its fodder in
free, which, Philp says, is the thing it is
craving for. Also, See, if he really wants
fodder, can do the same. Of course such a
scheme wouldn’t work if they wanted to send
that imported fodder into some adjacent State,
for then the adjacent State would demand its
share of the duty ; but either See or Philp can
cover his oivn State with imported fodder 6ft.
deep and not pay a copper of duty—he has merely to
hand over his State Government’s cheque for the
customs tax, and get it back again. If the distress
in their respective States can be alleviated by
imports of fodder—if the pastoralists want im-
ported fodder, and have money to pay for it —
then Philp is about the most callous politician
Australia ever possessed, and is deliberately
ruining his own State for no visible reason unless
to keep up a cry against the Federal Government
—the Government which is taking away his
beloved Kanaka. If he adopted the simple pro-
cess of importing fodder duty-free, as he can do
any day, then, of course, his yarn that a brutal
Federal Government prevented him doing it would
fall through, and apparently it is better to let
Queensland be ruined than have that lie fall
through. Premier See, apparently, doesn’t want
dutyfree imported fodder in N.S.W. (which he
can get any day) nearly so much as an all-round
suspension of the duties so as to deprive the hated
Victorian of his share of Protection. And Irvine,
of Victoria, as his State doesn’t want imported
Argentine green stuff, naturally refuses to ask the
Federal Government to suspend the duties merely
to gratify anybody’s parochial animosity. As See
and Philp can work their own suspension of duty
any moment they like, Irvine very naturally
leaves them to help themselves. There is no cause
why he should sacrifice what he regards as the
interests of his own State to oblige two men who
don’t in the least degree require the sacrifice, and
who would gain nothing by it, except possibly the
gratification of a mean, sordid animosity. There
is no reason at all why the Federal Gov-
ernment, either, should suspend the fodder
duties in Victoria, Tasmania, and S.A. at the
demand of any ill-conditioned person who is
playing to the gallery. If Philp and See are so
callous to the alleged needs of their own peoples
that they won’t help them when they can do it
without a penny of expense, they can’t expect
sympathy. Supposing any association of dis-
tressed pastoralists imported 1000, or 50,000, tons
of Argentine or any other hay to Brisbane or
Sydney, they would certainly be met with a
demand for the duty. The money thus paid would
go to the Federal Treasurer, and from him back
within a month to Philp and See. And if they
chose to refund it, then all would be well, accord-
ing to their own showing. As a matter of fact,
they callously decline to refund a copper, and
their attitude apparently is that every animal in
their two States may die before they will refund a
copper.

Kingston’s Gliost.
James Hingston being dead and unable to pre-
vent the waste of coin, it is hardly surprising that
his bequest to Melbourne Benevolent Asylum is
now “ before the courts.” Any man who aspires
to be a public benefactor after death can safely
reckon on providing sport for the lawyers.
It is always happening when the executors
of deceased are not personally interested
in keeping the last will and testament out
of the courts. James Hingston thought he
had provided against the probability of the
residue being nibbled by sharks when he said:
“As to the rest and residue of ray real and
personal estate, I give and devise the same to the
treasurer for the time being of Melbourne
Benevolent Asylum to be applied towards the
purpose of re-building that institution on a ground
floor plan.” The lay mind sees James Hingston’s
intention shining out of that sentence. He
bequeathed £25,000 towards rebuilding the
“ Benevolent ” in such a way that its aged inmates
would not need to climb upstairs to bed. If the
amount of his bequest is insufficient to pay for an
entire rebuilding the ghost of Hingston wishes it
to be safely invested until capital and interest will
suffice to accomplish the reformation. The lay
mind sees this. But the legal mind sees £25,000
of a dead man’s money available for a law-suit,
consequently the Benevolent Asylum has been
advised to bring a friendly suit (how much of dead
men’s gold has gone to pay the costs of friendly
suits?) against the Equity Trustees, Execs, and
Agency Co. A “summons for directions as to
place and mode of trial” happened last week,
when “ his Honor directed the parties to set out
the facts by way of a special case for consideration
of the court.” Perhaps a few interpleaders and
things will pave the way to the trial that looms in
the distance. Only two legal firms and a pair of
barristers (plus the representatives of the Crown,
which chips into such eases on general principles),
—only six makers-of-costs are on the job at
present, but there may be more to follow. Mean-
while, the ghost of Hingston shudders in dismay.
The sharks have got their teeth into his intentions.

Cohen and his Rand Slave System.
Those Australian “democratic” members and
“friends of the working-man” who profess
now to be sorrily surprised that a conse-
quence of the destruction of the Boer
Republics is the boycotting of white labor
in favor of Chinese and enslaved Kaffirs on
the Rand, have more cuteness than candour. It’s
all very well for them to say, or to hint, that “if
they had but known,” their war-attitude would
have been different. They did know, must have
known, the main object of Cohen in waging war
upon the Boers with the British army ; but were
weak and cowardly enough to drift with the Jingo
movement and assist with Australian men the
biggest movement to boycott white labor in the
world’s history. Cohen showed his hand long
before the war was waged. If Kruger had been
amenable to the suggestions of Cecil Rhodes,
and the German Cohens and the French and
American Cohens, he might have had their help
to paint all South Africa'yellow, but he preferred
a pastoral Republic to a plutocratic bureaucracy,
and so went down before the cohorts of Cohen.
As recently as 1897 a Commission was appointed
by the Transvaal Govt, to inquire into the ques-
tion of labor at the Rand mines, and before the
Commission Cohen formulated these demands:
(1) The reduction of the wages paid to Kaffirs
working in the mines from £3O to £l5 a year-; (2)
the imposition of a head-tax (one witness suggested
one of £24 a year !) on all Kaffirs not working in the
mines. The two suggestions involved the absolute
enslavement of the Kaffir population for the bene-
fit of the mine-owners. The Boer Govt, refused
to agree to the proposals, though a modification
of them was recommended by the Commission (in-
fluenced by the unanimous testimony of 14 mine
managers that some approach to Kaffir slavery
was necessary). The evidence of that Commission,
and the reports of various other movements on the
part of Cohen to work the Rand mines by slave
labor, have been available in Australia for the past
five years to anyone who cared to make inquiries ;
they were repeatedly commented on in the anti-
Jingo papers; but most of the “democratic”
members were too anxious to curry favor with the
respectable merchants who had butter and meat
to sell at the War Office, to take any independent
stand then. Now they wish to say “if only they
had known ! ” Sincerity is indeed rare in politics.
