Dairymen and Factory Legislation.
From shire councils throughout the length and
breadth of Victoria there is proceeding, at present,
one broad stream of motions, beseeching the
Government, in agonised terms, to refrain from
extending the operations of the Factories Act to
country districts. At present the Act is confined
to the larger towns, but it can be applied to any
shire by the simple method of proclamation. The
measure expires shortly, but the Irvine Govt,
has promised to re-enact it until it can be recon-
sidered, as a whole, in the light of the nearly-
ready report of the Factories Act Special Com-
mission. As the House of 48 Rich Landlords is
doing its best to block the re-enacting Bill, and
so allow the Act to lapse altogether, the munici-
pal councils deem it a favorable time for putting
in their plea; and, until the composi-
tion of these councils is considered, their
unanimity on the matter is truly surprising.
Most of the councils adopted the resolution
unanimously ; not two per cent, threw it out; and
even local bodies which have heretofore stood
wisely by their rule of not meddling in politics
have, for the first time, gone on a political burst
and cursed the Act vigorously. The composition
of the councils, however, makes their denuncia-
tion almost valueless. The great bulk of their
members are either graziers or dairymen, and the
majority of the ratepayers who elect them are also
either landowners or dairymen. The employees
in the butter factories and dairies are either
without a vote at all, or are in an insignificant
minority. The wail of the shire councils is that
the application of the Factories Act to the dairy-
ing industry “is impracticable,” that “it will
cripple one of our great industries,” and that it will
absolutely ruin the poor, unfortunate dairyman.
At the present time there are four classes of the
country community highly interested in the dairy-
ing industry—the landowners, the tenants, the
factory and dairy employees and the business
people. The only class that will be affected by
the Act is the first —the landowners, who are orga-
nising the present httack on the measure. Even
amongst this class the operation of the Act will be
but partial. The small landowners who do their
own work, or only employ one hand, have nothing
to fear from the Act. Put in a nutshell, its effect
will be to reduce the tremendous rents which the big
landowners at present draw from dairying tenants.
A few years ago the best grazing land in the western
district of Victoria was only worth 10s. per acre per
annum. To-day, however, the same land is being
eagerly competed for at up to £2 per acre, accord-
ing to its proximity to a creamery. The success of
the butter industry is the cause of the immense
jump in land values, which jump has, of course,
also extended to capital values.' Men who gave
£lO and £l5 per acre for their farms 12 or 15 years
ago find that they can sell them to-day at £3O or
£4O. The result of the new industry has been to
vastly increase the wealth of the farmer, whether
he be landowner or tenant; the storekeeper and
other business men have shared the prosperity;
but the employee has been left out in the cold
completely. To pay the high rent which com-
petition has caused, and to enable the dairyman
to live well, the wages of the man who does the
hard work have been ruthlessly cut down. It is
only in the rarest circumstances that £1 a week
and board is given ; 17s.‘ 6d. is reckoned a good
wage; and the majority of the hands have to be
content with 12s. fid. or 15s. In numbers of cases
as low as ss. and 7s. 6d. is paid. To earn this dole
the men have to work about 14 hours per day, and
put up with inferior accommodation and, in too
many instances, with poor food also ! It is the
exception for a man to be employed all the year
round. The same paper that constantly wails that
the dairying industry cannot pay Factories Act
wages states that the monthly payments from the
Colac factory include cheques to individual far-
mers for £lB3, £162, and so on. The industry can
pay wages, and something over, if the rents are
reduced and dairymen are content with a fair
thing. There is a standard gibe at “ the proposal
to subject cows to the eight hours and holiday
system.” No one proposes to join trades-
men’s conditions on to dairying necessities. What
the employees want is good wages, good food,
and good sleeping accommodation. If these were
granted there would be nothing heard of the
difficulty of securing labor in the busy season.
Under present conditions no dairy employee can
dream of marrying and becoming a responsible
citizen. Even the factory employees, whose
condition—though not satisfactory—is several
stages above that of dairy hands, find marrying a
precarious venture. “ 1 had once,” writes a
Bulletin correspondent, “a little sympathy
for the dairyman who rented his land ; but I find
he is as little deserving of it as his landlord. In
many cases his first proceeding is to employ a
‘family,’ consisting of father, mother and
children, who are given a third or a fourth of the
gross returns, or else a lump sum per annum.
This lump sum is the most outrageous sweating.
It ranges from £5O a year, with only a house
found, for a married couple, up to £l5O for (say)
husband, wife, four sons and two daughters, who
all milk. The herd milked by the latter will
range from 100 to 150 cows. The tenant has the
rosiest of times when he has ‘ a good family ’
doing his actual work ; and cases are even known
in which the tenant takes a house in a neighbor-
ing town, where he lives in comfort with Ins own
family, on the difference between his receipts
and what he spends on rent and ‘ family.’ ” The
extension of the Minimum Wage Law to the
dairying industry would secure better conditions
for factory and dairy employees, and attract a
better class of employees than the hulks and semi-
larrikins who at present do the work. And the
extra cost would bring down rent values by, say,
2s. 6d. or ss. per acre per annum. Even then the
landlord would get times what he received a
very few years ago.
Our Mother-in-law’s Navy.
There is much significance in the steady
hostility which England displays to any proposal
that Australia should build and man war-ships of
her own; _ Such action by Australia would
mean a reinforcement of the Imperial naval
power in any time of foreign trouble. Whilst
England preserves her attitude of benevo-
lent non-interference in Australian affairs, each
Australian warship would be practically an
addition to the English navy, with the prospec-
tive additional value that it would possibly, in
time, evolve some differences in training and
equipment which (as in the case of the army)
would be serviceable to British naval commanders.
But England is resolute that we shall not build
ships, but shall confine our naval enterprise to
providing old-age pensions for an out-of-date
squadron of her navy. Further, and more signifi-
cant still, England is equally resolute that we
shall not even train a modern naval force by
giving them a period of active service on one of
the cruisers in Australian waters. It is a remark-
able attitude for a country to take up which is so
sure of the “union of hearts and hands,” and
which confesses to so deep an obligation to Aus-
tralian soldiers in the last war. Fortunately, it
is an attitude which may be completely ignored
as soon as the Australian Parliament _ cares
to take the responsibility of building or buying its
first battleship. And that should be soon. The
present “ Australian Squadron ” is admittedly
obsolete ; it is admitted that it can, and possibly
would, be withdrawn from the Australian coast
when war was raging. For the protection of our
coast shipping a few, very few, modern cruisers
and coast-defence ships are required. Over-sea
shipping (after we’ve had a proper Protectionist
system for a few years, and make our own iron and
steel, and weave our own cloth) might be inter-
rupted for a few months without actual disaster.
Australia therefore only wants a small, up-to-date
squadron to police her own waters, and to pay for
it would not involve very much greater expense
than the present subsidy to the obsolete Auxiliary
Squadron. The issue is one of paying 30s. for
what is useful rather than 20s. for what is useless.
Bird’s Dream.
Treasurer Bird, of Tasmania, has ideas about
the advisability of establishing a regular sinking
fund for the repayment of the island’s debt, now
very large and very burdensome. To this end he
suggests that portion of the money received from
land-sales, and possibly something from general
revenue, should be set apart and accumulated.
At a rather late hour, Bird admits sorrowfully
that many of the “ permanent and reproductive ”
works on which loans have been spent answered
to neither adjective. As to the reproductiveness,
published official statistics show that out of
£8,500,000 which Tasmania owes, about £4,500,000
has gone for unproductive jobs, which it was
a gross and disreputable swindle to charge to pos-
terity. As regards the alleged permanence, Bird
admits lugubriously in his old age that in many
cases the tracks are overgrown, the bridges and
wharves are worn out, the rolling-stock has gone
to the scrap-heap, and other things are dead, lost,
decayed, damned, perished, or obsolete. All
these things Bird has discovered in his declining
years—and he proposes that Tasmania shall go on
borrowing, the new debt being a dead certainty,
and that, as a set-off, Tasmania shall either pro-
mise to put away a certain sum every year, or
actually put it away, with the great probability
that some hard-up Treasurer will seize it later on
to make good his shortage. The simple fact that
can’t and won’t soak into Bird’s intelligence is
that the simplest, cheapest, and surest sinking-
fund is just to leave off borrowing and pay for all
new works out of revenue. The orthodox
sinking-fund policy consists in borrowing (say) <£l
in London at 3 per cent., receiving only 18s. 6d.
for the money, and paying 4d. out of that for
expenses. Then the interest is remitted to
London every year for 47 years, by which time
the country has paid another 3d. for exchange.
And, meanwhile, if the sinking fund is a real
success, £1 is laboriously put away here to re-
pay that 18s. 6d. (which professes to be £1) at
maturity, and while the £1 that is borrowed there
costs about 3h per cent, interest, the £1 that is
accumulated here probably realises about 3J per
cent. And after the £1 here (which is the sinking
fund) has been gloated over for 28J years it goes
amissing, and there isn’t any sinking fund after
all. The country that doesn’t borrow needs no
sinking fund, just as the person who isn’t diseased
requires no bottle of medicine. The promise to
establish one is only the opium pill that
this country uses to deaden its conscience
with; Australia is full of financiers who are
borrowing now and are going to establish a sink-
ing fund year after next. If these financiers will
leave off loans and use the money that is going to
be put into that fund for their public works, it
will save much worry, discount, exchange, and un-
reality. It will be all the sinking fund that Aus-
tralia needs for the present anyhow. Later on,
when the habit of not incurring new debts has
become fixed, the repayment of old ones may be
considered, but the habit of not incurring new
debts is required first. N.S.W. is still trying to
pay off its liabilities with the sinking fund that
jDibbs was going to start in 1892, and it knows
the elusive character of sinking funds. And every
other State has at some time or other been going
to do the same thing that Dibbs was going to do.
N.S.W. Public Service Board.
“Canonicus” (from an inside knowledge) writes :
“ As the career of an officer in the Public Service will
depend entirely upon his personal conduct and the
manner in which his duties are performed, he is pro-
hibited from seeking the interest of influential persons in
order to obtain promotion, removal, or any other
advantage.” Thus regulation 20 made by N.S.W. Public
Service Board—admirable in tone, and perfect in theory,
but, unfortunately, in practice a dead letter. These are
the facts :
The State of N.S.W. contains a large area. A certain
portion, east of the coastal range, cool, green, healthy,
and pleasant to live in ; west of the Blue Mountains, hot,
parched, drought-stricken, the necessaries of life dear, its
conveniences and pleasures absent. These conditions in-
crease in intensity the further back one travels, until
west of the Darling, what with climate and absence of
railway communication, life is a perfect hell upon earth.
In every Dept, there is a number of officers, equal in
rank and ability, who are continually being promoted and
transferred. But, while some are mysteriously kept dur-
ing the whole of their service on the eastern side of the
dividing range, others are plainly told that if they desire
promotion they must consent to go to the extreme
western parts of N.S. Wales.
Why is this difference made ? That it is made may
easily be proved by searching the Govt. Gazette and
noticing the towns each officer has been stationed at
since he joined the service. The P.S. Board cannot evade
the responsibility by saying that they depend upon the
recommendations of the various Under-Secretaries ; they
are not obliged to accept those recommendations, and
they either have, or should have, before them, when con-
sidering such matters, the record of each officer’s service.
And they surely know enough of the geography of the
State to be able to estimate the difference between Wal-
gett, Narrabri, Cobar, Bourke, Wilcannia. and Milparinka,
on the one hand, and Armidale, Glen Innes, Goulburn,
Cooma, Yass and Maitland on the other. They are sup-
posed to act impartially. Why do they not do so?
Here are a few recent instances picked at random from
the Gazette 1. 14 years at Nowra —promoted to Glen
Innes. 2. 11 years (from commencement of service) at
Junee; thence to Moruya; thence to Braidwood. 3.
Goulburn to Cooma; Cooma to Braidwood; Braidwood
to a travelling inspectorship. 4. Sydney to Murwillum-
bah; Murwillumbah to Moruya. 5. Bingara to Glen •
Innes ; Glen Innes to Armidale. 6. Tenterfield to Glen
Innes ; Glen Innes to Grafton. And so on. On the other
side I saw a 1 fecent paragraph describing the send-off of
an officer who had been stationed 14 years at Tilpa, on
his promotion to Menindie ; another from Tibooburra to
Carrathool.
It is useless to ask the Board why such things occur,
and equally useless to ask the Under-Secretaries. The
point is—they do occur, and are sapping the morale of the
serviee. The old plain unvarnished system of patronage
was at least candid, since every officer knew it was
hopeless to expect promotion without it. The Board was
appointed to put a stop to the system, but it has
demonstrably failed to do so. If, with a proclaimed
policy of impartiality, patronage still flourishes rankly,
choking individual effort, something must be wrong.
That something must be either the system or the persons
who administer it. Which is it ?

*
TTie Brough season at Sydney Royal will
end, in the ordinary course of things, with “ The
Adventures of Lady Ursula” to-night (Thursday),
and “ The Liars ” to-morrow (Friday). This last
. Brough season closes brilliantly, in an artisttio
way of speaking; it doesn’t close half so brilliantly,
in a financial way of speaking, as it should do.
.Nobody has been killed, or even maimed, in the
rush on the stairs at the Royal, and the demand
for high-class plays produced in good style by a
good company has been frantic, but limited.
1 hat is to say, a few people have franti-
oally demanded these high-class plays, and
a good many haven’t. There is nobody visible
on the furthest, dimmest horizon who is likely to
take Brough’s place. _ Nobody in his line of art,
and with even half his capacity, proposes, so far,
to rush for his job of supplying Australia with a
brilliant new play about every fortnight, at half
London prices. His departure will leave a gap,
which the best , efforts of Robert Henry’s co. and
such-like organisations will be quite insufficient to
fill*. The fact that Brough goes and Pumpkin
Smith and Robert Henry remain is one of those
inscrutable matters that bristle up on this globe’s
surface like the spikes of the incomprehensible
porcupine.
*# * *
The Royal re-opens on Saturday, under
Musgrove’s management, with “A Chinese Honey-
moon.” Why the yellow man should be encouraged
to have a honeymoon when the white man in so
many cases has to take his bride direct to a cheap
hash-emporium The Bulletin knoweth not, but
the fact is there. There are to be 60 voices in the
chorus of the “Chinese Honeymoon,” and con-
ductor Gustave Slapoffski is to whirl the sapling
in the seat of office, and an immensity of pomp,
gorgeousness and frill is understood to have been
expended on the arrangements. The “Chinese
Honeymoon ” is said to be past its 300th night in
London—which may be a recommendation, and
again it may not.
** * *
“San Toy” still progresses merrily at
Sydney Palace, and the fun of it doesn’t become
wearisome with familiarity. Mr. Graham, as Li,
contrives to get a good deal of local color into his
songs, and the reference to the Australian soldiers
going to war amid cheers and coming back “to
get the sack ” always raises a sardonic laugh ; but
there are generally a few returned cohentingenters
in the audience to whom it is a cold, unpleasant
truth, and this section of the crowd laughs under
difficulties. The mandarin Yen How “makes
up ” to imitate a certain church dignitary not now
in Australia, and takes him off to the life in
appearance, voice, and lofty hauteur ; yet it took
a fortnight for anyone to notice it. The dignitary
in question has got a stammer in his nose, and
Mandarin How has got that to perfection.
*& * *
This week sees the last of “ With Flying
Colors,” at Sydney Lyceum. “Riding to Win”
take 3 its place this Saturday. Friday will conse-
quently be the last night of the unconvincing
murder and the convincing railway train, which
has a more solid look than its kind usually
possesses, besides blowing real steam out of itself
in a masterly fashion. Much horse and small
feminine-looking jockey may be looked for in the
next production.
** * #
“ The Lady Slavey ” continues to show
domestic service from its brightest aspect at
Sydney Criterion, while her millionaire admirer
occasionally walks between rows of admiring
ladies in a procession of one, while they chant
their desire to be his gilded bride. Judging from
the type Mr. Bradley presents, the American
millionaire is a much dressier and better-behaved
individual than the Australian sample. The
writer has seen few Commonwealth “million-
aires ” who couldn’t pretty easily be mistaken
for the man who comes for the garbage-box.
** * *
At Sydney Tivoli there are several new
faces and a bioscope, which, in realism, is equal
to, if not ahead of, anything that has been so far
seen in this city. The pictures were taken on
board an Atlantic liner, and the climbing waves
look so real that you can’t stand and look at them
without clutching at something. One visitor was
so much overcome that he clutched at a lady who.
didn’t belong to him, and, as he forgot to return
her, divorce proceedings are pending—at least,
some such rumor is circulated on improbable
authority. Spry, who made a first appearance on
Saturday, is a comedian of the Coleman type, and
gave a turn which was too long to keep the
audience interested to the end. The Drews now
share with Cinquevalli the chief favor of the
public. Illness kept Mrs. Drew off the boards
on Monday, and Mr. Drew very much lessened
the disappointment of the audience by a clever
speech of apology. By the time this issue goes to
press Mrs. Drew will probably be back again,
having her unworthy affection for a dog cured in
summary fashion by a small a but capable and
enraged husband.
** * *
Cinquevalli confesses to 43 years on this
earth, more than a quarter of a century of which
he has spent on the boards. The graceful,
panther-like athlete earns an enormous salary,
and some years ago, having amassed a satisfac-
tory fortune, decided to give up juggling and
retire into private life. At once he became ill.
His muscles shrank away ; his heart began to
fail, and he couldn’t sit down for a few minutes
but his legs would grow cold. Doctors saw him,
E rescribed, and left him in full enjoyment of
is mysterious illness. Then one day, wearied
with sickness and ennui, Cinque, went to a
cupboard and took out some juggling plant. An
hour spent in doing impossible things with a
cannon ball and other trifles acted on him like a
tonic, and in a month or two he was back on the
Btage. “It is better to be a well juggler than a
sick gentleman,” says Cinque, with a smile—
but he is juggler and gentleman, quiet,
unassuming, always. The secret of health, to
his mind, is to balance a chair on the tip of
your nose and catch a heavy weight on the back of
your neck several times a day. Cinquevalli,
though so strong, weighs but lOst. 41bs., and
would be counted lean by most; his greatest care
is not to get too muscular, or “ muscle-bound,” for
his need is to be agile. He, rightly, judges the
cannon-ball feats to be his best display of accurate
Strength.

Sydney Comedy Club, by kind permission
of Robert Brough, will, at the Queen’s Hall, next
Tuesday (Sept. 2nd), produce “The Pickpocket,”
in aid of the funds of that deserving object, the
Women’s Hospital. The plan is now open at
Nicholson’s, and there are hopes that the produc-
tion will be up to the high standard attained by
the club during the past five years.
** * *
Melbourne Royal, where the villain stalks
his prey and graves give up their dead, was a very
crowded place of resort last Saturday night, owing
to “The Ladder of Life.” Probably “The
Ladder ” is the strongest of the Andersonian melo-
dramas, in the purely commercial aspect. Nearly ’
everything that ever happened in any other drama
comes to pass what time Nihilist Kartchikoff and
the wicked nephew are earning their wages of sin.
Kartchikoff arrives early on the scene in a brown
velveteen cycling suit, which sets forth his chub-
biness to great advantage, and the moment he
comes to hand he draws a knife with the intention
of killing .Miss Duggan, whom he doesn’t yet
know by sight. As soon as he learns her identity
he forgets the knife, and thenceforth the
amusing foreigner adopts all possible means of not
effecting his purpose. When he gets the lady
in a chloroformed state he merely puts her under
a Great Nasymth Hammer and pulls a lever so
that the Hammer may smash her gently, but the
instrument of death is such a long-winded delusion
that three cockney Highlanders let themselves
down through the roof and avert the catastrophe.
Later on, the procrastinating Kartchi. is prevented
from killing Miss Duggan by her timely decease.
She prefers to pass away unaided. Miss D. is the
second person to die during this virile entertain-
ment. In the first act Inman’s wooden-headed
parent expires under the impression that Miss
Guildford has been guilty of an intrusive small
boy who calls her “mother.” Also Inman mis-
trusts Miss Guildford on account of this unclaimed
offspring, therefore the virtuous spinster wanders
at large and clings to the small boy in
her sorrow because he still calls her “Mother.”
Meanwhile, a gladsome old hag, whose counten-
ance shames the midnight sun, has been farming
the infant for blackmail purposes, and the Gallant
Gordons have scaled the heights of Dargai with a
screel of bagpipes. Also, Inman is kept out of
his property by a missing codicil which lies hidden
under the bodice of the steam-hammer lady, but
eventually a comic person named M‘Dougall
shoots Kartchikoff at sight, and the codicil is
accepted as proof that the evil nephew has out-
raged the martial law of Devonshire. So
M'Dougall, having murdered the Rooshan with
impunity, orders a file of soldiers to arrest the
nephew on principle, whereupon the clouds roll
away from Miss Guildford. And the waif who
called her “ Mother,” because there was no other,
is happy in her own sweet home, along with
Inman. Truly, a stirring domestic drama of
military interest.
* * • •
“ The Ladder of Life ” spares no expense
in the spectacular department. Ten tame steeds,
20 brass instruments, supported by a big drum,
and more than 100 human auxiliaries in uniform
are paraded before a roaring stage multitude of
lesser numerousness. The heroism of Anderson’s
Gordons is such that the enemy accuses them of
fighting like tigers, and the British Blag asserts
itself more than usual. There are no flies on
“ The Ladder.” Miss Duggan, who acts all the
better for having had a rest, Edmund Duggan,
Miss Guildford, Walter Rivers (as a silent con-
sumer of beer and ’baccer), and especially Miss
Leighford, as the elderly female with the com-
plexion of Coronation-red, deserve a good word
all round. Likewise Bert Bailey, and the violent-
colored scenery, and the gentlemanly villain who
appeared on Saturday instead of Willard.
#* * *
The second Saturday night of “Robin
Hood,” at Her Majesty’s (Melb.), promised a satis-
factory four weeks’ run for DeKoven’s opera. Public
opinion has decided by a considerable majority
that pretty music covers a multitude of weak-
nesses and foolishnesses in this instance. The
composer made a horrible mistake when he con-
fided his work to an unimaginative Smith and
sanctioned the perpetration of a hybrid opera
which lacks even a suspicion of romantic interest.
There was sufficient musical and historical incen-
tive to construct a pretty little story with some
“wholesome fun” and old-time color in it, but
Henry B. Smith missed the ’bus. He might have
borrowed a brighter idea for the plot from some
forgotten “Robin Hood” pantomime-book, and
he could have gone to half-a-dozen literary sources
for words and phrases that sound suitable to the
long-bow period. Smith’s dialogue is annoying.
Miss Florence Young, who plays the comparatively
serious character of Allan-a-Dale, has to say, in re-
ference to the 12th oentury hero, “Although he is
a peer, he is one of the finest bowshots in all
England. What a pity he is not an outlaw! ”
Dame Durden, who ought to be cut out of the
narrative altogether, tells Lauri that she will cook
a steak for him. The modern tone of the libret-
tist’s small-talk grates on one ear whilst the cheery
melodiousness of De Koven’s airs appeals to the
other. Fortunately the song words are inoffen-
sive when distinctly enunciated, and, as they are
mostly unintelligible, the music goes on its
delightful own. A charming number at the begin-
ning of the second act is given by Stannis Leslie
and chorus. The air is very sweet, but nobody
can guess what the foresters are singing about
until Florence Young remarks casually in her
strong, incisive voice that “it takes nine tailors
to make a man.” The audience feels grateful to
the mezzo for throwing light on the subject. Her
warble is ever honest and straightforward. She
sings for half the company and enunciates for the
whole crowd and makes no fuss about it. Also
she acts her part conscientiously, and doesn’t take
long to recover from a sore throat. When the
present scribe hears people remarking that Miss
Young is “very stiff” he acquiesces in the objec-
tion and wishes that a lot more Australian
sopranos would stiffen likewise. Further remarks
on De Koven’s feast of tune are held over till next
week. The performance grows easierall the time,
and Hugh Ward’s sham upper lip is becoming
part of his living self. In fact, the protruding lip
serves as a sounding board and gives, new force to
his limited power of speech. Having started a
resonant lip, Ward had better make a stage fixture
of it.
its * * *
“ Sweet Nell ” will be sassing Jeffries for
the last, last time, at the Princess’s, on Friday
night, and, on Saturday, a new comedy is to pre-
sent Musgrove’s Dramatic Co. in less gorgeous
apparel than it has hitherto worn in Mel-
bourne. The new comedy, written by a lady, is
called “Mice and Men,” and the character of
Peggy, a foundling, which Nellie Stewart has
undertaken, is said to fit that adaptable artist
admirably. Harcourt Beatty appears as “ a
scholar and philosopher,” and Peggy, judging
from her name, is the crude doctrine that breaks
the philosopher up. A theatrical Peggy always
does break things up.
** * *
The Bijou returns to life next Saturday,
when the Hawtrey Comedy Co. will play “ The
Lady of Ostend ” for the first time in Melbourne.
The co. comes this time with the recommendation
of a long career behind it, and the prospect of
some more career before it. Farcical comedy has
been off the local market for six months, or
thereabouts, and the frivolous populace that has
not witnessed a ‘ ‘ convulsing situation ” for half-
a-year is just about ready for a new shriek in
three acts. Persons in delicate health, who have
been warned not to laugh too heartily, can take
courage from the assurance that “Lady of
Ostend ” was written by the author of London
Punch.

Bourke-street by the roots and support the G.P.O.
on his beautifully developed stummick.

This is a time of concerts and casual en-
tertainments in Melbourne, and the halls are busy.
At the Athenaeum Hall the Marvellous Corricks —
and particularly Miss Alice of that ilk—will
appear for five nights, terminating on Saturday.
Next week the Fiske Jubilee Singers (M'Adoo
brand) will return to the Athenaeum and resume
their unorthodox ditties concerning Jordan, and
Moses, and the Ark.
** * *
At the Town Hall next Saturday afternoon
a benefit concert will be tendered to Walter Kirby,
to raise a fund whereby that much-improved
vocalist “may continue his studies in Europe,” a
district which is thusfar foreign to the young man.
The affair will be tendered very gently, for
Walter, as all the ladies know, is just about the
mildest mannered tenor who ever cut a note or
took a purse. An influential committee has taken
up this mellifluous and deserving cause, and the
list of artists on the bill includes the names of
many who rarely appear at Town Hall benefit
musters —Miss Nellie Stewart, Madame Slapoffski,
George Lauri, George Lyding, and Miss Olive
Kingston, the most cultured of young Australian
singers.
* * *
The death of Dick Stewart, the elder, at
Melbourne, last Sunday, brought forth some bio-
graphical notices which must have discon-
certed a number of greybeards. Many grizzly
inhabitants had a complacent way of claim-
ing to be about the same age as “Our
Nellie.” When they said, and they said it
habitually, ‘ ‘ Bless my soul! she’s as old as
I am, if she’s a day,” they were safe from contra-
diction. Their younger hearers could only gaze
at those sexagenarians in astonishment, and re-
mark that Nellie was a wonderful woman for her
years. It was rather a sore point with
the late Mr. Stewart that his clever daughter
had had a decade added to her age by
ungallant ancients, professing to speak from ex-
perience, but the actress who starts her career as
a child is bound to be horribly libelled in that
respect. Dick Stewart landed m Australia 50
years ago as a young man of 25, and, after trying
his luck on the goldfields in N.S.W., he was per-
suaded to take to the stage. Made his first ap-
pearance at the Victoria Theatre, Sydney, early
in ’56, and played there for fully four years before
shifting to Melbourne with Barry Sullivan,
in 1860. It was in Sydney, after he became
recognised as a rising comedian, that handsome
Dick Stewart married Mrs. Guerin, a popular
actress and singer, who survives him. Their two
children, young Dick and Nellie, were born in
Sydney prior to the Melbourne engagement in
1860. Any elderly cynic who can make out
Nellie Stewart as old as he is on those
figures, bless his soul ! must be a mar-
vellous mathematician. In 1877 Stewart pere
took his two stepdaughters, Maggie and Doey,
and daughter Nell, on a tour through Maoriland,
America, and England. They were back in Mel-
bourne for the Christmas Panto, at the Royal in
’7B, and it was then that the youthful, travelled
Nellie of Ours made her first appearance as a
principal boy. The Bulletin rises to affirm, not
necessarily on oath but as an answer to future
disputants, that the Nellie who played principal
boy in Melb. Princess’s panto, last Xmas, was
“ about 18 ” when she got her first engagement in
that capacity 24 years ago next Xmas. To err ia
human where dates are concerned. To err grace-
fully is divine.
»* * *
Melbourne Princess’s was closed on Mon-
day night out of respect for the late Mr. Stewart.
On Tuesday “Sweet Nell” was to be resumed
with Miss Sadler in the name part for that night,
perhaps for the remainder of the week.
** * *
At Adelaide Royal, Charles Holloway has
produced “Dangerous Women,” a title that may
fairly be used for almost any of his plays. This
one ends with the coming to life of a drugged
heroine in a vault, but does not noticeably resem-
ble “ Romeo and Juliet ”in other respects. Ade-
laide has supported Holloway so well that he is
about to move over to the Tivoli and play a
further season there. Comic opera will reign in
his stead. The Tivoli itself has been doing splen-
did business at slightly increased prices during the
Sandow boom, but has now for the moment closed
its doors. The co. is working along to Melbourne
via Bendigo and Ballarat, a projected Broken
Hill season having fallen through. The strong
man introduced some showy feats with a big grey
horse towards the last. In one of the most effec-
tive, an assistant lies flat on the stage, and
Sandow, lifting him by the belt with one hand,
tosses him into the saddle. Tom Nawn and the
Wilton Brothers helped to make up an excellent
programme.

• * • *
The prospectus of the Commonwealth Mutual
Insurance Co. (for fire, marine and any other kind of in-
surance, life excepted) is published in Sydney, with the
names of a strong directorate attached. J. E. Ducker,
of the Mutual Life Buildings, Sydney, acts as broker. It
is 16 years since a new fire insurance co. (the North
Queensland) was founded in the Commonwealth and
survived, for the Victoria General was merely a reor-
ganisation of an old business. And a woeful number of
Australian fire offices have died, perished, burst, or
sold out—mostly sold out—since the North Queens-
land (which is a Sydney institution, despite its name)
was born. Both the Queensland offices, all four
S.A. offices, six N.S.W., five Victorian, and three
Tasmanian fire insurance companies have disappeared
in sixteen years! Possibly more have vanished, for
the writer speaks principally from memory. For
the sake of keeping Australian business in Australian
hands, and retaining the profit in the country,
the new company is a thing to be highly commended—
and, if it had sufficient interest in the institutions of its
own country to keep its bank account in an Australian
bank instead of an English one, it would be still more
commendable. The fact that it is a non-tariff office would
be a pity if the tariff, as it is in Sydney, was not so
ridiculously ill-managed, and, in some cases, so ex-
tortionate. The nominal capital of the Common-
wealth Mutual Insurance Co. is £250,000. At pre-
sent, 100,000 £1 shares are offered, ss. to be paid up, and,
if they are all sold, the co. will start with £25,000 paid-up
capital. Very few Australian offices began on so large a
scale as that. In the days when Sydney was the head-
quarters of eleven fire offices (instead of five, and one of
them really in English hands, as at present), and you fell
over a fire office at every corner, only three of them had
over £20,000 paid-up capital. In these days (1888), about
the biggest fire and marine offices in the Federal States
were the Cornwall of Tas. (£BO,OOO paid up), South Aus-
tralian (£62,500), Mercantile Marine of S.A. (£50,000),
Southern, Vic. (£50,C00), U.A.M. of Vic. (£31,858),
Australian Mercantile Union of Vic. (£33,655), Australian
General of N.S.W. (£42,000), Sydney Lloyds (£33,240)—a1l
as dead as Julius Caesar. Luckily, the business seems to
have grown more stable of late years and the existing
offices are mostly doing well. In fact, some of them are
doing so well that the profits screwed out of their clients
amount to an outrage.

The Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank
(Melbourne), with a revenue that is not improving
materially and with no special immediate prospects, con-

balance was written off out of forfeited capital, results

The earnings represent 6s. 7d. per cent, per annum on the
paid-up capital of £920,048 (£558,655 preference and
£361,393 ordinary), or 10s. lid. per cent, per annum on
preference capital only. How long the A. D. and M.
Bank hopes to carry on with its dividend absorbing nearly
twice the earnings is a mystery. A Writing-down of
capital is talked of, and is certainly much needed. If the
ordinary capital was wholly written off, and the preference
capital written down to £lOO,OOO, the A. D. and M. Bank
would be able to pay 3 per cent, dividends on the basis of
its present earnings and have a little—not very much-
over. That would mean a writing-off of £820,048, which
would be a very drastic proceeding, but it is quite evident
that a considerable sacrifice is required to put the institu-
tion on a good working basis again. At present the new
deposit business appears to be dying away to nothing,
current accounts being only £9527. A little, but only a
little, is being done at'repaying the old reconstructed
deposits, which were reduced during the past half-year
by £1153 (from £413,269 to £412,116). While the present
condition of things lasts the A. D. and M. Bank is almost
wholly a property co., holding about £1,300,000 worth of
mortgages and foreclosed assets left on its hands from
the days before ’93. And until it writes off all losses it
doesn’t seem likely to get together a large new business
again.

luck shown above. There are still £601,540 of debentures to

further calls. When a sufficient amount has been paid off,
the reduction in the interest bill will enable the Land
Mortgage Bank to show a profit, and then a continuous
improvement may be looked for. At present, however,
the investments yield a gross return of only £3 10s. 7d!
per cent, per annum, and, when allowance is made for
working expenses, the £860,000 worth of assets don’t
yield enough to pay 3£ per cent, interest on the £601,540
worth of debentures. There is no possibility of doing
new business under present conditions, and the Land
Mortgage Bank is merely a property-holding concern.

have just been completed at the engineering works of
Mephen Ferguson, Melbourne. The principal item of

Terms, £1 a month. Write for plans and particulars to
The Esperance Land Company, Nicholson Ohambers,
Swanston-st., Melbourne.*
* * • •
Many doubtful claims have been advanced on
behalf of duplex pumps. Some makers state that their
duplex pumps are “ double the capacity of single pumps
of same diameter and stroke.” An A. S. Cameron single-
acting pump can be run safely and comfortably at a speed
that would knock a duplex pump to pieces. But even at
a low speed, duplex pumps seldom attain even their
theoretical capacities, and the reason for this can be
readily explained. Apply to the Agents, Messrs. W. and
J. Lempriere, 506 Little Collins Street, Melbourne, for
further particulars. The A. S. Cameron pumps are noted
for their simplicity, durability, and absence of outside
valve gear.*
* * * *
The Mount Lyell Mining and Railway Coy.
lately decided to instal electric power transmission plant,
to drive portions of their extensive plant, and call for
competetive tenders for the work. The contract was
awarded to the Austral Otis Engineering Company of
South Melbourne, Australian agents for the well-known
firm Continental firm Ganz and Co. The plant consists
of two sets of generators direct coupled to high speed
compound engines, each 120 horse power, and four
motors, together with all the electric connections and
switchboards. The Austral Otis Company have also
lately installed as agents for Granz and Co. a complete
lighting and power plant for the Great Southern Consols
Gold Mine, Rutherglen, including electric locomotives
for underground haulage. To enable them to give com-
petent and reliable advice to users of electric plant, they
have engaged on their staff a highly trained expert
electrician from the works of Granz and Co., Buda Pest,
Hungary.*

Long Tunnel amalgamation project has been
further complicated by a proposal to take over the two
mines, on behalf of an English eo. This proposal has
intensified the difference of opinion between the co’s as
to the relative values of the two mines. The project for
the formation of an English co. is a good one, and indi-
cates a desire on the part of English investors to acquire
sound producing properties rather than mere prospecting
ventures. Should the project for the purchase by an
English syndicate fall through, Long Tunnel co. has
signified its willingness to submit the question of values
to arbitration.

Tenders have been received for the long-post-
poned Sydney-North Shore bridge, and it appears that
the structure can be built for something under £1,500,000,
which isn’t so very much after all. The work is not a
State undertaking in any fair or proper sense of the term,
though so far no one seems to dream of regarding it
otherwise ; it is strictly municipal, and should be carried
out by the Council of Greater Sydney. There is no need
to go into debt for it either. The building of the bridge
would spread over two, or possibly three, years, and a
special rate of 2s. in the £, levied on the metropolitan
area for three years, would pay the whole bill. That
would make the rates of Greater Sydney for these three
years about 3s. 6d. or 3s. 9d. in the £, and in England the
average is about ss. or 6s. in the £—not for three years
only, but as a regular thing. Sydney has been waiting
and crying out to the State for 40 years for a bridge that
it could have built itself any day without turning a hair,
and that any British city of its size would have built long
ago, as a matter of course.

The N.S.W. Fresh Food and loe Co., by the
way, has an imposing asset in its balance-sheet: “ Plant,
Goodwill , etc., £208,517.** Goodwill items are always
objectionable, and always dubious—especially in the case
of a co. whioh isn’t paying dividends, for then it is
exceedingly doubtful if the goodwill is saleable at any
price. And when the amount of the dubious item is con-
cealed it is especially open to objection.

The total assets of the Mutual Life Insurance Co. of New
York at the close of 1901 were £71,975,777. Its income is
larger than the Government revenues of Bulgaria,
Denmark, Greece, Holland, Portugal, Roumania, Servia,
Sweden, Norway, Switzerland, of any of the seven States
of Australasia, of any of the South American republics,
bar possibly Brazil; of any Central American State, or
Mexico, or any of the smaller German principalities. In
fact, the States which have a larger income can almost be
counted on the fingers.
The expense rate of the Mutual Life of New
York continues to be rather heavy, though it is distinctly
improving. This is how it shows up :
Expenses Compared with Total In6ome.
1895 .. £2l 8 1 per cent. 1899 .. £l9 12 2 per cent.
1896.. 20 4 6 „ 1900.. 20 10 11 „
1897.. 18 4 9 „ 1901.. 19 16 9
1898 .. 17 16 1 „
If the revenue of the Mutual Life of New York, like that
of Australian offices, consisted solely of premiums and
interest, it would have only been about £13,400,000 last
year, and the expense rate would have been over 21 per
cent. But the sale of bonds and securities at a profit
added £864,509 to the income (in depressed Australia it
is generally rather a case of writing off depreciation of
the assets), and this greatly increased the revenue, and
correspondingly reduced the expense rate. Taking all
things into account, however, the stupendous Mutual of
New York is not a very cheaply-managed office.
The interest earnings last year equalled £4
os. 7d. per cent, per annum on the average funds for the
year, but the interest and profit on investments taken
together represented £5 ss. 6d. per cent., which is an un-
usually large return and one calculated to help the
bonuses along materially. The extra revenue from the
improvement in the value of the investments keeps on
steadily year after year, and considering the remarkable
prosperity of the United States, it seems that it may
do so almost indefinitely. The Mutual Life of New
¥ork has a list of assets to which no exception can be-
taken, barring always that item “ balance of the year’s
premiums when paid annually or quarterly, deductible
from the amount assured in case of claim.” This dubious
paper item amounts to only £402,024, and out of the sur-
plus of nearly £13,000,000 the co. could write off such a
miserable and obscure “asset” without its loss being
noticed. Why it doesn’t do so is a mystery. The Mutual
Life of New York is an exceedingly solid and prosperous
institution, and, considering how enormously its business
has grown even in the last seven years, the point at
which its expansion will cease (presumably everything
must cease somewhere, some day) is a matter for interest-
ing speculation.

Pitt, Son and Badgery, Limited (Sydney)
closed what looks like an exceedingly good year on 30th
June, 1902, though there is a legend that the last
12 months have been a period of unmitigated disaster to
everybody connected, directly or indirectly, with the
pastoral industry. There is still, however, a complete
silence on the matter of dividends:—

The reserve fund has remained stationary at £7500 till it
becomes a weariness. The profits for the year to June
1902, show a splendid increase, but the necessity of pro-
viding for “ bad and doubtful debts ” swallows them up
as usual. During the past six years £33,038 has been
applied to provide for bad and doubtful debts, which
is a good deal in the case of a co. whose assets, even on
paper, only total £110,839. Among them, by the way
there is a paper or goodwill item of unknown amount (it
reads “Goodwill, office furniture, plant, &c., £25,000 ”
and how much of it is goodwill doesn’t appear) which
requires writing off as well as the " bad and doubtfu
debts.” No debt is worse or more doubtful in bad times
than goodwill. Nothing whatever is said as to how
much more “ bad and doubtful debts ” there may be to
write off before dividends are resumed, but a good sign
of progress is the reduction of the liabilities, which has
progressed as follows

however, the reduction made in the aggregate liabilities
during the past year is less than usual.
• * * •
London County Council proposes starting a
fleet of municipal ferry-boats on the Thames. It will
probably succeed, because in England the profits of such
enterprises are generally applied in reduction of the
original debt till it is wiped out, and then if bad times
come there are only working expenses to pay. It would
probably fail in Australia, because here the profits would
be spent as they came in, and even repairs would be
partly paid for out of loans, and finally the enterprise
would carry a bigger debt than it could stagger under.
The greatest enemies of State Socialism in Australia are
its own advocates, who allow a grand principle to be dis-
credited by dishonest administration.

• * * *
S.A. Govt., which is making some kind of a try to
keep its accounts square, has not .yet sacrificed the State
Wine and Produce Depot in London, though tempted to
do it by a Royal Commission which lately sat, with the
Agent-General as chairman, in London. Ex.-Gov. Kin-
tore, who has a keen scent for divs., proposes a co. to
take up the business, the Govt, putting up about half
the capital of £70,000 ; and the Commission half-heartedly
recommends this, or something like it. The trouble is
that the depot takes the wines that are sent to it and
makes the best of them, and does not, as an ordinary
trader would, select and buy only the wines for which
there is a market; though why the State shouldn’t do
what every sane private person does is not clear. And
anyhow it seems a bad time to quit the business now ;
for while in the years ’9B and ’99 it cost in advertising
and subsidies £7975 to sell 139,399 gallons, 226,519 gallons
were sold in 1900 and 1901 for £4962. Apparently a very
big success is not far off.

Dear B., —Seeing that sec. 26 of the Melbourne
Tramways Act, ’B3, gives Parliament the right, at any
time after ’99, to revise the fares oharged by the co.—
without said 00. being entitled to any compensation—one
wonders why Melbourne citizens and councillors haven’t
moved Parliament to “ revise ” the fares to 2d. all round
long ago. Then, instead of the grasping monopoly urging
the value of its unexpired concession as a reason why it
demands an outrageous price to be bought out, it would
have represented itself as a poverty-stricken body on the
verge of insolvency—-and could have been suddenly
brought to a state of sweet reasonableness. Is it that
too many councillors and Ms.L.A. are shareholders?—
F.O.

Over the recent forgery of Hustler’s scrip, Mr.
C. W. Long, one of the best-esteemed members of Sydney
Stoek Exchange, loses £lBOO or so. The “ plant ” was
most deliberately “put up” on him, and seems to have
been an absolutely perfect exposition of the confidence-
trick. This is the first forged scrip which has been
accepted by Mr. Long’s office in over 30 yearß.
• * * *
The most important change made for many a
day in the internal management of the B.H. Prop, is the
abolition of night-shift (midnight to 8 a.m.). Night
shift means that the man has to try to sleep by day ; and,
in a climate like Broken Hill’s, that is often impossible.
Result, limp workmen and poor results. The change is
possible through the gradual opening up of much wider
faces of ore than formerly, so that the men on the
other two shifts can be increased without crowding. To
keep these big faces of ore continually available the
1000 ft. level is now being developed. So far, only Block
10 works ore at that depth. The Sulphide Corporation is
also preparing to explore at 1000 ft. It has not a deep
enough shaft on its own mine, but has bought an old
prospecting venture next door (the Extended, which
thought it was going to get the underlay), and is driving
from that.
Of B.H. Prop.’s 960,000 shares, 632,735 are now
on the London register. Adelaide holds 162,016, Mel-
bourne 102,878, Sydney 43,138.
• * * «
The market was justified in its scare at the news
of the movement underground in B.H. Block 10 ; for
there was absolutely no telling where it would stop. As
it turns out, the close re-filling of worked-out ground
with refuse brought the creep to a standstill before
much actual harm was done. It is worth noting that
most of these movements take place on the boundary of
two mines—in this case, Block 10 and the Central (Sul-
phide Corporation). The truth is that each co. depends
on the other to keep that section of the lode propped up
for its own sake, and in the end both of them take out too
much ore and put in too little support, and the trouble
then isn’t long in coming. On the whole, Block 10 is
well plugged up ; but it needs it all, for it occupies a
most ricketty section of the lode.
The people on the other side who are generally
able to give some idea of the course of the metal markets
have been owning lately that they don’t know. So far as
they think they may know a little, copper and zinc
(spelter) prospects are good, tin is uncertain : lead, bad.
So far as lead is concerned, what disturbs them is that
the imports to the U.K. go on increasing in spite of the
low price. With the highest at £l7 10s., 98,378 tons were
imported in the first half of 1900 ; with the highest at £l6
10s. in the first half of 1901, the imports got up to 107,231
tons ; now, in the past half-year, with £ll 16s. 3d., as the
top price, 116,942 tons came in. Meanwhile the exports
keep fa11ing—25,952 tons in 1900, 26,686 tons in
1901, 20,433 tons this year. There isn’t a single big
lead-producing country bar Australia that is not dump-
ing in more of the metal, apparently in an effort to even
up profits ; Australia can’t do that because—except as to
two or three mines—the more ore they would handle at
present prices the bigger their loss would be. Of the
98,378 tons imported to the U.K. in the first half of 1900,
Spain supplied 41,867 tons, U.S. 18,560 tons, Australia,
30,207 tons; in the first half of this year Spain sent in
49,272 tons, U.S. 28,968, and Australia 30,964. It is just
possible that U.S. lead-producers can’t keep this going ;
the recent vague suggestion of an Eastern States lead-
combine looks like that.|

Not less than a quarter of a million in solid cash
has been subscribed for N.S.W. dredging propositions
within the past four years— i.e., for dredges and working
expenses, and probably over £lOO,OOO more has been paid
for shares over and above capital value. Total returned
in divs. to date is £10,125 —£6000 from Araluen and £4125
from Jembaicumbene. These are the only places likely
to return further divs., and if they are credited with
£30,000 more, the ghastliness of the boom of three and
four years ago is made strikingly manifest. The average
value of the eight co.’s, shares of which are now market-
able, is only a trifle over 50 per cent, of the capital value—
. which doesn’t seem to indicate that the speculative public
regard these “as good industrial concerns,” or believe
that the industry “ never looked so hopeful.”
• * • *
“Jack” Pearce, manager of Mt. David, reports
that the mine is “ one of great possibilities, even prob-
abilities.” Mine has been working for five years, and the
prophecy that it now has “ great probabilities ” may be
some solace to people who were induced to pay big prices
for shares. Pearce is a capable manager, who deserves
control of a good mine, and for his sake writer hopes that
the probabilities about which he writes will soon become
realities.

Some enthusiasts who are supposed to be inte-
rested in Payne’s process for leaching copper ores talk
glibly about floating a £500,000 co. to run the process in
N.S.W. only—the rights elsewhere are to be worth count-
less millions I Time enough to talk in this strain when
the process'has been proved successful and when patents
have been secured, for it. Paragraphs, evidently inspired,
appeared in some of the dailies, pronouncing the bulk
experiment on Girilambone ore a complete success. As a
matter of fact, the experiment was not half completed
when the statement was published. There has been
significant silence since the test was finished.

Burbank’s Birthday Gift, Coolgardie (W.A.),
seems to have taken a new lease of life. The reef, which
was supposed to have completely petered out going south,
has again been located, after driving a short distance,
wider and richer than ever. Levels at 180, 250, 330 and
430 ft, are following it with marked success. Orushings
during last four months have averaged about 29000 z.
monthly from 25dwt. ore.
• * * •
Princess Royal, Norseman (W.A.), is opening
up splendidly at 200, 300 and 380 ft. levels, and is looking
better than at any previous time in its history. Last
yield of 21510 z. from 1709 tons is far the best on record,
and may be eclipsed in near future. Outlook for divs.
rosy.
• * * «
Childe Harold, Laverton (W.A.), like most of
the mines in that much-belauded district, is a very one-
horse proposition, and will never pay divs. unless worked
on a much larger scale. Yield of 805 oz. from 1831 tons
would read all right ff quantity were multiplied by ten,
but such a low average as 9dwts. needs wholesale handling
to give shareholders any hope of return.

Directors of Hannan’s Brown Hill (W. A.) boast
in their annual report of their low costs. Figures prove
the emptiness of their boast. During year, co. treated
20,700 tons for 51,9000 z., worth £192,500 ; net profit was
£106,500, making net costs £86,000, or £4 Is. lOd. a ton.
Cost of Diehl treatment is given as £1 4s. BJd., so that
mining totted up £2l7s. lfd. I Nothing written off for
depreciation, as plant was paid for by shares valued at
par. The mining expenses are excessive, and the Diehl
process costs exactly double what Ivanhoe’s battery treats
sulphide ore for. And this is what Oroya shareholders
have saddled themselves with.
• * • *
Great Boulder Proprietary (WA.), for some in-
scrutable reason, has almost suspended sinking during
the last three months, the figures reading nil, 2ft. and
34ft. respectively. Nansen’s shaft, which was to prove
the continuation of the Ivanhoe lodes to the north, seems
to have been abandoned as a vain hope. The main shaft
is close on 1600 ft., but apparently there is no keen desire
to get there. The Merton furnaces are now in course
of erection, but whether they are to aid or replace the
Edwards type is not quite clear. Latter alternative
seems more likely.

* • * *
Ivanhoe (W.A.), continues to open satisfactorily
and shows little change. Part of new battery to replace
original 60 head has arrived, and will be substituted
gradually so as to disturb output as little as possible.
July return £45,600. Total costs £21,600. Net profit
£24,000. Cost of mining given at 14s. as against 12s. 9d.
for June, an advance of 10 per cent. This is squared by
less being expended on capital account.
* * * »
Paddington Consols (W. A.), after a stoppage of
over 18 months brought about by the London and Globe
smash, is again under weigh, but some weeks must elapse
ere all is got into working order. Developments are
rather backward, as the 40-head mill under Hartmann,
subsequently of L.V. Consols, was run to its utmost capa-
city regardless of the future. Mine is a 12dwt. proposi-
tion, but the lode is big and easily worked.

Ex-Mayor Tom Doyle, of Kanowna (W.A.),
has had a further clean-up from his sensationally rich
leader ; result, 2680 z. from 10 tons. This brings up total
to date to 13280 z. from 32 tons. The leader continues to
develop well, and the genial Tom looks in a fair way to
realising a handsome fortune from it.

pended on, divs. might come. The great shortening of
the chute at No. 6,7, and 8 levels is the breaker ahead
which may wreck the show.

* * * *
After years of battling with water and drift, the
Prentice and Southern Co. (Rutherglen, Vic.) appears to
be nearing the producing stage. Boring has proved the
lead payable for a width of over 300 ft. The water diffi-
culty has been mastered, the pressure on the bore pipes
having been reduced lllbs. in a fortnight, now registering
only 41bs. per sq. in. This co.’s property extends to the
Vic. Murray boundary, and should its operations be
attended with the anticipated success, it is not unlikely
that steps will be taken to work the leads on the N.S.W.
side. That will be a mammoth undertaking, which will
cost certainly not less than £160,000 before puddlers can
be got to work.

Re the Quiraing. Does the N.S.W. Govt,
intend to appoint a Special Commission of
Inquiry ? Not unless it is forced —of that Parlia-
ment may be certain. There was a very marked
desire from the first not to make any fuss about the
matter. On July 10th, when Mr. Nobbs (for Mr.
Oakes) asked a question in the Assembly, he was
informed that the Quiraing had a registered ton-
nage of 653 tons, and she carried 814 tons of coal.
To quote question and answer literally from the
official paper:—
Q. How many tons of coal had she on board when
leaving Newcastle ?
A. 814 tons of coal.
But on Aug. 18 (when, by the way, the true facts
had been published in one of the Sydney news-
papers) there was this question and answer in the
Assembly:—
Q. What was the amount of coal carried as cargo ; also
the amount carried in the bunkers ?
A. The amount of coal carried was:—Cargo, 438 tons
East Greta best, 384 tons East Greta small ; bunker,
148 tons Co-operative small, 165 tons Rhondda. Total
Cargo, 822 tons ; total bunker, 317 tons. Total, 1139 tons.
The first question (J uly 10) was precise enough—
“how many tons were on board” — but the answer
was incorrect by over 300 tons, and the true facts
(registered tonnage, 653 tons; coal cargo, 1139
tons) had to be dragged out, so to speak. No, the
N.S.W. Government is not exactly
hankering after a full Quiraing
inquiry. - -
A HARD WORKER .
“And, to look at her, would you believe that the
works like a horse? "
“Urn! Ah, yaas?—well, p’waps-like a clothes,
horse, eh?”
Despite all the evidence as to the un-
pleasant habits of the men on the Drayton
Grange, and the want of discipline, also the com-
plete adherence to the Admiralty regulations,
there remains ever the damning fact —admitted
by the ship’s captain, and practically by the
transport officers—that the responsible Imperial
authorities sent to sea some 2000 men in a vessel
which had boats and life-saving appliances for
only 1000. That is to say, they deliberately sent
1000 “ defenders of the Empire ” on a long
voyage, knowing well that, in the event of a
disaster, they would have had to stand to attention
and go down with the ship. And who knows
how many were lost overboard ? When an
alarm was raised one night that a man had
rolled overboard from the roofs of the horse-boxes,
and the only muster during the voyage took place,
the roll showed one man extra. Yet a man with
a good war record—Trooper Wilson, of the Duke
of Edinburgh’s Rifles and the A.C.H. —writes to
Melb. Age stating positively that there were 473
stowaways on board when the ship left Durban ;
and the captain said he had to supply five extra
messes for men of whom he had no account, and
for whom he got no pay. Where are all these
men ?
* • • *
A particularly shabby trick was tried on,
in connection with the deaths of the victims of the
Drayton Grange scandal. In the cases where the
deceased troopers were from other States, claims
from alleged relatives were put in for their per-
sonal effects. In one instance the authorities
had almost handed over the articles, when by the
merest chance the “grief-stricken mother” was
proved to be a fraud. As the name of the dead
man was a common one, the would-be swindler
was given the benefit of the doubt. But as the
trick was tried more than once, the officials re-
gretted their clemency afterwards.
• * • •
So far as the stories of the various parties
go, George Savey, the alleged Frenchman or
Spaniard, who hangs disreputably on the out-
skirts of the Dandenong (Vic.) tragedy, is the only
one, bar the lawyers, who makes a dividend out
of it. Old man Sangal is dead. His widow,
Selina, and her alleged accomplice, Tisler, are in
prison, awaiting trial. But Savey says that he
was brought down from Melbourne by Tisler with
the promise of a job, that he was lavishly shouted
for by Tisler, and that a revolver was then given
him, and he was told that the job was the shoot-
ing of old man Sangal—cash on delivery of corpse.
And Savey, by his own statement, promptly fled
back to Melbourne and sold the “ gun ” of a trust-
ing fellow-creature, and kept the half-crown.
Then he added insult to larceny as a bailee by
telling his story to the police. On the stage
the old style of bravo still survives—gentlemen
shot at their own residences, promptness and
secrecy guaranteed, orders for the removal of
relatives attended to punctually and without need-
less splash on the carpet, families and schools
extirpated at reduced rates, and so on. But in
real life it seems that someone sidles up to a
wharf-laborer and fills him with beer, and then
puts a revolver into his unsteady hand, and he
straightway runs for his life and sells the weapon
at a great reduction.
- • * * *
One of the brightest and most pleasing
features of the Dandenong tragedy is contained in
Russian-Finn Tisler’s confession. In the most
casual way, Tisler remarks : “I hit him on the
head and then I finished him.” For directness
and simplicity this almost equals the Biblical
“And he slew him.” Tisler describes, in a
matter-of-fact way, how Mrs. Sangal cleared up
the blood from her husband ; and adds: “1
wanted to go away, but she would not let me ; so
I stayed on till about six o’clock.” The picture of
it : The woman and her paramour breathing love
to each other with the corpse of the dead husband
n the floor!
• * • *'
The Victorian Chief Justice has decided
that an ex-convict is not barred by the Statute of
Limitations from bringing an action against a
gaol-surgeon for alleged unskilful treatment while
he was in prison. This seems consistent with
good sense and Equity. But N.S.W. Supreme
Court, a couple of years ago, ruled that an ex-
prisoner had no action against the State when,
through the negligence of the Govt., he lost an
eye by a boiler accident whilst in Trial Bay
prison.
* * * *
A sight to make unregenerate Gentiles
weep tears of blood was that of 28 stout, long-
nosed gents, who attended at Melb. City Court
t’other week when a lad was charged with helping
himself to the jewellery-stock of his employers.
Every one of those 28 gents, arose in turn to depose
that he was a pawnbroker, and had, in the good-
ness of his heart, “ lent ” said lad various sums on
different articles, which he (the lad) had deceived
them into accepting as cycling trophies won by
the sweat of his brow. The bench was so over-
come at this story of betrayed innocence that it
gave the prisoner six months hard, and authorised
the robbed firm to recover its goods from the 28
noses by paying half-price on the tickets.
* • * *
A recent flagrant Victorian money-lending
case once more emphasises the necessity of abolish-
ing the honorary-justice system. No mere J.P.
should ever be allowed to adjudicate (especially in
trading cases) where a police magistrate is pro-
curable. When present writer first attended
Melbourne police-courts as a small boy reporter
he was astonished at the ever-recurring evidences
of magisterial, lodge and trading-guild corruption;
and the stink has cried aloud for disinfection ever
since.

An alleged “great reduction” in the fare to
Flemington racecourse brings it down to 2s. for a
first-class return. This is 100 per cent, more than
is demanded of visitors to the adjacent Vic. Agri-
cultural Show, who are themselves overcharged.
In short, the race-going sinner is not to be fairly
treated by comparison with other excursionists of
a more holy type—he is merely going to be less
plundered than formerly. The railway depart-
ment, like the excise department, regards the
sinner as a fair mark for special taxation. Ihe
sinner suffers that the saint may be able to
point the finger of scorn at his extravagance.
Chadband snuffles complacently whilst England
drinks itself out of debt ” and smokes a surplus
for Revenue. If everybody swore-off intoxicating
liquor and joined the Anti ; Tobacco League,
whereof Chadband is an oily pillar, the Revenue
would probably have to levy a poll-tax on followers
of the Terewth. But as long as the bad man con-
tinues to smoke, drink and try his luck at
Fleming ton he must pay the Revenue for the
privilege of losing his soul, and the saint
will continue to feel the worldly benefit of him.
Why, then, cannot Chadband and Ebenezer look
kindly on the d—d one the little time they are
with him here below ? They won’t have to endure
his presence hereafter.
The A.J.C. has instituted a new method
of issuing ladies’ tickets to members. In
future books are to be given instead of the ordinary
cardboard pass, and a ticket torn out (and collected
at the entrance-gates) when required. Ttyisshould
effectually block the misuse of tickets (the A.J.C.
being troubled by the fact that more tickets are
presented by ladies than are issued). The new sys-
tem should also prove useful to those members who,
having lent their tickets, find difficulty in getting
them back from their friends without verging on
the rude. There is, however, the objection that
“ undesirables ” will more easily obtain possession
of a casual ticket, when, perhaps, a giddily-
inclined member would not allow his own particu-
lar tickets to be used. This point is always a sore
one with Randwick grandstand goers.

“A STUDENT OF
FASHION ”:
Young lady who objects to
the trailing skirt.
Divorce robs
marriage of half its terrors.
Be the squabble over an asylum doctor
who is alleged to have broken down in
mind and nerve. It would be a fair thing, in
view of the allegations frequently made against
medical men at asylums, to institute periodical
examinations as to their own mental soundness
and fitness for these positions. The majority of
asylum doctors seem, after a few years of constant
contact with lunatics, to get some germ gnawing
at their own brains. One has only to compare
them with ordinary sane persons to note their
strange manner and optical expression—the same
expression that frequently characterises the out-
and-out lunatic. Drug-taking is alleged in the
present instance, and there are probably few
asylum medicoes free of that habit. For that
matter, their colleagues outside notoriously owe
their downfall in many cases to the same insidious
habit.
* * • *
Adelaide Supreme Court Bench has now
set up quite a rule to prevent the publication of
evidence in important cases, and the result is that
hearings in camera are becoming very common.
The anomaly is that, whereas the newspapers are
prevented from publishing the evidence, the
general public are not excluded from the galleries.
PROPHETIC.
THE FORGER (who has just got seven years ): “ Ah,
now I remember the last words of Cecil
Rhodes: ‘So little done—so much to do!’ ”
The lamentable Coronation Dinner to the
Poor of Melbourne— i.e., 500 of them—duly came
off, and its promoter, L. L. Lewis, got the advt.
for which his soul had hungered. The advt. was
mostly pictorial, though there was also a news-
paper paragraph recording the enthusiasm with
which the feeders drank the only two toasts —the
health of the King and the Queen, and the health
of Mr. and Mrs. L. L. Lewis. The pictorial part
of the ad. was supplied by Melbourne Leader in
two photos, of the poor as they appeared at table.
Each picture showed L. L. Lewis standing erect
between two lines of seated figures with his
philanthropic gaze towards the camera. In the
one he stands empty-handed, but in the other
picture he is seen bearing a vegetable-dish. Those
newspaper paragraphs show a simply disgusting
lack of newspaper decency. Many of the Corona-
tion diners, it is true, positively enjoyed the
chance to be made a charitable show of, and their
faces were turned to the photographer; but others
hid their heads in shame to escape the parade of
their faces with the brand of pauper on them in a
public print. Why couldn’t the Leader have
contented itself with photographing Mr. Lewis,
the soup, the meat, and other insensate things ?

“ Sprat ” on a social question :
After 12 years of personal observation, I conclude that
female teachers escape the altar oftener than female fol-
lowers of any other trade or profession. I can tally over
SO lady teachers in the vicinity of 40 years—and unwed,
ihe proportion of girl pupil teachers and young assist-
ants of average type of looks, and personal attractiveness,
compares about equally with outside the ranks. The
allurements of the over-30 crowd are generally ininmsrc.
Either from her own choice or Mister Hobson’s, Miss Roll-
call keeps at teaching the nippers instead of rearing them.
A hundred scribes will rush at me to remark that!
generalise on the dregs, and ignore those who drop from
the ranks. I dispute the point. Defections from the
school-room to the bridal veil are markedly fewer than
from any other individual source. Men endure the et.ain
of the school-room without physical penalty beyond
ordinary wear and tear, and they marry. Women seldom
survive, say, ten years’ school-teaching without seriout
Physical deterioration.

Writes '‘Veteran” :
Gen. Hutton has invented a fine new game for his
officers. He is going to send them out for “staff-rides
in the spring and autumn ” (why other seasons are barred
is not stated, unless it is feared that they will get wet or
sunburnt). No doubt it will be great fun, especially as
each “ ride” is to last three or four days—a picnic at
public expense. The troops required for instruction
during these “ staff-rides ” will be represented “ by a few
mounted and dismounted men from the permanent
troops.” A few ! Yet a regiment, according to the new
drill-book, will form a line half a mile long, a brigade a
line a mile and a half or two miles. Every one of these
“ staff-rides” would cost, on the most moderate computa-
tion, £lOOO. No wonder Defence Minister Lyne is in-
dignant and wants to know who has authorised such a
preposterous scheme. Perhaps the funniest thing about
the affair is that Gen. Finn of N.S.W. told Sydney D.T.
that it would “ teach the officers to write orders.” Any
fool who can write two consecutive sentences of under-
standable English can “write orders "if he knows what
to put into them.
LOSS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She gave the day its heart of fire,
She gave the night her soul of flame ;
The sun and moon, translated through
Her love, as gods became.
She filled me with unearthly strength,
A power not of my own was mine:
She passed, and crumbled into dust
And ashes, my divine.
The Night knows not how fair she is,
Before the stars come in the sky :
It is the light within ourselves
We see ourselves and others hy.
N.S.W.

gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage

a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
