Arguments pro and con an organised
Disaster Fund are almost equally strong. Un-
doubtedly a fixed fund would, in a measure, dry
up the springs of charity which flow now at the
call of any great disaster. On the other hand, it
would relieve distress in those cases where Death
kills in a retail fashion and attracts no wide-
spread sympathy for his victims. Probably, on
tne whole, it would be best to establish a fixed
fund, to supplement the State fund in cases of
mining-accidents, to give full relief in other cases.
Then, when a big disaster came, it could be used
as an occasion for appeals to replenish the fund.
That the State fund is not nearly sufficient to
relieve destitution by mining accidents can be
shown by a concrete instance : under its rules a
Kembla widow with four young children would
get just 18s. a week.
SHERLOCK HOLMES & CO.
PATSY: “Be cripes, Bull, keep yer eyes open, it’s
two hun'red shinin’ Jim apiece if we cops ’em!”
Two matters which help to bring about
the “ gratifying decrease in crime” of which so
much is heard—crime being measured wholly by
the number of convictions and the number of
persons in gaol—are the failure to capture the two
men who murdered policeman Guilfoyle, at
Redfem, Sydney, and the fact that the individual
who robbed and nearly killed Mandarin Quong
Tart in his own office at Sydney markets has got
away—or appears to have done so. It is no special
reflection on the police that these things are so.
A policeman is a person who is expected to have
enormous strength, considerable speed, unflinching
courage, the detective instinct highly developed,
intelligence, and the capacity to look after 39
miles of streets, alleys and lanes simultaneously,
all for a little over £2 a week. Also he is expected
to make up electoral rolls and do casual work in
between times; and if he is battered in “the
execution of his jooty” till he is a permanent
cripple he can’t even rely on a pension, because
the Police Fund is broke. The police created that
fund mostly by their own contributions, and
various N.S.W. Governments embezzled it by
paying out of it rewards for special services (i.e.
the Government would grant the reward and make
the police pay it). But i£ is a long bobby that has
no turning, and the Sydney force has turned on
Magistrate Isaacs. That dreary dweller on the
bench, when he heard of the outrage on Mandarin
Tart, got off some brilliant remarks about the un-
safe condition of Sydney streets, and the force
indignantly replied that the streets would be
much safer if Isaacs didn’t turn loose, with a 10s.
fine, offenders who had just fallen a little short of
murder, and thus teach them that they could
commit crime almost with impunity,
• • • •
N.S.W. Attorney-General has decided not
to put anyone on trial in connection with the
killing of Joseph Heaps at Ginninderra, where a
mixed party of police and civilians (every member
of which seemed to have had the jumps badly)
shot down-an inoffensive man at his camp-fire on
suspicion that he was a murderer. Possibly a jury
would not have convicted for manslaughter under
the circumstances, but the community’s interests
would have been served if, by a public trial, it was
impressed on people that it is not the correct
thing to take a pot-shot at any man whom a
bucolic policeman chooses to suspect of murder.
At present there is almost as much promiscuous
shooting in N.S.W. as in a Californian diggers’
camp, and criminals, even in a small retail line of
business, are encouraged to carry firearms, and to
use them in case of interference, by the fact that
the police are armed, and use their arms on the
slightest provocation. In every lawless transac-
tion nowadays, from petty larceny to passing bad
coin, the criminal has to reckon the possibility
of having to face firearms, so he carries his
“squirt,” and aspires always to get his shot in
first. A country with so small a population as
N.S.W. can’t really afford to arm its police with
revolvers.
• * * •
Theodore Fink has just got through Vic.
Assembly a Bill to give the reciprocal right of
admission to lawyers from other States to prac-
tise locally. The Bill is not so much intended to
open the way for a Federal legal system as to give
workless Vic. practitioners a chance to escape
from that land of legal drought. The times for
lawyers have never been so bad in other States as
in Vic. It was noticeable that Fink’s Bill was
supported by nearly every solicitor or amalgam in
the Assembly and opposed by all the barristers,
including Irvine. The reason is that it clears the
way for top-notch barristers like Symon, Reid,
and O’Connor to open up work in Melb. while the
Federal Parliament sits there. With solicitors in
Federal politics, of whom there are but one or
two, anyhow, the case is different. Their interests
in other capitals are in the form of permanent and
built-up businesses, and cannot be abandoned,
whereas the counsel just takes cases as they come,
and knows nob to-day what to-morrow will bring
forth.
Little Adelaide, which is remarkably rich
in philanthropic institutions for the benefit of
the blind and deaf and dumb, now begins the
surely risky experiment of teaching its blind girls
to cook. The idea started this way : A pair in
the industrial blind school married—against the
advice of the officials, who thought that at least
one in every partnership should see. To prove
how well they got on, the couple invited the presi-
dent to dinner, and he, who knew a good deal of
what the blind can do, was astonished at the wife’s
cooking and the husband’s carving. The manager
of this industrial school, by the way, who knows
the business part of Adelaide as few men do, is
himself stone-blind.
m • m *
In the August number of the Golden
Fleece (Sydney) “Helen Jerome” writes to ask
“ why should the franchise be thrust upon
an unwilling majority of women?” . . .
That should be easily answered. None
of the Bills that have been introduced to
give woman the vote for Parliament have
contained any compulsory-voting clauses,
and, therefore, there is no attempt to
thrust the franchise on any lady who
doesn’t want it. “The young wife and
mother, with a baby in her arms ” will
not be dragged by mounted police to
scramble “at the booth with a crowd of
wildeyed, unsexed, immodest harridans ”
—the Golden Fleece lady evidently has
a delusion that the rush at a polling booth
is like that at a drapery cheap sale —
which it isn’t. There is no rush, and
everything is calm, not to say peace-
fully indifferent. And if there is here and
there a hysterical lady who can’t go to
vote without carrying her baby head-
downwards by one leg while she
scratches John Norton’s name off the
ballot paper, that is no reason why a
calmer female should be deprived of the
chance to scratch John’s name off the
decisive slip. If only one woman in Aus-
tralia wished to scratch John Norton’s
name off the slip, and all the rest simply
detested the idea of scratching any name
at all off a slip, that woman has every
moral right to scratch.

Who died Jan. 19,1810. Aged 42.

Careless I am
What you can say of me.
What you can see amiss in me
Take care to shun ;
Look at home,
There is enough to be done !
Deceased was a woman. The inevitable “last
word.”

Melb. Women’s Hospital committee elec-
tion resulted in a rout of the old brigade, whose
nominees were one and all defeated. The reform
party wisely decided upon a “ticket,” and those
who desired to see the institution run on humane
lines sank all individual feelings and voted it.
The rejected ladies include Colonel Goldstein (for
long the stumbling-block of all who advocated
reform), Mrs. Shields (the hon. sec., whose hus-
band filled the place of the dismissed doctors pend-
ing the appointment of Dr. Martell), and Mrs. Ste-
phen (the mother-in-law of Chief Justice Madden).
Another thrown out was Mrs. David Syme. She
had taken no prominent par t in the recent squabbles,
but the new party adjudged her, rightly or wrongly,
to be responsible for the pro-cat proclivities of the
Age, so she went out too. The last ex-member
rejected was Lady Hutton, wife of the Federal
Commandant, who was appointed but a couple of
months back. Lady Hutton was entirely innocent
of the troubles of the institution, but she was
nominated by the cat-party shortly, after her
arrival, obviously for her social position, for she
could not possibly have had any knowledge of the
institution or its needs. The result of the election
is most gratifying. But the work is only half
done. The subscribers should remember to repeat
their tactics at next election, when the rest of the
committee come before them.

An elderly co-respondent stepped jauntily

dultry.” Petitioner was 64, and the giddy lady,

ments with the white-whiskered one, was but 10
years her husband’s junior. There seems to be a
boom in aged love-making of late. Only the other
day three people, whose united ages amounted to
over 180 years, got mixed up at Ballarat.

When the Melbourne municipali-
ties finally get possession of their tram-
way system —in about 14 years’ time—
that prospective revenue-producer will
probably turn out to be worth about
£lOO as scrap-iron and firewood. Already
there are several schemes being con-
sidered for electrical lines in the outer
suburbs as feeders to the railways, and
the motor-car promises to knock much
of the inner tram traffic on the head.
Lately, to show Essendon Council what
it could do, a motor, carrying nine pas-
sengers, ran from the city to Braybrook
and back in little over an hour, and,
despite the breakdown of one cylinder
on the way back, covered the miles
from Flemington Bridge to the G.P.O.
in 6min.—as against the tram’s 23min.
A motor co. promises a lOmin. service
of equal speed at Id. per passenger per
mile ; and as total cost of running such
a motor only amounts to 4 l-10d. per
mile (including wear and tear, wages,
repairs, management, and depreciation),
it looks as if the project would material-
ise. If co. wants a good advt. and the
blessings of the public, it might run a
fast and frequent service to Flemington
at Cup time (a 4d. fare should pay it),
and thereby knock a hole in the railway
profits during that momentous occasion,
and also tend to abolish the rascally
charge of 3s. and 2s. from city to course
and back.
* * * *
That recent Vic. case in which two
painters sued a former employer for
slander, claiming £99 damages, affords
further evidence of the handicap it is to
be a workman when you come before
some judges. Defendant publicly called
the men “thieves and rogues,” and in
the hearing of a constable; and then at
the trial—despite the overwhelming evi-
dence-denied using the words, but would
not go into the box. Judge Johnston
remarked sympathetically that “ defendant had
acted ‘foolishly,’ ‘immoderately’ and ‘intemper-
ately,’ ” but he was so manifestly guilty that a
verdict had to be recorded against him. S’onah,
however, concluded that the workmen’s claim for
£99 was “ monstrous,” and he awarded only £5.

“ Kay lee ” : Oh why, why did the
penny-’bus burst upon our peaceful, slow-going
Adelaide and make us realise the iniquity of the
3d. fare? I get into a tram and pay 3d. “ Only
Id. to ,” says the boy. Next day, again enter
the car and offer Id ” ; “3d. now, sir.” “ Why ? ”
“ The ’busses have stopped running.” Couple of
days later offer 3d. “Only Id., sir.” Next day
have occasion to go further than usual. “Penny
all the way, sir.” Later, again go to terminus
and show my Id., but after a time boy comes
round a second time and explains that “the
company has started Id. sections.” Fare to
terminus yesterday, Id; to-day, 3d. Just accord-
ing to how Reid’s ’busses run or don’t run, car
fares rise and fall like the share market.

Melbourne Age, reporting a case at Oak-
leigh in which the police objected to an old age
pension:—
Sergeant Ryan said that as a citizen, and entirely apart
from his status as a policeman, he was sick and tired of
the old age pensions business. He had to contribute his
share towards supporting the parents of well-to-do men
and women, and he was amazed thata woman who ought to
support her mother should remain away from Court and
fee a solicitor.
The old age pension system has been abso-
lutely undermined in Vic. by the amendment
of the Act providing that well-to-do relatives
must contribute to the allowances of pensioners.
It is no longer a meed of justice but a grudging
charity under police censorship.
N.S.W. old age pension boards disqualify
for pension women who are married to non-
naturalised men, but are otherwise eligible. The
administration of the Act seems to be directed
towards limiting the pensions as far as possible.

FOR SHAKESPERTAN SCHOLARS AND HAPPY
FATHERS ONLY.
“ Doomed for a certain term to walk the night.”
N.S.W. Crown Law officers were stuck up
lately. It all arose out of a sordid row in that back
part of Broken Hill where numerous empires
meet. The Syrian Ambassador, with the Chinese
Envoy’s assistance, sent a Note to the British
Representative asking him to pay his alleged
board-bill; and the Note, having been first dis-
respectfully used, was returned with some written
expressions of further contempt. What to charge
the filthy person with puzzled the police, who
finally abandoned their old friend “ riotous be-
haviour ” in favor of “sending an offensive packet
through the post.” When the man was committed
the Crown Law Office began to oscillate, and
finally it decided that the “exhibit” constituted
part of a criminal libel. The jury found him
guilty of that, but “ respectfully suggested ” that
it would be a very dirty piece of paper that would
show up conspicuously against the morals of the
neighborhood ; and his Honor agreed, and simply
ordered the libeller to pay a small fine.
* • •
Four men recently quarantined because of
an outbreak of smallpox on board the boat that
landed them in Sydney arose one morning, walked
o J the premises, took the boat from Manly, and
passed a few hours enjoying themselves in the
city. They would have stayed longer but for a
poiiceman. What is the use of quarantine unless
the authorities display better vigilance? And
what are penalties for breaking quarantine legal-
ised for, if breakers, as has often previously been
the case, are treated as if they played a mere
harmless joke ?
* * * *
The curious circumstance is noted by
writers on mental science that the “official insane
population” does not increase during war-time.
There was an actual decrease in the United King-
dom in 1900 and 1901 ; ditto in some at least of
the Australias. Same thing was noted dur-
ing the U.S. war of 1861-65, and in France
in 1870-71. The officials’ explanation is
that war gives the people something to
think about outside themselves. It probably
hasn’t occurred to them that at such times
scores of people, who at other times would be put
under lock and key, are permitted to roam free

BUCHANAN WHISKY.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 26, 1902.
My Dear Arini.—
An English lady newly arrived in N.S.W. was
amongst the guests at the opening of the tram-line
to Lilyville, Minister Crick’s new model suburb,
and was much amused at the contrast between the
general free-and-easiness and the formal after-
dinner toasts and speeches. Luncheon in a
primitive bower was served in bush-hut style-
one knife between three guests, and thick cups for
drinking, &c. Yet the speechifying could not
have been of a more pretentious cumbersomeness
had the British Premier and all the European
sovereigns been present in force. Then the
eternal allusions to England and the Royal Family
struck English ears very curiously. “ You seem
unable to keep England out of your mouths,” said
the visitor; “we at home know nothing of Aus-
tralia and care less, but here your talk is more of
things British than of Australia. Never in all my
life in London did I hear so much of the Royal
Family as I have done since coming to Sydney.
Hardly an Englishman or an Englishwoman knows
the names of others than the King or the Prince
of Wales, but here* I am continually being pelted
with little slabs of information about Princess this
or Princess that. Curious, too, how you can’t
open a small plate of oysters without drinking the
health of the King, or close the same without the
Anthem. lam quite beginning to know the
Anthem since spending a month in Australia.”.
Coronation dinners at the rate of two a week
still go on (the table) at Sydney State Govt.
House. The Rawsons must surely be near the end
of the list of people whom duty to the Empiah
compels them to feed.
Public appearances of their Exes, last week
were all in aid of the Kembla Disaster Fund. One
musical enterprise which shared its receipts with
the Antilles Disaster Fund brought, in conse-
quence, almost the whole French colony to the
Town Hall—some of the women, dark and bright,
and sweet-looking, as if they had been fed on
chocolates and coffee. Cleveland-st. Boys’ Choir
nearly filled the programme, and quite filled the
hall with its wonderful volume of small boys’
voices. There were several hundred of the
oreatures that Carlyle says ought to be brought up
underground, but looking unutterably good and
well behaved. They surely never greased a door-
step or tied a can to a dog’s tail, and they make a
choir on whose account any conductor might sin
the sin of the angels. It is time Australian star
female voices were balanced by Australian male
ditto.
The youngster who was soloist in the “Spin-
ning Chorus” of the “Flying Dutchman,” is a
most harmonious knickerbocker, and promises
.melodiously. Signor Rossi drove a flock of man-
dolin maidens on to the stage, then master and
maids formed a group—a real photographic bonne
faces, pretty wrists, nice hair at
the bread-and-butter stage of dressing, lots of rib-
bon on the prows of the mandolins, which twinky-
twanked joyfully a “ Trovatore” selection.
Amy Castles, at Sydney Town Hall, had an
audience of over 4000 persons, and realised close
upon £3BO for the Disaster Fynd—after the exes
about £75, were deducted. The Cranbrook couple
escorted by Mayor Hughes and Mr. J. C. Wif-
liamson, were backed by the Huttons, Finns
divers consuls and their wives, and all Sydney
society. The soprano was in fine voice and a
lovely not-very-blue-blue gown ; and the clamour
of her welcome might be heard two streets away
Her singing of “Parigi O Cara ” with tenor New-
bury caused more applausive noise than all the
rest of the programme, unless, perhaps, the
tenor’s rendering of “Annie Laurie.” Breathes
there an audience with soul so dead which
wouldn’t respond with all its heart and hand and
feet to Newbury’s singing of that sweet song ?
. General Hutton gave the audience a treat dur-
ing the interval, by facing it while he talked to
Lady Rawson. (The poor General has had to
face the music so much lately that to face some-
thing else was probably a welcome change ) He
was not in uniform, but wore orders, and the star
on his breast was a treat. It blazed as Venus
does in the sky these nights. Silver and
tin and things make a wonderful shine when the
prisms catch the light. Lady Hutton, with the
Swedish eyes, fair complexion, and golden head
has such a tranquil expression that looking long
at her acts like a mild soporific. Most military
women with good-looking husbands have an
expression that speaks of a peck of troubles. But
after all, the General isn’t the Adonis that his
portraits make him.
“ Le shakehand ” among Parisians of good form
is to be superseded by the hand kiss. The French
gentleman no longer shakes a lady’s hand ; he
bows over it and kisses it. The wrist is the
kissing terminus (in public). None but a greatly
favored friend can venture on the wrist kiss (again
tn public). “As the friendship ripens the kiss
graduafly steals from the finger tips along the back
of the hand. Well, now, here’s our French Vice-
Consul s chance to introduceabeautifulcustom into
the jungle of Sydney society. If he could be
induced to give a platform demonstration I am
sure he would be overwhelmed by an ocean of
beautiful hands offered, “for this occasion only ”
on the altar of a more artistic style of life. ‘lt
takes a man with a Narcissus face, and a patent
silver hinge to his back, to bend gracefully to the
hand-kiss. All the men in the Count’s audience
no matter how squabby, Esau-like, and blanket-
clothed, would say, “ Yes, a man looks well doing
tnat; 111 start in on my own account.” But stay 1
Perhaps better not Begin at the finger-tips and
steal along the back of the hand ? A man, finding
himself on so pleasant a road, mightn’t be willing
to stop at the wrist. °

uniform, whether naval or military, no matter,
and the glimmer of a white frock. Each was
seated by the other Thou, and didn’t seem
to yearn for the Jug, the Loaf, or the Book.
l°ve you, darling!” (he was overheard),
do J° ve you so ! ” and there were dual sounds,

than I love you, dearest! ” she responded ; “ but
1 do hope your wife won’t come along.”

—Cards are out for a garden-party at State Govt.

. Miss Grace Townsend, the only woman’s name
m along list of well-plucked ones, won her bronze
medal and certificate (awarded at last Monday’s
Jubilee meeting of the Humane Society of N. S. W.)
for diving into a waterhole near Parkes and fetch-
ing from the bottom an 11-year-old girl, who was
drowning.
Mr. Norman Giblin, erstwhile manager of the
Bank of New Zealand in Sydney, who died
suddenly at Chatswood the other day, came of the
well-known Tas. family of that name, and had, of
late years, been an official assignee of N.S.W.
Bankruptcy Court. As his friends knew, the
latter position hardly suited him ; for his tempera-
ment was one best fitted to look on the kindly
side of things. He died very suddenly, of pa-
ralysis of the brain, and his remains were followed
to the grave by a host of genuine friends.
• a hysterical young female drowned herself
in Sydney Harbor, to fulfil the prophecy of a
fortune-teller, there has been a great outcry
against such humbugs, and a consequent great
increase in their trade. Anyone going to a
fortune-teller now is promised long life, “ freedom
from haccidents, hespecially by water,” and above
all, “ you’ll never die by yer hown ’and. ” All the
fortune-tellers whom I have interviewed, in order
to find the whereabouts of my stolen Gordon
setter, used their “ h’s ” despitefully. Not one of
them can come up to the fortune-teller that Saul
consulted at Endor, though one or two, from their
appearance, See., might be suspected of a “familiar
spirit ” —in one case it was palpably rum. By the
way, the Early. Closing Act doesn’t apply to the
sale of prophecies by witches and wizards; if it
does it is not enforced.
Mr. Goring Thomas will give a concert in Sydney
Y.M.C.A. Hall on Wednesday, September 3rd.
Several things of the musician-composer’s own
brewing may be sampled by the audience and a
girl violiniste from S’Africa will bow on the cat-
strings. . She . studied in Berlin under our own
Kruse, it is said, with gratifying success.
My Brisbane correspondent writes:—
“ The General Hospital is out of favor with the
people who wish to be considered ‘Society.’
Among other things urged against it, they say
that ‘no women will go there.’ Lady Lamington,
some while ago, founded a hospital for women
with £lo—the cheapest thing on record. This
hospital is a small house, quite unfit for the pur-
pose, and badly situated. At last annual meeting
the report stated ‘ seven women had died during
the year because they had no room for them.’
And the committee, though in funds, stated their
determination not to build at present.
“ Yet, it is an undisputed fact that there are
two large, well-ventilated women-wards in the
general hospital—one for the special diseases of
women, the very thing the Lamington Hospital
has been founded for. Why is that ward un-
occupied ? What is the committee of the hospital
about? What is the Inspector of Charities doing?
A ball in aid of the hospital’s funds recently was
a failure, and we are now going to have a display
of fireworks for the hospital. If it could but
throw some light on what has become a grave
scandal, it would indeed be a boon, both to the
general hospital and the Brisbane public.

trar-General Blakeney,
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“There was an unusual flutter at the Dante
evening last Tuesday night, when several meri-
torious officials and random booky Bohemians
were drummed up to meet the Gov. Clarkes.
Max tsimon the hon. sec., with bumps of energy
forming undulations all over his bald head—rallied
the worshippers of Dante together. Even R S
bmythe, the untiring exploiter of talent, was there
with some Cecilias under his wing. The Clarkes
pere, itihre, et fille, smiled and smiled, and approved
every number, and commissioned Aide Browning
to clap for them, and beamed on the Max men-
tioned, and thanked him and the grey Italian
Oonsul for a pleasant evening—an instructive
evening, a highly interesting evening—and bowed
themselves from the company in the naicest
manner in the world. And the Dante Society, in
worshipping his condescending Excellency, forgot
to adore Dan te.
“The Naicest assembled in force at the first
Spring shivoo at the Sargoods’ last Thursday.
Music was provided and silence enjoined, and
wheneer a warbler took the floor, a death-like
stillness reigned. The hostess, clad in win .'-red,
with chenille full stops rounding off passages of
lace, stood at the ball-room entrance, as business-
like as a janitress, with a practical steel bag
depending from her belt. Keen eyes and spec-
tacles further denoted the absence of all Sassiety
airs and fluffiness. The daughter of the house
helped her cream turn-out to a garnishing of sky-
blue, and, rather prettily, echoed the remarks of
her shrewd and amiable parent. The Waxellency’s
two daughters stood afar off from the multitude,
and gave their shell-like ears to the sweet piping
of Walter Kirby and Cesca Vollugi. . Mrs.
Urquhart was the most-diamonded dame in the
gathering. The most dressy vision was a Sydneyite
(known to very few) whose bodice of a dark blue
silk ground bore rows and rows of flannel-flower
petals. The sleeves provided groundwork for
more petals, which curled up at the point quite
naturally and allowed glimpses of the under-silki-
ness. Senator Drake’s better half sailed about
the ball-room shedding her conversational pearls
discriminatingly on the (groups of the Naicest, &
la grande dame. Mrs. I. Isaacs made a departure
in thatch-building. Her style was Nell Gwynne’s;
a rope plait circled her head, leaving a forehead
margin for a clump of soft Willy-nilly tendrils.
“ A, to me, regrettable thing about the Women’s
Hospital Catmittee elections was the vetoing of
Lady Hutton as a member. In the whole of the
State, there isn’t a pleasanter, more amiable, or
more harmless personage than the boss fighting
man’s wife. No ‘side,’ no aggressiveness about
that very charming person. Besides she is bright,
sunny-haired, and highly ornamental. Alas, she
had to suffer for the sins of her sponsors. Col.
Goldstein—that well-meaning tom catmitteeite—
won’t, however, be missed. His intentions were
good, bub ——. The time he saves from the
weekly hospital meetings he can more profitably
spend cultivating his prize dahlias at the Leon-
gatha Labor Colony. By the way, the Colonel is
the parent of suffragist Vida Goldstein, whose
views he opposes strenuously. There was an
occasion wien father and daughter headed rival
crusades, and laughingly, and in the best of humor
with each other, fought their rival causes.
“Miss Storey, of Melb., who wants to estab-
lish a school of domestic economy for the purpose
of giving young girls the instruction necessary to
make them good wives, is getting herself recog-
nised. Minister Reid favors the idea, and has
promised to ‘look into it.’ If the school will
provide the other things necessary to the making
of good wives—the husbands —it will be a tre-
mendous success, but that important point must
not be overlooked. The institution will give cer-
tificates to trained girls, and it is taken for granted
that bachelors wanting wives will ask for
those with Miss Storey’s trade-mark, and will see
that they get them. If men married women
because they thought they would make good
wives the theory would be all right, but they don’t;
in nine cases out of 10 they marry and chance it.
And women are not particularly anxious to make
good wives; they prefer devoting their energies to
the task of making good husbands.
“At Prahran Town-hall, the place where the
Amateur Runaway Girls frivolled their brief three
nights upon the stage, a concert will be given next
Wednesday (3rd) in aid of a Church of England
choristers’ fund, or something in that semi-reli-
gious . way. The Caledonian choir is to sing;
likewise several soloists whose names have a
tartan and haggis sound, and a novelty is
threatened in the form of a junior quartet,
coached by Miss Gladys Ure. The task of creat-
) n £> a junior quartet party has never commended
itself to the men who know most about chorister
boys, but the fair Gladys reckons that a girl-
teacher is quite another matter. It is all done by
patience and—smiles. You put your four future
men in a row and make slaves of them.
“The ‘King Charles spaniel’craze is catching
on m this capital. Modish women, who think
the begetting of children a barbarous relic of
savage ages, are cutting fine figures, at the present
hour, coddling canine brutes for the edification of
their friends. Formerly, a hostess posed her
fairest babe afc her confidential ‘ at-homes. * Now-
adays, the smart women cushion their spaniels,
and give them biscuits to chew, while their friends
admire mistress and poodle. Quite a score of
fashionable women in Melbourne lavish excessive
care on the spaniels that they have introduced
into their doggeries since the Nell Gwynne boom
—King Charles spaniels if they can get them
monstrous little wretches who, by in-breeding’
have lost most of their noses, and whose tears
consequently corrode their cheeks, deprived of
the proper outflow—a good symbol, maybe, of
perverted femininism.
“ The honeymoon used to be a matter of spoon-
ing—and dolce far niente : nowadays it must have
some more solid amusement. Last week a newly-
wed couple (very smart and very popular) bore off
their golfing kits to Sydney with the intention of
playing out the honeymoon at the Bondi links. In
other ways honeymooners change. A wedding
nowadays is more of a practical business ceremony
and less of a long-drawn-out gush,(at which brides
tremble and mothers weep. Recentlv a prosaic
bridal pair, finding a half-hour to spare between
the tying and the train, took a promenade down
Collms-street, and bought some boxes of sweets
by way of killing time. There are no Paolos and
i rancescas among our modern lovers. The
hymeneal sacrifice is becoming as brief and as
business-like as the operations of a Chicago
canning factory.
“ Miss . ‘ Stonington ’ Clarke caused a stir in
musical circles when she elected to sing at a bene-
fiting tenor’s concert. But when the time came
for the programme-printing, the Guv’nor’s daugh-
ter regretfully retracted her promise, on the
ground that she was under Marchesi’s orders not
to warble—that is, publicly—till her vocal educa-
tion is completed. The Weidermann element pre-
vails at the Malvern mansion at present.
“A gifted Orpheus, warbling before a petticoat
society the other day, was so carried away by the
success of a new song he was rendering, and the
wild enthusiasm of the audience, that he skipped
over to the lady who accompanied and planted a
chaste kiss on each cheek, to her horror and the
mingled surprise and. enjoyment of the women
looking on. The piamste wasn’t in any way
‘willin’,’ and, ’tis said, administered a severe
rebuke to the emotional warbler. He pleaded
‘ temperament ’ as his excuse.
“ Madame Yeamans-Titus was born casually at
sea between Sydney and Melbourne. One of the
most brilliant girl violinistes in the Marshall-Hall
Conservatorium tells a startling story of her birth
at sea. It happened prematurely, on a raft after
a shipwreck.. The mother and the newly-arrived
salt-washed infant were picked up by a Norwegian
barque, and coaxed on to health and strength by
a course of very rough fare. The promising
young musician revels in a fine complexion and
abounding avoirdupois ; so that salt-water ohrist-
ening and rough baby diet had no bad results.
“ A son of Mars recently returned from Africa
with a bit of extra gold-braid to his honor, and no
bones broken. He had been in three hot engage-
ments and came back safe and sound. By way of
celebrating his immunity from breaks and bruises,
he gave his Melb. men-friends a very fine dinner
at a swagger hotel. The spirits of the guests rose
with each course. In the end there was some
horse play, some table-lifting, glass-breaking, and
a sort of Follow-the-Man-from-Cook’s steeplechase
which resulted disastrously for the host. He
caught his leg a blow from one of the hurdles, and
was laid low with a broken limb. And the hero
is at last in splints.
“ The fat funny man of a certain show expresses
his astonishment sub rosa, at the tolerance of Aus-
tralian audiences. Nowhere else within the
range of J. Bull’s Bible, says he, is such latitude
allowed mummers. The by-play among them
during the progress of a show, and the wireless
telegraphy between the chorus-girls and the front-
row Johnnies make this fat wag scant of breath
with astonishment. He thinks it shocking, and
will have none of it when he is under way with
his drolleries. But the girls wink the other eye,
give him impudence, and turn their smiles on
some other fellow who wants them. Too much
latitude on the boards certainly is permitted in
this _ particular co. Much energy goes out in
specific smiles and signals to the spacious shirt-
fronts in the stalls. To that part of tne house men
should be charged double rates of admission.
“ Dress-making will shortly be the simplest and
most expeditious thing in the world. Spring
novelties include extensive dress decorations,
whole panels of applique, lengths of tucking,
bodice strips and whole costume designs in lace
and chiffon, so that the parts have simply to be
joined, and you have the stunning altogether.
Veils, by latest advices, are to reach the tip
of the nose only. This is a cruel freak for the
not-so-young. A woman ages most quickly about
the neck and lower part of the face, and the long
enveloping veil had its excellent uses. But now
only the youthful and fresh-complexioned can
score with the brief veil and its very fine meshing.
And those who resort to the kindly chemist for
the bloom of youth will bo still worse off, for
whilst the glow of pinked cheeks may seem natural
under a veil, how are the chin and neck to hide
the etching of Time’s graving chisel ?
Overheard in a Victorian railway car. Bell-
topper to Frockcoat: ‘ I really think we should
have had a holiday for the King’s coronation. It
looks so disloyal.’ Frockcoat: ‘I quite agree
with you. Do you know, there is a great deal of
disloyalty in the Government. There’s Jack
Murray, he’s a pro-Boer ! ’ Belltopper: ‘ Oh,
yes, we should have had a holiday. It’s a dis-
grace. Look what a loss it would have been to
Victoria if the King had died 1 All the summer
goods had been ordered, and do you know there
were only three black hats in all our warehouse.
It would have been a loss if he had died ! ’
“ One of the prettiest chorus charmers at Her
Majesty’s is the sister of a brainy young member
of the State Parliament, and she is the most
circumspect siren on the boards.
“The Tasmanians are much amusecf at their
taut little Governor. He has a mincing gait and
a mincing manner, and dons and doffs his monocle
at regular intervals. He would make, think they,
a capital Gilbertian figure to pace a vessel’s deck
and indulge in quizzical capers; or their fancy
pictures him as a nice little gaitered-and-shovel-
hatted bishop. On Coronation Day he saw the
parade through the driving sleet, and valiantly
endangered his rheumatic person by standing some
hours of chilling downpour. Therefore the
islanders think Gov. Havelock a game little party.
_“ A recent mail brought Melb. friends news of
Lionel Lindsay and his labors in Spain, and of his
arrival with his pile of canvasses in the Big Smoke.
A male correspondent tells of having been
thunderstruck at the luxuriant and tropical look of
L.L., and avers he is brimming over with life and
enthusiasm and capacity for work, and satisfac-

folio of drawings illustrative of Merrimee’s

“The economic spirit lays hold of the Mel-
bourne hostess, and her At-Homing takes the
meagre form of afternoon teas. There has been
scarcely any evening illumination for At Home
purposes. Society dames find themselves better
off as a result. But the opportunities for securing
‘ mixed-doubles ’ have been rare. How can busi-
ness in the marriage-market be brisk if there is
no forward movement on the part of the little
pets to be put under the hammer, or if the
mamma of the mart does nothing to attract
bidders to the goods offering ? If society girls are
to be married off with any celerity, their mothers
must turn on dinner and supper parties and dis-
creetly shaded electric-light. Afternoon teas don’t
promote love-making ; only scandal.
Yours affectionately,

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Stereotyped in all daily-newspaper offices :
“To-day Mr. Barton lunched with ”
“Vic.” We have nearly reached the time
when Treasurer Turner said he would retire. Let
us hope he won’t.
The President of Ballarat School of Mines
lately stated that 19 ex-pupils of that establish-
ment are earning £17,000 a year, amongst them.
Gr. Bron, the quaint and imaginative
draftsman now known to Bulletin readers,
was for years frontispiece artist of the War Cry.
Sydney Evening News is so industriously
circulating various disturbing political rumors of
late that it looks as though Mr. .Tim Hogue were
quite hungry for office again.
Sydney D.T., in a spasm of repentant
honesty, describes B. R. Wise in its leading
columns as “the best and ablest friend trades-
unionists have in Australian public life."
The Earl of Dudley, just appointed Lord-
Lieutenant of Ireland, will be chiefly remembered
in Sydney as the handsome young fellow who
attended Randwick races attired in a white linen
suit and a black belltopper.
What a gay old sea-dog air there is about
the very signature of N.S. W. Governor ! To see on
an Act of Parliament “ Harry H. Rawson,” in-
stead of the formal “Henry,” gives a “merrily-
my-hearties ” air to the whole document.
Not generally known that Clovelly House
(now uninhabitable), at Watson's Bay, Sydney,
the former residence of N.S.W.’s beloved old
Jaolr Robertson, was built by Henry Watson
Parker, when N.S.W. Premier, in 1856-7.
Enigmatic remark by Major - General
Hutton at a recent soldiers’ meeting :—
I feel that my time (for retirement) also will arrive
before many years are over, and that should be some
little consolation to you all. from the top to the bottom
of the military service.
One of the letters which Australia’s dear
old friend, Elijah Dowie, the Man of the Purple
Wings, now of Zion City, Chicago, prints in his
“Zion ” paper:—
I sent a request for prayer to you a short time ago,
asking you to pray for my fowls, which were dying off
with some kind of sickness.
I thank God that He has healed them. Not one has
died since I sent the request.
How good it is to have a loving Heavenly Father to go
to in our troubles, knowing that He will never forsake us
if we do His will and live close to Him.
I thank you for your prayers in our behalf
I pray God to spare your
life for many years to
carry on your Divine
mission as Elijah the
Restorer.
Elijah, the Eowl
Restorer!
OUTSIDE THE SUNDAY SCHOOL.
BOY: “Give something towards the Foreign Mis-
sions, sir? ”
THE OLD 'UN : "Ah, my boy, when it’s for a
Mission I always put my hand in my pocket ” —
BOY: “Thanks, sir.”
THE OLD 'UN : “But if it’s for a Foreign one —
I keep it there.”
Dr. Mercer, Tasmania’s new C.E. bishop,
is about due from England. Dr. Nixon, the first
bishop of the State, stayed a few years there and
then returned to England ; and Dr. Montgomery,
its last bishop, also left Tas. after a while’s ex-
perience.
Minister Bent sustained himself through-
out the recent trying time with chocolates, which
he carried in his tail pocket, and from which he
drew sustenance every few moments. Tommy
wolfs lollies wholesale, and is just now Mel-
bourne’s conspicuous apostle of sweetness.
“Dagonet,” in London Referee, on the
over-feeding of Australasian Premiers in England :
It is understood that directly they get on board ship to
return home the victims of Imperial unity will be put on
milk diet, and sent to bed every evening at nine, by order
of the Board of Health and the Society for the Prevention
of Cruelty to Colonial Premiers.
The reappearance of Bent in Vic. politics
recalls that there was once a “ Thomas Bent Land
Co.” in Victoria. It is dead now and it isn’t worth
while, speaking disrespectfully of the corpse.
Years have alleviated the fragrance of many a cat
of those old days, for Time is a great deodorant—
especially a deodorant of cats.
President Lackey, of N.S.W. Leg. Council,
goes for a six months’ rest. Seventy-two is not a
great age in polities, but he entered the lists early
and stands as one of the few survivors of those
who worked with and against him in his prime.
J. S. Earnell, Byrnes, Robertson, Parkes, Dailey,
Stuart dropped off years ago. Lackey, in his
young days, succeeded Hay as M.L.A. for Central
Cumberland, and in his old age succeeded him as
President of the Leg. Council,
Melb. Argus was shocked at Judge Hamil-
ton showing public sympathy with the civil-
servant victims of retrenchment, just as it was
shocked when Higinbotham sympathised with
the maritime strikers. But it reported, with sleek
approval, Judge Hodges’ Mafficking speech and
Judge Madden’s Jingp joy at the Stock Exchange
pow-wow. A J udge, in the opinion of the Argus,
must never climb down from the Bench to the
platform, unless to barrack for Argus views.
Fleming, the Melbourne “agitator,” has,
for the best part of his life, been, firstly, the
spokesman of the local unemployed; and,
secondly, a working bootmaker. Trenwith, on
the other hand, was, firstly, a bootmaker ; and
secondly, a labor-leader. Now he is mainly a poli-
tician—the member for Trenny.
“Melb.”: Bent’s full-length portrait in
his robes as Speaker, at the Aquarium, is sumptu-
ous ! A similar portrait, at the University, of
Redmond Barry in his robes as Chancellor, is a
fool to it. The Bent portrait was first shown on
a Collins St., Block staircase. People said it was
a scandal. “ Never mind,” said L. L. Smith, who
bosses the Aquarium, “Tommy ’ll come round
again. ”
Old Arthur Quintal, who died the other
day, was the patriarch of the Pitcairners. When,
a few years ago, a party of would-be scientists
built up a theory of degeneracy on the Pitcairners’
Sosed lapse into gibberish, old Arthur waxed
tily indignant, and maintained that the so-
gibberish was really Polynesian lingo, which
his critics didn’t understand. He was a perfervid
royalist.
Mr. Henniker Heaton has had a marriage
in his family, one of his sons marrying a lady
whose father is an “ honorable ” and whose grand-
father is a small Welsh lord. Among those cap-
tured to attend the wedding were: item, one
prince and princess (small German goods, but still
of genuine princely rank); item, three ambassa-
dors (from very small to medium) ; item, one lord ;
and in addition quite a number of “sirs.”
The British public is now crowning with
flowers De Wet, who, a few months ago, was a
dirty, treacherous murderer, so perhaps old-man
Kruger may yet have his character rehabilitated
in the eyes of London. At any rate, the yarn that
he took millions of money away fi-om S’Africa is
now contradicted by London D.T., which gives
print to an assertion that Kruger lent all his avail-
able private funds (£40,000), without interest, to
his country to help in the fight.
Paris Nesbit is again enlivening Adelaide
with a weekly paper, edited by himself. Last
time it was Morning, price one penny. This
time, it is the Morning Star, circulated gratuit-
ously ; “and it is worth it, too,” as Mark Twain
said. However, as a success of scandal, it would
be worth many pence, the attack on Chief-Justice
Way being in particular a bare-knuckles affair,
that nobody will countenance but everybody
wants to read. There is also much erotic verse
addressed ‘ ‘ To Paris ” by anonymous poetesses.
Contrariwise, S. Australia, the dusty wheat
State, where, for the life of them, they can’t keep
a mine outside Moonta going profitably, supplies
Vic. with a director for one of its bigger schools of
mines, at Stawell. Adelaide University issues a
diploma in mining ; and young Whitington, a
Science graduate, who now goes over the border,
was the first to win it. Failing to get any other
sort of billet after he had won the diploma, he
went underground, at Broken Hill, and got prac-
tical experience, which has lifted him out of the
ruck of school-trained men.
Henry Lucy tells S.M. Herald a yarn
about Dick Seddon. Dick, when a mere M.P.,
was invited by a one-time Governor of Maoriland
to “dine and sleep at Government House.”
Dined and slept well, but did not put in an
appearance at breakfast. He had left, and when
seen afterwards, said that he had not been invited
to breakfast, and so did not wait for the meal.
Same yarn been told of dozens of others—of
Joseph Arch, M.P., when asked to the Prince of
Wales’ house, for instance, and of Elijah when he
stopped for the night with Ahab.
The firm of W. M‘Arthur and Co., Sydney,
since its start, has been always addicted to poli-
tics. Alex. M‘Arthur, of the pre-Responsible
Government times, was a politician of note, and
became afterwards a member of the House of
Commons. His son, W. A., also took to politics,
and attained office in the Treasury; and W.
M‘Millan, now in the business, is the real
leader of the Calico Jimmies of the Common-
wealth (G. H. Reid is only chief comedian). The
politics of the M‘Arthur clan are always directed
to the fostering of their own commercial under-
takings.
Acting-Premier Ward’s chief, in fact, only
difficulty in leading Maoriland Parliament is in
the weakness of his colleagues. Outside himself,
there is not an average debater in the Cabinet,
with the exception of Carroll (Timi Kara), and he
is too indolent to speak often. In the lobby,
however, Timi is a host in himself, and he is
always told off to palaver any member who
threatens to “ kick over the traces.” Seldom,
indeed, does his saponaceous tongue fail him, and
with the Maori representatives (whose votes have
served the Ministry before to-day) he is an unfail-
ing peacemaker.
Amongst reliable traditions of early days
in Australia is the giant race, the sons of Anak,
who grew by scores about the old settlement.
Here is a remarkable instance indicating unrecog-
nised influences. Captain Browne (father of “Rolf
Boldrewood ”), one of the settlers of the thirties,
lived on his stations one decade and travelled the
next. He had eight children born of the one
mother, and those begotten in Australia of both
sexes were gigantic, while the boys and girls, who
first saw light in other lands were scarcely up to
the average. Any other cases of this sort would
be worth recording.
Sydney D.T.’s London
special re the Canadian Pre-
mier :
Sir Wilfred Laurier is a striking
personality in appearance, and still
more striking as a speaker. He
has a quaintly pleasing deliver}', in
which the traces of his French-
Canadian origin are charmingly
blended with the occasional pictur-
esque Americanisms, both of idiom
and utterance, that make the effort
of listening to his speeches one of
delight. The matter of his speeches
is as good as the manner. You
have no public speaker in Australia
who could very well be compared
with him, because his style is alto-
gether his own. But it is a, most
charming style.

his Melbourne flock t’other
Coronation-day on the fact that
“the Hanoverian dynasty had
been proclaimed first in public
by a Nonconformist minister in
a Nonconformist chapel.” Dr.
Bevan—bless him ! —is proud of
the brutal, ignorant, licentious
German of 1714. The white-
fleeced shepherd should be set
to entertain his brethren and
sistern with readings from
Thackeray’s “Four Georges.”

When lately J. H. Gordon,
S.A.’s Education Minister,
severely lectured country liter-
aryinstitutes upon the degraded
tastes which they for the most
part cultivate, one of those
nuisances with a long memory
for trifles dug up something.
It was a contribution to a long-
since - dead and never-resur-
rected country print, whose
authorship was traced to “a
forward youth named Gordon.”
In the course of some wildly-
mixed metaphors this critic
called on the local institute to
“eradicate this taste for vile
and nauseous intellectual pabu-
lum ” —in short, to Put Down
the Novel. And then some
Opposition leader of the future

then Master Gordon was intended for another

Preacher Torrey, in one of his Sydney
addresses, declared that almost every anarchist
and every scoundrel was an infidel. It is com-
forting, anyhow, to know that Caesar Borgia was
an ex-cleric, and that his father, Alexander, was in
the same line of business as Torrey, while Louis
XI. hung on solidly to his faith and prayed hard.
Also, nobody ever cast any reflections on the piety
of Mary, who used to incinerate bishops at Smith-
field ; or her husband, Philip; or the Inquisitors;
or Cromwell, who massacred in a general way
around Ireland ; or the Scotch Presbyterians
when they got the upper hand and killed off their
enemies in the name of the Lord ; or the generally
bloodthirsty Israelites ; and even the devil has
never been justly accused of being an infidel,
whatever else may have been said against him.
Bent, by one who knows Thomas well
Bent, who so nearly bullied Vic. railway system out of
gear, is one of the survivals of the burly land-boomers.
A product of abundance of fertiliser and the sandy scrubs
about. Brighton he blustered his way into politics 30 years
ago, and filled out offensively in all ways as his prosperity
increased. The 1871 election, when the market gardener
beat the Liberal statesman Higinbotham, is one of the
puzzles of Vic. history. Explanation would be found if
the records of the self-styled Constitutional Party were
published. Bent served their turn, and, though repug-
nant to them in every sense, established claims which had
to be recognised. S rvice had tojfind him a job at the
tail-end of his first Ministry, and he was admitted without
protest into the O’Loghlen Government. But whenever
Democracy,' strongly led, held the reins, Thomas the
Bully was outside. Graham Berry would never touch
him, though in the big fight of the ’7o’s he hung on
to the skirts of the Liberal party, and his last Ministerial
office was 19 years ago, until Irvine dug him up
again the other day. The Speakership capped the
mystery, for why Thomas should be put in the
place of the first Commoner of the Sta: e no man outside
the secret caucus could discover. If traditions have any
weight he was all that a Speaker should not be. Asser-
tive, ignorant, rude, tactless —and yet he came within
one vote of the one-summer millionaire, Davies,
and subsequently put down J. G. Duffy and
Wrixon. Thomas, in all his instincts and habits,
is Bounderby over again—Bounderby incarnate,
and unless Democracy in Victoria is hobbled very
short, he is in the way now of a Bounderby fall. And yet
Brighton may continue faithful, for Brighton, like Bent,
has risen from the Cabbage Garden stage to what’s called
Respectability. It is the home of the small suburban
villa, and the small suburban mind, whose idol is cheap-
ness at any cost. And Thomas has been a cheap man all
his days, dead set against national policy and native
industry, and ready always to bring down wages and to
boom up land.
JOHN FORREST.
S. Australia lately lost by retirement the
matron of its one lunatic asylum after an un-
broken service of 37 years. The lady, Miss Lucy,
had a remarkable influence over her patients,
mainly, no doubt, because of her untiring efforts
to turn the institution into a cheerful, artistic
home, and keep it like that. S. Aus. has an
average record of “recoveries” of 55 - 8 per cent,
of male and 59'6 per cent, of female patients.
There died recently in Armidale (N.S.W.)
Hospital a man whose name was widely known in
the Australian backblocks. From him—John
Kinsella—came the idea known to every man who
has ever used hand-shears as “ Putting Kinchler
on ’em.” For the information of non-sheepish
people who may be interested, the simple opera-
tion is performed by pulling back the blades of
the shears from the point where they join the
handles, thus making them take more wool—tech-
nical term, “a bigger blow.” The pulling back
of the blades throws the points apart, and the
“knockers ” have to be filed-dowp correspondingly
to allow them to close.
Pretty plain by now that there’s more
than the King’s health behind the notable unrest
at Windsor. Some considerable part of the old
Edward survives in the divinely-appointed; the
old Edward was mostly old Adam, without the
chill off. Lord Salisbury’s refusal to “ennoble”
the King’s private financier, Sir Edward Cassel,
has made no end of trouble, and it is alleged that
the King is on exceedingly ill terms with the
Duchess of Buccleuch, a stickler for form and
Mistress of the Robes to Queen Alexandra.
Edward was to dine with the Duchess, and stipu-
lated that a lady friend of his should be of the
party. The Duchess kicked, and the King caved
in with a bad grace.
Mr. G. A. Murray, who died at Manly
last week, had been lost to the public eye since
the burst-up of the P. N. Russell ironworks through
a strike 30 years ago. Mr. Murray, partner with
Mr. Russell, was a gentleman who “intended to
manage his own affairs, ” and did not admit that
the workers should interfere with his property.
No conference, no compromise—consequence, a
big concern snuffed out, and a most useful
life reduced to impotence. Sydney papers credit
(or discredit) Mr. Murray with having been the
real originator of the London donkey contingent.
Well, the true author certainly lived at Manly
(whether it was W. B. Dailey, Gen. Strickland, or
G. A. Murray, who all used to live in a bunch in
the seaside village). All three claimed paternity,
and Strickland went to his grave a disappointed
man because Dailey got the kudos and the statue
—the poor, dumpy little statue.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the case ef ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
The good effect upon the railway-
traffic which a general bursting-up of big
landed estates would have is shown in the recent
cutting-up of the sheep stations in a portion of
the Yic. Western District. The goods traffic at
Wickliffe-road railway station (distributing centre
for the new farming community) has increased
fully a hundredfold during the last year or two.
Before that it was a novelty (except for a few
weeks during the wool season) to see a truck in the
station yard. Now the yard promises soon to
need enlargement to meet the demands upon it.
The passenger traffic shows a similar increase.
Why cannot the dull-brained Vic. Govt, see this
as a sample of how the railway deficit may be
wiped out ? The lines run through scores of miles
of big non-producing estates to reach the farmers
beyond. Why not have farmers all along the
route ?
OLD STOCK.
BUNG : “This is a drop of good stuff. Had it by me seven years.”
COOKY : “ Well, don’t try workin' yer old tack off on me. Trot
out something fresh, or I’ll go across the road.”
A teamster talked to a Bulletin man of
his last trip across from Broken Hill to the White
Cliffs opal-field, where from 1500 to 2000 people
have been for several weeks making acquaintance
with Famine: —
The man gave me three coppers change out of the
shilling I threw down for a 21b. loaf ; and as three
coppers are three coppers on a long dry trip, I turned
into the next shop for threepenn’orth of apples. The
woman turned over her little shrivelled stock till she
found one the right size—my threepenn’orth. It’s no use
trying to get through now with a big load unless you’ve
got a very strong team and don’t mind losing some of it.
It’s only 160 miles, but several of the stages are dry.
I met a bullooky last trip, whose stock hadn’t been
watered for three days, ana then for the end of the third
day he. was reckoning on a tank which I knew was run
out. Poor brutes, they’re lying all along the track. If
one knocks up you must leave it—you risk all the rest
and yourself, too, if you stay or go slow to suit it. Most
of us just rush through now with light loads on express
waggons, and then we’re two days without water, the
last day into the Cliffs and the first day out. You
couldn't get a drink for a beast there for love or lucre.
And we have to cart every mouthful of feed for both
ways. The last trip I came back on rice; they were
round my camp, poor little kids, begging for bran to
make a bit of bread. There hadn’t been any sold for
eight days, and for nearly a week the stores had
been clean out of flour. ' I had picked up three
bags of it for a team that had broken down, and
the word got through the township that it was
coming. There were people who walked out two miles to
meet me, they wanted to make sure. The man it belonged
to divided it up equally, about a couple of pints to each.
That was a shilling’s worth. An Afghan came in with six
701 b. bags of chaff on his camel. A teamster drawing
water put down a pound a bag for it. He had 12 horses,
poor as wood ; he might get two feeds for his team out of
it. A few days after that it was 275. 6d. a bag. There’s
a station tank of water 16 miles out, but there isn’t the
power to fetch it in. Most of the young fellows have
left, or lots of them ; but the men with their families
—how can they ? They just hang on, God only
knows how; hang on and hope. Yes, it’s dead
miserable and you get out as soon as your
load’s off, and your team has had a blow—back through
the sand that growls under your wheels, to the water you
left at daybreak yesterday morning, flogging your poor,
parched, leg-weary, staggering brutes to save their lives
and your own—if it isn’t hell, it’s pretty close to it.
And that’s how sometimes they win the opal that
burns and dances in my lady’s bosom.
A girl’s letter from the West :
We live out west, so are in the worst of the drought.
Not a drop of rain has fallen on it for over 12 months.
One tank has held out wonderfully during this drought
and still has a few inches of what we once called water in
it. What it is now I can’t sa3 T . It is of a greenish tint
and you can smell it from any corner of the thirteen
thousand acre run. Have been carting water from the
nearest neighbor for what stock there is left alive. Had
one cow until recently. It was a young cow. I found it
one night leaning against a tree, looking rather thin, I
thought. I took a fancy to it and sneaked it some hay
every night after dark.' I kept on doing this for a good
while. She was always in the same spot every night and
seemed to know we were engaged in a nefarious trans-
action, for she never made any noise when I brought the
feed, as some silly animals would have done. After I had
been fetching her the hay every night for about a week, I
began to notice a very strong smell. As each night it was
getting worse, I thought there must be a dead iguana in
the tree and said to myself that I would make it my
business to go down in the morning and have a look.
When I got into the paddock next morning I could see
my cow lying down dead, and the smell was even w'orse
than before. All the hair was coming off ray cow and she
was green and decayed. She had been dead for a fort-
night leaning against the tree. I then discovered that
the other cattle had come up every night and eaten the
hay after I was gone, and this night they had been fight-
ing over it and had knocked my cow down. I always look
in the daylight now to see what I am feeding.

A yarn is told of a very big sheep-man in
W. Queensland during last year’s drought. He
took 20,000 sheep to Longreach for trucking. A
brother sheep-man, who heard him mention that
he was going to the station to order trucks, got
there before him, and ordered all those available
for next week. He got those 20,000 muttons at
Is. a head.
• • * •
“Rad.”: Re sheep-slaying horses. When
I was on Tolarno Station, Lower Darling, in ’93,
the station thoroughbred sire. “Kit Knubbles”
(he had raced 'in Caulfield Cup in the early
’9o’s), was exercised every day in a large stock-
yard at the home station. Into the yard I had
occasion once to put 150 ration-sheep for a few
minutes, and on returning found the horse charg-
ing the sheep, striking them with his forefeet and
then shaking them in his teeth like a dog scruffing
a rat. On investigating, found two sheep with
broken backs and one severely worried.
“T.H.W.”: Recount-
ing power of Australian
blacks. I know a nigger
who marks billiards accu-
rately—not by shifting score
at every stroke, but in or-
thodox style: “12 to go, 15
to go, 17 to go,” etc., adding
total on to score at finish of
break. He also marks devil’s
pool. Plays the piano as
well as many a public-house
pianist, but cannot read or
write. Another N.W. native
black in same district (Koo-
kynie) tends sheep and reads
Marie Corelli. Likes “ Thel-
ma,” but thinks “The Mas-
ter Christian ” somewhat
heavy. Lent me “The Origin
of Species,” upon which he
discourses intelligently, but
repudiates a theory for which
his own countenance is a
strong argument.

ward of a Westralian hospi-
tal when a big burly team-
ster, dying of enteric, half
raised himself in bed and
started “driving.” “Baw-
ley ” was apostrophised,
Rose was reminded of her

on the off-side ! ”
.** * *
The question of water supply for Queens-
land Railway Department is getting very serious.
Scarcely a week passes without some of the usual
watering stations giving out, and what is being
used is of very bad quality. At Rockhampton
the water used contains 91 grs. of salt per gal.,
and has a total hardness of 33 per cent. In one
week 11 engines failed from the effects of the bad
water used.
* • • *
“P.” : If the present drought continues
much longer we shall all be cleared out-
man, bird and beast. The feathered folk of the
bush are having a terrible time. No grain, no
fruit, and the ground so hard that worm-prospect-
ing is a blank. For the past month I have been
feeding about 50 birds, comprising grey magpies
(jays), black-and-white magpies, satin-birds,
parrots, and one John-Bottom (foreman). As soon
as day breaks they arrive and perch about the
yard, and, when the cook rattles the plates, there
is a general flutter of feathers, and down they
swoop at her feet and devour the scraps. They
are tame as cats, and no sooner is their feast
over than their liquid warblings begin, as if in
thanks. The black-and-white magpies are parti-
cularly fond of picking bones.
• » • •
“ Chas. R.” : Re a recent Bulletin par.
to the effect that the drought had not yet affected
the trees in Central Queensland. Present writer
has been travelling there, and in many places
found not only, trees dying and scrub giving out,
but even the prickly pear withering-up.

Large quantities of half-grown sugarcane
are now being sold at the Brisbane markets for
cattle-food. When cut up in an ordinary chaff-
cutter it has been found to make excellent and
fattening feed, besides being very cheap.
Lucerne chaff is locally selling at £lO 6s. per ton.

Just as N.S.W. Govt, had decided to offer
£5OOO for the best means of eradicating the
prickly pear (which, by the way, was once culti-
vated, like “farmer’s friend,” “Bathurst burr,”
and other “souvenirs of Home”), a little insect
comes forward which promises to do the eradi-
eating for a living. The Govt. Entomologist
reports that, at Mungindi, the pear is being killed
off in large patches by the Rutherglen bug
(Nysius vinitor). This insect breeds in wild
mustard, and when that dies it swarms on to
anything green, notably prickly pear, which then
decays rapidly right to the roots. Experiments
are now being made at the Hawkesbury Agricul-
tural College to determine if the organism of
disease communicated by the bug can be used on
a large scale to wipe out the pear.

“Cecil P.”: Re ringbarking (“D.H.R.”
15/6/’01). Per contra: the system undoubtedly
produces springs where otherwise non-existent.
Further, moisture is either in the ground, in which
case one has it; or it evaporates and helps to pro-
duce more rain without, of course, adding the oxy-
gen that the timber would. In any case, what
good is rain when timber prevents growth of
grass ? Am far from denying the evil effects of
general deforestation in some cases, but does
“D.H.R.” think that clearing of heavy masses of,
say, mallee scrub would make parts of south Cen-
tralia any dryer ? It couldn’t.
How quickly the rain affects Out-back !
On Aug. 20th it was announced that Govt, con-
templated moving the population of White Cliffs
to a concentration camp on the Darling on account
of threatened water-famine. Aug. 22 the mail-
coach from the south into the township was
blocked by a rain-storm, which filled up all the
creeks and covered the country with a sheet of
water.
* * * *
On Shaw’s Rawbelle station, Burnett
district (Q.), is a bullock which has a fifth leg
growing between the shoulder blades. This limb
has three claw-like appendages instead of the
usual hoof. The animal is now seven years old
and quite healthy, but exceedingly vicious.
“W.M.8.”: Re that minimum-
wage. T’other day I asked a she dairy-
farmer, who was cleaning up after 0
cows, why she didn’t get a man to do the
work. “Well,” she replied, “I ’ad a
man ’ere for three years, but he went
’ome to the old country.” “ What wages
did you pay him ?” I asked. “ Five shil-
lings a week,” she said, almost proudly.
“ Mac. D.” : I killed a calf t’other
evening while the cows were feeding in
the paddock about a mile way. The
victim died without a bleat, yet in
10 minutes’ time the mother of that
calf arrived at the yard. She was
still there in the morning, and
would not go into the yard with
the other cows. When I tried to
drive her in she charged me, but
at length I persuaded her with the
stockwhip. Talk about wireless
telegraphy! How did that cow
know I was butchering her calf ?

“Pelican” : W.A. cohentin-
genters should have been valuable
experts in S. Africa for discover-
ing buried arms, Kimberley
“ graves ” being a peculiar feature
of the sand State. When lost
camels or dead pack-horses left
prospectors with stores on hand that
could not be humped out of the wil-
derness and that would be stolen if
left, it was usual to inter the stuff in a shal-
low grave and put rough hand-rails round the
heap. Worked all right until the dodge got to be
generally known when body-snatching became the
order of the day.
* • * •
The country is now so barren around
Merriwa (N.S.W.) that horses have taken to
burrowing after roots. This causes them to
swallow a good deal of sand, and death in time
results from ‘ ‘ sandball. ” Many owners, after
feeding the animals in stables or yards, now turn
them into the paddocks with nosebags on; and
they wander about, or stand head to tail, keeping
flies off each other, till next feed-time.
* * » *
Messrs. Archer Brothers, of Gracemere
(C.Q.), have been successfully experimenting with
Erickly pear as a cattle food. The spines are first
urned off, and the pear is next chaffed and
cooked, and when mixed with bran or chaff is
eagerly eaten by the cattle. There has been no
falling off in the milking capacity of the 400 cows
fed on the pear, and the saving through its use is
estimated at from £6O to £7O per week.
VOICES LOW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VIC.
Through many a year with small respite,
We’ve born the strain and stress of toil;
Our motto still to work and fight.
Though Fate appropriate the spoil.
Where are the ideals of our dreams—
The home on hillside green and plain,
The wealth of acres and of teams,
The rich response to drenching rain ?
Ah I pity us who blindly tread
Before the Drought’s consuming breath :
Around us, bones of the dumb dead,
In front, the scourge—and balm—of death
WILL JARRATT.

A batch to finish-up the question :
“Albo” to “Hypo” once again : Allowing that trees
do shoot from the ground, to begin with : How can the
fork of a tree reach a given height without being carried
up with the tree’s growth ? As a nipper, I was very fond
of transplanting young pines, and with some of these
limbs that were 2ft. from the ground when planted are
now over 6ft. up. Marks, natural or otherwise, remain
stationary on full-grown trees only.
[This seems to be the point that has caused all the
discussion.—Ed. B.]
“Thurla” to “Albo” (B. 20/6 ’O2) re trees carrying
marks upwards. How about surveyors when looking up
old survey-marks on trees ? Do they climb up amongst
the limbs to find them ? I remember once seeing a sur-
veyor in New England looking for a tree marked about
40 years before. When he located it, the marks were
covered by new wood, which, when chopped out, had
the raised numbers on it—the original numbers being
about the usual height of survey-marks. Also, do trees
used as straining-posts for fences root the other posts out
eventually ?
lie trees and marks. “ Richlands,” an
original “Crown grant” to William M’Arthur, was
surveyed, I think, in ’39, and in ’BO a surveyor had occa-
sion to locate one corner of this grant. After much
trouble the corner-tree was found, in a yellow box about
3ft. in dia., the blaze being entirely grown over, though a
mark was visible on the bark. After cutting this away
and taking out a shield-shaped piece of new wood the
original blaze and “ broad-arrow ” were found perfect as
if freshly cut. I have been present many times at
similar recoveries of old survey-marks on trees; and in no
case had the old mark appreciably increased its distance
from the ground.
“G.F.J.": In proof that trees do not carry mark up-
ward. lam at present surveying on a block i. 4 miles sq.
laid out over 60 years ago. The boundary-lines ran
through a score of different timbers, and I have found the
old markings in trees varying from inches to feet in dia.,
yet have never noticed a blaze higher than a man could
conveniently cut with an axe. Again in railway work I
know of a bench-mark having been cut in a small tree and
20 years afterwards the level on the same mark was
checked to the hundredth of a foot.
• • * »
William MacGregor, when Governor of
British New Guinea, was amongst the first to
suppose a connection between mosquito-bite and
malarial fever. In view of the fact that such
connection has since been conclusively demon-
strated, there is interest in the following ex-
tract from a letter recently received in Australia
from S’William—now Governor of Lagos, in
West Africa :
But we shall be able to dispense with the weekly 15gr.
of quinine and with the mosquito-proof rooms in a very
short time. A few months ago this house was infested by
mosquitoes, and Lady Benton died in it of black-water
fever. I had fever last year every day for two or three
months. By destroying the breeding grounds of mos-
quitoes we have all but killed them off That is going to
the root of the evil. It consists in draining, in filling-in
all swamps and puddles, in removing all empty tins,
bottles, &c., and in killing every mosquito in the house.
That rids one of fever, of the pests themselves, of mos-
quito wire-gauge, and of quinine. There has been no
case of fever in my house this year.

An order has been received in Maoriland

Showing how they do business in the Vie.
departments, it is recorded that the Mines, as an
experiment, shipped 60 tons of local brown coal to
Germany to be squeezed into briquettes, the idea
being to turn the State s vast lignite lodes to com-
mercial ad vantage, if possible. That is practically
the end of the experiment, everybody having for-
gotten all about the whole business for years. The
transaction was paid for at top rates, and promptly
jerked out of the official mind until a few months
back, when outside curiosity prompted officialdom
to arise and think. Then inquiries were made in
Germany and all over the place, but no trace of
the lost 60 tons of briquettes has been found, and
the department | resumes in a tired way that the
sample was returned and used for tooth-paste, or
building material, or something without anybody
knowing anything about it.

Kiwi : In your oath-argument you’re being
deceived by a half-truth. There is no moral obligation,
perhaps, to keep an oath taken under compulsion, but no
principle of British law admits that fact, though many
British statutes have at various times prescribed oaths
under pressure (the Test Act, the Corporation Act, and
so on). Further, the King is not compelled to take his
Coronation oath ; he could certainly get out of it by
abdicating. As to the Boer “ traitors ” shot for breaking
neutrality oaths forced upon them, of course the British
shouldn't have shot them after compelling them (in a
way) to the oath. Their cases, if tried under the S’African
civil law, would have come under the Roman-Dutch law,
which is a good deal more liberal and just in many re-
spects than English common law... .Lackobite : That's

Korero : What’s in a name ? A great deal. A man’s
name, when it is given, shows something of the character
of his parents. When a boy is named Ebenezer he mostly
deserves it, because that is the sort of person his
father or his mother is; and the name is in him
a mark of hereditary predisposition. Considering
Christian names—isn’t Percy usually a very naice
youth, and Jabez a young man with Sunday-school
leanings and an inclination for embezzlement?

The toughness of the Queensland infant !
A few weeks ago a 2|-year-old girl named Evelyn
Harris drifted into the bush at Ballon, and dis-
appeared. She was out the whole of a bitterly
cold night, and was found next day making very
respectable progress along the Mitchell-road,
having covered 20 miles in the 24 hours ! ! The fact
that this sturdy and valiant mite was named Evelyn
has no direct bearing on the case. Most children
at the Back of Beyond are named either that or
Muriel.

J KISSED her once, and held
her tight,
She blushed a vivid red ;
I watched her eye to note its
light:
“You HORRID BEAST 1” sh«
said.
I kissed her twice, and held her
still
(I watched her eye in dread);
I squeezed her gently, too, until
“ You WICKED THING ! ” she
said.
I kissed her thrice. She wasn’t
“ wild,”
She only turned her head ;
And then she watched my eye,
and smiled :
“ You DARLING BOY ! ” she
said. '
OKTIN.
A Railway Strike.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The driver was having a look around his engine
to see that all was ready to “go for it ” as soon as
he could get away.
“Not on strike! No. The strike threat is
withdrawn. We are going to try ‘ constitutional ’
means to get Parliament to say that we shall not
be retrenched.
“Do I think the trains would have run had we
gone on strike ? No. If the signalmen had come
out, as I believe they would have, not a train
could have left Melbourne or arrived there. That
is my belief.
“ What about the retired men taking our places ?
They could not do it. That was mere bluff.
Besides, I doubt if many would have attempted it.
I heard of one or two who had arranged to start,
and then take a ‘ hiding ’ from the strikers, and
go off sick. They claimed that that would have
given them a right to compensation—which they’d
have forfeited if they got the sack.
“ The mining engine-drivers? Yes, I saw the
letters in the papers stating that they were pre-
pared to drive the locomotives at a day’s notice.
Look here, the writers did not know what they
were talking about. I doubt if there was one
letter from a genuine mining engine-driver. They
know that they could not do our work right away
as well as we know we could not do theirs. I like
to hear people talk about engine-driving ! Yes ;
it looks simple. You just put tlie lever in forward
and open the ‘regulator’ gear to go ahead, and
reverse the lever to go back. That is all there
seems to be in it. But I would like to ask these
smart men a few questions as to what they would
do under different circumstances. For instance,
what should be done if, on coming to a station, it
was found impossible to shut the steam off ? Or
if the engine got off the road ? Or a piston-rod
broke? Or the reversing-lever broke ? Or if the
pump would not shut off? Or the injector would
not work ? Or a cylinder end knocked out ? Ora
‘ big end ’ cotter was lost ? Or a link-hanger
broke? Or the drawhook behind the tender
broke ? Or dozens of similar common engine
failures occurred ? Then there is the Westing-
house Brake. That would not be used till the
new chums were it, you say ? Then
God help the travelling public ! To stop when
necessary, without any ‘ Westinghouse, ’ would be
almost impossible, especially if the necessity arose
suddenly. We look upon a temporary failure of
the brake as one of the most awkward things that
can happen to us on the road.”
“ How.did they get on before it was invented?”
“Well, I don’t know ; that often puzzles me.
Of course, the trains have increased enormously
in weight since then. Numerous other difficulties
arise that the outsider knows nothing of. The
engine might start ‘ priming ’ going up a bank.
In two minutes the boiler would be in danger if
not skilfully handled, so quickly is the water
thrown out. The very strong blast of the loco-
motive would be one of the greatest surprises,
among many surprises, to the stationary engine-
driver. For that reason alone a raw hand would
never keep a full pressure of steam. Every driver
who takes a young engine-cleaner out of the sheds
knows that he has to do most of the firing himself
for the first few trips. Suppose, then, the men
were both .new chums ! No, it is not so simple as
it may look. I have merely touched upon one or
two of the things that may happen, and are hap-
pening, every day. But we must go now. Wa’ve
got ‘line clear.’”

land for settlement
It has been decided by the Government
that an ares of about 1200 acres on tbe Moe
Swamp shall be thrown open for settlement
as soon as possible. This land is situate on
the north side of the main drain, midway
between Moe and Trafalgar. It is as yet
but poorly drained, and the sum of £lOOO
.has therefore been allotted \ for the purpose
of making it suitable for settlement. The
work of draining is to be done in small
contracts, let to men registered at the Labor
Bureau, and the first three will be let on
Thursday next, a deposit of £1 being neoes-
sary in each case.

AN INTERCEPTED - FUTTIN<V’
Violet Town, Tuesday.
Considerable excitement was created at the
railway station last evening at the time the
t> o clock train arrived (from Benalla, when
Mrs. Copeman. an elderly lady, anxiously
shouto 1 to the .station-master to keep the
train until she could secure her daughter,
aged 18, from the company of a young man,
whom she charged with being the abductor
of the girl, having induced her to leave her
home ai Baddaginnie The young lady re-
turned home through th- joint persuasion [of
the .mother and police.*
