TREATMENT of returned ,
SOLDIERS.
TO THE EDITOR OR THE AGE.
Sir, you allow me space to call the
attention of the public to the very serious
condition of the unemployed returned soldierß
—a condition which is more serious than is
generally known. At a meeting held this
week some very unfavorable revelations were
made, several of the men stating that
although they had been home from South
Africa for three months, amounts varying
up to .€5O are still owing to them, repeated
application having been made
for the amount.
A .JUDGE’S SOLICITUDE
PRISONER OFFERED CHOICE
OF GAOLS
COMFORT OR CONVENIENCE.
In The charge of stealing wearing apparel
brought against Harold Serkoff, a Russian,
Mr. •Justice Williams, to the interpreter,
said . “ Ask him what gaol he would like to
go to. Melbourne or Castlemaine. Tell him
that the Castlemaine gaol is a better-class
gaol than the other, but Melbourne may be
more convenient for him.”

POLITICAL
POINTS
The permanent garrison of ‘ ‘ conquered ”
S’Africa is to be 60,000 of all arms, or double the
number sent to the Crimea from first to last.
One theory about the High Commissioner-
ship is that Premier Barton is surveying things
in London and considering how the billet would
fit himself.
Said that Mr. James Ashton’s motion on
the fodder duties, in N.S.W. Assembly, repre-
sented a bid on his part for the leadership of the
Opposition.
Poor old Solemn Lee, leader of N.S.W.
Opposition ! H</ has cause to be solemn. He can’t
take to his bed for a few days with a cold but
there is at once a rumor that he is about to resign
his leadership, and hungry eyes are cast on his
sick room.
From the Gulf :
Does The Bulletin know that the Chows intend to pour
Into the Commonwealth from Port Darwin, via Camoo-
weal, and Burketown, and that already batches of them
have come through ? Reason is, lam told, that Queens-
land Govt, finds it too expensive to hold them in check
on the border and quod them when they try to slip over,
so the poll-tax is suspended. As The Bulletin is the
“ White Australia ” paper, and it was the N.Q. electors
that put Queensland into the Federation, perhaps you
will draw public attention to this important matter.
A CORONATION INCIDENT .
1ST COACHIE (in the crush, to another coachie
who has also got the Royal livery): "'Ullo,
Tom. You on this gime, too? What sort of a
nigger ’ave you got to drive? ”
2ND COACHIE: “Not my luck; hain't got a black
swell at all, only a blanky Horstralian.”
Victorian public servants are growling
about a reduction which touches nobody under
£ 125, and up to £l5O to the tune of only 3 per cent.
What do they say to the Queensland retrenchment,
under which only £lOO exemption is allowed, and
incomes over that pay per cent, to the revenue ?
What reception would the Commonwealth
Parliament give a motion specifying that in all
matters relating to subsidies to squadrons, salaries
to Governors-General, and such like, despatches
from the Imperial Government shall be laid on
the table before the questions concerned are
debated ?
Maoriland Lands Minister Duncan told
the Farmers’ Union that he had tried to induce
his colleagues to borrow £3,000,000 to make roads
for the benefit of the farmers, but they were un-
decided on the point. Even the suggestion, how-
ever, shows how the borrowing boom is rising in
Maoriland.
John Bull is quite feminine in his
caprices; a year ago the Boers were getting all
his abuse and the Australians all his marmalade.
Now the King runs after the Boer leaders, and
the Australians, it is argued, are so filthy a lot
that they can’t travel on a transport without
causing an epidemic.
“Ramp” : A practical railway man who
is an ardent admirer of Q. Minister for Railways
Leahy informs me that in many parts of the North
Coast line between Brisbane and Maryborough
you can pull the dog-spikes out of the sleepers
with your fingers! Some day the rails will spread
and then the writs will come along.
Premier Philp recently :
The country is suffering from unprecedented party
politics, which should be dropped, and all should pull
together to lift the State out of its troubles.
T’other day Q. Labor party took Philp’s advice
and gave him a hand with his income-tax against
the Govt, discontents—and neither he nor his
Ministers looked either grateful or happy.
Anomalies of Tas. Treasurer Bird’s house-
cwm-income-tax proposals. A Hobart alderman
lives in a house assessed at £4O, has an income of
at least £5OOO, and will be taxed on the basis of
an income of £270. A Hobart pressman lives in
a house assessed at £6O, has an income of £2OO or
so, and will be taxed on the basis of an income of
£490.
Commonwealth Executive soundly, if
tardily, decides to charge the same telegraphic
rates for Ministers’ as for other political speeches.
The arrangement which, for instance, let Deakin’s
speech through to Brisbane at Is. 6d. for the first
100 words, and Is. per 100 after, while charging
M ‘Millan’s 4s. 6d. for the first hundred, was in-
defensible.
The suspension-of-the-fodder-duties cry
grows more farcical with each development.
N.S.W. Premier John See having offered to im-
port, as a Govt, enterprise, fodder from abroad
(when no duty would need to be paid) the Cob-
denites still squeal discontentedly. It’s not duty-
free fodder they want, but the chance to discredit
the Federal tariff.
Mr. Seddon is credited by Review of
Reviews Stead with the desire to be first Premier
of United S’Africa. The absurdity of it! That
first Premier will have to be a person more or less
acceptable to the Dutch population, which pre-
dominates in Cape Colony, not a mad howler for
Boer blood. And Bun Tuck would have to work
in S’African politics, anyhow, for years ere he
could get a show, whatever his views. No ! Mr.
Richard Seddon is after something much softer
than that.
The glad news comes from South Australia
that Private Enterprise is willing to build a grav-
ing dock at Port Adelaide, on
condition that in any year
when the profits are less than
per cent, on capital the
State pays enough to make
them up to 3J per cent.; that
when there is a loss the State
stands the loss and pays the
per cent.; and that when
the profits are more than 3J
percent. Private Enterprise
keeps the lot. And this
Bort of thing calls itself
Private Enterprise!
The lately-proposed
Junction of the two Houses
in Tasmania resembles
closely a N.S.W. consti-
tutional device of 70 years
ago. Numerous theories
were then discussed.
Wentworth fathered three
or four on his own account,
and he was esteemed a
poor politician who had
not at least one to his
i credit. The Patriotic As-
sociation of the period ex-
haustively considered most
of them, and in a public
meeting in Sydney in 1835
inclined to a junction of two
Houses into one body, to num-
ber 50 members, 10 of them to
be Government nominees like
the present Leg. Council. No-
thing was done, however, and it
took another 20 years to reach the
bicameral system with which Re-
sponsible Government opened.
The shearers’ strike has dis-
closed the fact that that queerly-constituted body,
the N.S.W. Labor Bureau, has spent £86,463 on
free passes to laborers for the country, and has, to
date, received back as refunds only £28,471. When
power was given the Bureau to issue those passes
rt was on the understanding that employers would
deduct the money out of wages and pay it back.
Have the laborers stuck to that £50,000 or have
the employers stuck to it ? And why did not the
Bureau make some effort to recover ?
If the threatened Victorian railway-strike
had eventuated it is considered possible that jbhe
Irvine-Shiels-Tommy Bent Government would
have dissolved Parliament and gone nobly and
fearlessly to the country to ask for an endorse-
ment of its “firm attitude.” And the Factories
Act, which dies at the close of this session, would
have perished comfortably in the confusion.
The Boer Generals have been greeted
with exuberant enthusiasm in London:
This trait should make the papers glad,
We chased the little Boers about,
And having stolen-all they had,
And put their ragged bands to rout,
With kindling hearts, from sweep to King,
We now.forgive them everything !
“ Demos ” writes : .
It would prove very instructive if some Vic. Labor
member were to Call for a return of the sums of money
paid by parents of children for school requisites of all
kinds in an average three months in Melbourne State
schools. The totals would open the eyes of many who
cherish delusions about the “ freeness ” of the system of
“ free education.” It is alleged that teachers occasionally
compel compliance with their demands by alluding to
children who are unable to get these requisites as
“ paupers.”
General Hutton struck an awkward snag
when h<? gave out his costly “schools of instruc-
tion” scheme without Minister Bill Lyne’s
sanction. He afterwards explained that when he
published the scheme he was under the impression
that it had received Ministerial approval, and that
“he had been misled by a telephone message.”
General Hutton’s luck is clean out lately : he has
to provide an awkward explanation on an average
twice a week.
Senator Walker has returned from
America , .
greatly struck with the good feeling that existed
towards Great Britain. # .
Doubtless that good feeling does exist in
the American circles in which Senator
Walker moved; just as in the Australian
circles in which Senator Walker now moves
there will be a general approval of his action
in running away from his Senatorial duties
for some months at a most important period.
But in neither case is public i opinion reflected
by Senator Walker’s circle. The Yankee
papers are, almost without exception, hos-
tile to England or contemptuously pat-
ronising towards her; and they represent
the genuine U.S.A. opinion.
Governments that howl for paltry
reductions in the petty salaries of employees
and let a tax on betting machinery go by
the board are quacks in the business of their
calling. Maoriland made £17,500 last year
out of a tax onbetting, and whom did it
hurt ? France makes hundreds of thousands
out of the tax. Victoria should be able to
make at least £40,000 by a tote and a
reasonably-heavy tote-tax.
Labor member W. G. Spence pleaded
hard in the Reps., during the discussion of
the duties on vegetables, for the pastoralists
of N.S. Wales, and showed in harrowing
terms how hard-up they were on account of
the drought. Spence is also boss organiser
of the Shearers’ Union, which is demanding
from said pastoralists an increase of 12$ per
cent, on the shearing rates.
Q. Govt, had, perforce, to drop its
scheme for taxing civil servants, with less
than £lOO salary, to the tune of 5 per cent.
But now comes the income-tax, and the Q.
Govt, (which touches nothing that it does
not adorn) has interpolated a poll-tax of £1 on all
men with incomes under £lOO. The Q. unem-
ployed can now be set to work collecting the
poll-tax one from the other.
“ Kiwi ” from Maoriland :
A surprise in local matters political |s Acting-Premier
Ward’s successful leadership—surprising especially to
those who honestly believed thatnoman could possibly take
the place of King Seddon. Seddon’s mana has dwindled
severely. Ward’s tactful leadership, coupled with the woe-
ful collapse of the audacious attempts to drag the self-
governing States into the military madness that passes
for genuine Imperialism, and the no less ignoble break-
down of the much-advertised Customs Union, have shown
plainly, even to the Premier’s most ardent admirers, that
he is, after all, a small man when he gets among really
big things.
Boyd, member for Melbourne City, made
one strong point in his speech on the Vic. Re-
trenchment Bills. He showed that the Credit
Foncier, which cuts its valuations very low, values
the taxable lands of the State (averaging from
many advances made) at £7,875,000 over the muni-
cipal valuations. “ If,” said he “ shires were to
practise a little common honesty in their valua-
tions they would be £393,750 better off, and the
Govt, subsidy could be done away with.” Also,
said subsidy would go far towards annihilating the
deficit.
Tasmania’s Parliament spends a large part
of its time talking hazily about Tattersall without
coming to any definite conclusion, and the other
day M‘Call, M.H.R., grew tired. He proposed
that, at next election, a referendum should be
taken as to whether the State approved of
Tattersall, and, if it approved, whether it pre-
ferred that the Government should take over the
business. This proposal to do something definite
about the misty grievance shocked everybody,
and M’Call’s proposal was rejected by more than
two to one.
Vic. Public Service Committee, after
climbing down and submitting to the magnitude
and magnificence of Bent, actually carried a re-
solution congratulating “ Tommy ” on “ his appre-
ciation of the gravity of the situation and the
firmness, care and ability with which he has
averted the disastrous consequences of a threatened
strike ! ” The Vic. public servants have discovered
the policy of turning the other cheek. There
were many hard names for the talented and
courteous minister when the trouble was brewing,
but he is now found to have a winning way with
him.
To prove how little the N.S.W. suspension-
of-the-fodder-duties’ people know what they are
really driving at, Premier See has published
figures showing thgt, for seven months past, the
duties collected on “fodder” hardly averaged
£lOOO a month for the whole State. Further, a
member last week asked Premier See to take
steps to prevent the large exportations of produce
now going on in N.S.W., instancing one shipment
of 30,000 bags. Queer that the suspension-of-
duties crowd, who generally deprecate Govt,
interference with the sacred rights of private
enterprise, are now the first to claim the State’s
protection.
Ex-Premier Peacock publicly avows that,
it he is to boss Vic. politics again, Trenwith will
be the first man to be taken into his Ministry.
Peacock would revel in the chance of getting back
in this way upon David Syme for smashing him
up, and there is little doubt that two of the Age
proprietor’s friends, M'Culloch and Wynne, would
be left out—the former as a hopeless old muddler,
and the tricky Wynne for his shameless abandon-
ment of the Factories Act directly he got out of
office. It would only need the throwing over of
Syme’s other pal, “ Sam ” Gillott, and the inclu-
sion of Prendergast, or some other plain-speaking
person, to fill David’s cup with wormwood.
There was a gushing women’s demonstra-
tion to Bill Lyne at Sydney last Saturday—
announced as from N.S.W. Womanhood Suffrage
League, but really representing “ the other cat.”
Enfranchised woman in N.S.W. is broken up
into two camps whose bitter mutual animosity
gives great promise of a lovely scratching-match
before next general election. It was the Women’s
Progressive Association which shed tears of
gratitude over Bill’s hand on Saturday. The

womanhood suffragists intend shortly to enthuse

In collecting her income-tax, South Aus-
tralia always has distinguished between incomes
from personal exertion and incomes from property.
She began in ’B4 with 3d. on the one and 6d. on
the other; sprang them to 4M. and 9d. in ’93;
and to 6d. and Is. in ’94. Began next to dis-
tinguish also between fat incomes (£BOO and over)
and lean ones in ’95, by collecting 4£d. from the
one and 6d. from the other when derived from
personal exertion, and 9d. and Is. respectively
when derived from property; and now her
Jenkins proposes and 7d. in the one case,
and 10£d. and Is. ljd. in the other. But even
this very top-notch tax of Is. 1,4 d. is a halfpenny
short of the all-round tax in England.
The Owe’Sullivan on the latest Sydney
tramway extension to La Perouse:—
The total cost of the loop is given as £13,569 7S. 6d. Mr.
O’Sullivan, however, points out that it has practically
paid for its construction, inasmuch as it has rendered
possible a saving of eight shillings per thousand on two
million bricks in connection with the penitentiary
buildings now in course of erection, and also afforded an
economical means of transit for the great number of
coffins which were removed to the La Verouse Cemetery
from Devonshire-street.
This story about how the new tram has paid for
itself has just one drawback —the same old in-
variable drawback. The tramway isn’t paid for.
If it has paid for itself then somebody has inter-
cepted and embezzled the money, as per usual.
The unspeakable meanness of the pre-
sent Tory Government in Melbourne! Its re-
duction-of-wages scheme includes public servants
getting as little as £125 a year. Now it employs
casual printers at Bs. a day. If they were employed
all the year, which they are not, including Xmas,
New Year’s Day, Good Friday, etc.—when the
Govt, offices are closed and they are not allowed
to work—they would get £125 4s. per annum—4s. a
year over the limit at which retrenchment begins.
Not being allowed to work on Xmas and Good
Friday they can’t make £125. But the Irvine
Government reckons them as employees receiving
ver £125 per annum, and docks them of 3 per
°ent. !
Sydney morning papers are fighting so
fiercely for the right to send their bundles free by
rail that one would think there was “ millions in
it.” As a matter of fact they are only asked to
find £2500 a year between them; and as the
passage of the Federal postal laws will shortly put
the matter on a definite (and a paying) basis the
whole sum at issue is probably not more than
£1250—£625 each. Over that sum each paper is
squealing at the rate of 16 columns or so a week,
which is surely bad business. A newspaper
column ought to be worth about £10; and the
space devoted to verbatim reports of the select
committee inquiry on the subject is, so far as
public interest is concerned, absolutely thrown
away.
Curious par. from a Sydney D.T. article
(23/8/’O2) discussing the urgent need for water-
pipes for the Sydney supply :
To ask pipe manufacturers, however, to construct 13
miles of pipes in less than six months’ time, and with not
a twentieth part of the material in the State to execute
the order, shows once again how necessary it is to have
in the States—to meet such-a grave emergency as this-
smelting works and plate-rolling mills, such as it is urged
might have been established had not the Bonus Bill been
thrown out.
And only three months ago the D.T. shrieked its
loudest against the Bonus Bill which would have
made “necessary provision for such grave emer-
gencies.” Is the D.T. going to change its coat, or
do occasional fiscal truths creep into its columns
merely by accident ?

VITADATIO
Read Mr. O’Connor’s Testimony.
Venetian, Kennedy Street, Glebe Point,
9th April, 1900.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir, —You will be glad to know of the cure your
medicine, VITADATIO, has worked on me. About 12
months ago I was attacked with inflammation of the
Bladder. For eight months I was under the treatment
of three of the Sydney doctors, but their efforts failed to
cure me. In November last I commenced taking VITA-
DATIO, and in less than two months I was thoroughly
cured. I have had no trouble since then.
I trust this letter will be of use to you, and induce
others to try VITADATIO.
Yours faithfully,
(Signed) S. W. O’CONNOR.

If You Please, Miss, Give Me Heaven.
“ Papa, I’m so sad and lonely,”
Sobbed a tearful little child ;
“ Since dear mamma’s gone to Heaven,
Pa,pa, darling, you’ve not smiled.
I will speak to her and tell her
That we want her to come home;
Just you listen, and I’ll call her
Through the telephone:
Chorus—
“ l£ you please, Miss, give me Heaven—for my Mamma’s
there;
You can find her with the angels on the golden stair.
She’ll be glad it’s me who’s speaking—call her, won’t you
please, J ’
For I want to surely tell her we’re so lonely here.”
When the girl received this message, s
Coming o’er the telephone,
How her heart thrilled in that moment,
And the wires seemed to moan ;
“ I will answer, just to please her:
‘ Yes, dear heart, I’ll soon come home.’ ”
“ Kiss me, Mamma, kiss your darling,
Through the telephone.”
Chorus— “ If you please, Miss," etc.

“An Exile ” writing from S’Africa under
date June 28 :
Six months without a Bulletin ! Not that they
haven’t been sent, but the censor has had the benefit
of them. Now one comes to hand cunningly con-
cealed inside a Jingo newspaper. Such is martial law. I
may not even post this openly to you, but must send it
under cover to a friend.
The Boers have recognised the fact that 10 to 1 is
longer odds in war than in racing and have cried a go
for the present. That is all it amounts to I really believe.
“ They are proud to become citizens of this great Empire.”
Of course. How credulous we are all of a sudden 1
What filthy, treacherous ruffians they were a few weeks
ago 1 And what brave, unselfish, patriotic heroes they are
now, to be sure ! An Afrikander woman who has through-
out taken a great interest in the welfare of the inmates of
the concentration camps, addressed the women of Port
Elizabeth the other day : “ Ons Land (our country) will
be Ons Land yet, in spite of all. The British have over-
whelmed us by numbers in the field, but it will be by
numbers in the land that the Dutch will conquer yet. For
we bear children by dozens, whilst the English bear them
by twos and threes, and it is ourselves, Dutch women of
South Africa, who must see to it that the Dutch shall
inherit the land.” This highly treasonable speech was
not reported in the papers, but it showed that the lady
had not studied the statistics of recent years for nothing.
The Dutch seem to have successfully transplanted them-
selves in this country, whilst the English emphatically
have not. Perhaps it is because the Dutch stick to the
land, the English to the towns. The Boers must be about
the most prolific race on earth, whilst the vital statistics
of England and English colonies are sad reading. And
gold mines work out in a life time but the land is there
for ever. The Boer spirit is as strong as ever. There
may or may not be another Anglo-Boer war. But,
whether by bullet or ballot, the result in the long run
will be the same. The Dutch will rule the land. Now
some want to suspend the Constitution of Cape Colony, I
because, under representative government, the Dutch will
have the balance of power This has been a British colony
for a century. Looks well for British South Africa ? The
Imperial Government, it is said, will force a Federation
of S’African States, whether the people are ready for it or
not. The Chartered Co. shareholders will then be relieved
of the burden of Rhodesia with the S’African taxpayers’
money.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A member of the betting public has been pro-
ceeded against by the police for making a bet on Canter-
bury (Sydney) Park racecourse. Action was taken under
a by-law of the local municipality, which provides for
any person who shall “ frequent” a street or public place
within the niunicipality for the purposes of wae'erin (y or
making a bet being fined £2. Accused’s lawyer success-
fulb’took the point that a casual visit to the park was
not frequenting ” it, and as the meetings at Canterbury
are only held every few weeks the by-law is plainly un-
workable. In the event of the Canterbury aldermen
framing a workable rule against betting on the part
of the public, the absurdity will be reached of fining
a man heavily for doing at Canterbury what the world
and his wife (not always his own wife) do with impunity
at Randwick. J
* * * *
Sydney Mo. Club (Tatts.) meeting at Randwick,
Saturday, was not a success for the bejewelled one.
Mostly heavily-backed nags got home, and the Gentiles
had one good day’s outing. But it is only a passing
shock that the next meeting will probably balance, while
the following one will provide a surplus.
* • • *
Cyanide’s easy gatbering-in of Spring Handicap
at Sydney Tatt’s. meet, Saturday, further emphasises her
prospects of doing something big during coming A. J.C.
meeting. She fairly romped away with the money, and
made hacks of the opposition in the finishing half-mile.
Bar the possibility of Patronage starting, the Metrop.
looks a fairly good thing for Cyanide, who has done some
excellent track work ; but as she is prepared at Rosehill,
touts know nothing about her, except through rumor.
• • » *
Looks as if unlucky Randwick trainer-owner,
Dick Wootten, is going to have a turn t’other way. About
three weeks ago out of late Mr. R. Richards’ racing stud
he secured Baccarat for 230g5., and on Saturday, just
before flagfall for Tatts.’ Tramway Handicap, put a com-
mission on the market, getting from twenties down to
eights, which brought him a big parcel of money and the
stakes, lOOsovs. Wootten was fortunate to land the coin.
Great Scot got away lengths last and finished a head
behind, while Myall (third), perhaps a similar distance
further off, was twice blocked in the concluding 200yds.
Despite the judge’s decision, experienced men well
placed to see declared the finish a dead-heat.
• • • •
W.f.a. Hampden Stakes at Randwick, Satur-
day, fell to Abundance, who, on form, promises well for
coming A.J.O. Derby. Abundance is owned by bookie
Bob Phillip, is beautifully formed and uncommonly tract-
able, but wants plenty of riding. His only win prior to
Saturday was V.R.O. Aidful Stakes. Abundance is half-
brother to Wink, by Pilgrim’s Progress (imp.) from Bean-
fast, by Plebeian, from Festival by Sterling. Beanfast was
brought Australiawards 12 years ago by late Mr. W. R.
Wilson, who bred Abundance.
• * • *
Patronage’s showing at Rosehill t’other Satur-
day has considerably cramped the odds about him for
Metrop. and Caulfield and Melbourne Cups. Right from
the opening he topped the list of Metrop. quotations, and
now owners (Messrs. Brown of Newcastle) are peculiarly
placed. If they pull him out of the big A.J.O. event
'twill make no difference ; they’ll have to take short odds
in either of the other two they send him for. Then
there’s the always present possibility of his getting stale
if kept too long. Probably he’ll have a cut for the
Metrop. with its substantial prize and Tatts. sweep possi-
bilities, even if the stable doesn’t feel inclined to “buy”
Mo.’s money.
* * _ • •
Old-time sportsman, Mr. Geo. Matthews, who
died at Bathurst, t’other day. was prominent on N.S.W.
turf and owned many forefronters of their time. His
Blair Athol and Ghinni Ghinni won Hawkesbury Spring
Handicap and Guineas. The deceased had the Sports-
man’s Arms at Kelso (N.S.W.), and was among the oldest
members of Bathurst Agricultural Association.
• • * •
The totalisator in France protects the public
against the “ accident ” of a favorite getting beaten by a
despised stable companion. A recent tale is told of three
Australian mining-men (J. E. Scantlebury was one of
them) who backed a winner at Longchamps without
knowing they had any interest in its chance. They
bought tote tickets for a certain gee-gee on the advice of
a casual tipster, and when the animal came in “ nowhere ”
they tore the tickets up. Presently, they learned that
the winner being a stable-companion of their defeated
dead-bird they were entitled to half dividends, so they
gathered the fragments of their tickets, fixed them up
satisfactorily, and got paid. The French tote doesn’t
allow one horse only to be backed when it has a stable
companion in the race. The investment is divided
between the pair. In short, the machine stops the stable
and its jockeys from “going for one” as far as ticket-
Surchasers are concerned. Their troubles asj to which
orse is trying!

The V.R.C. loses more than £2OOO a year
“ royalty ” by the reduction in railway fares to Fleming-
ton, whereas the Railway Dept, forfeits only £l3OO, with
the hope of recovering some of it through an increase in
the traffic.

Victor Trumper’s splendid vindication of his
baptismal name reminds one that when Stoddart’s first
eleven met him as a college boy in a match at Sydney
they were almost unanimous in predicting that he would
develop into a champion batsman. Their blowing of
Trumpet trumpet was thought at the time to be an
indirect disparagement of Clem Hill. Now it seems that
the visitors were judging boy Trumper honestly from
their knowledge of the style in which runs are made on
English wickets. Trumper’s record for the season is equal
to the best of Grace’s early achievements.
* * * *
A feature of the Australian-Kent match, ended
Saturday, was the number of accidental “outs.” Gregory
and Day (Kent), l.b.w. twice. Trumper was l.b.w. and
run out. Duff, Noble, Hill, Saunders and Blythe (Kent),
run out. Huish, 1.b.w., and a couple of instances of
“ playing on ” occurred. Of 11 Kent-Australia matches
the odd success belongs to Australia. Trumper scored
another best through beating Darling’s record (1941)
for Australians in England by 35, he only needs 24
more to complete his 2000.
* * * *
Indications are that Trumble’s craze for golf
will lure him from cricket, for the two won’t run toge-
ther. One English scribe writes that heavy work on
Hawksworth golf-links day before made Trumble glad to
be excused from shaping against a mixed and mediocre
England 11 at Bradford. Trumble is even keener on golf
than cricket ; he puts his soul into the one and only his
head into the other.

Apropos the three centuries notched by Noble,
Hill and Hopkins in opposition to Gloucestershire. This
feat has been achieved often. The English record (four)
belongs jointly to Yorkshire (in ’96) and Derbyshire (’9B),
but the world’; est is owned out here and was accom-
plished in a iiK -uh, S.A. v. N.S.W., by five of the latter
side—Gregory (168), Noble (153), Poidevin (140 n. 0.), Duff
(119), and Iredale (118). Then N.S.W. compiled 918—the
highest total for a first-class contest.
• • * *
Return matches N.S.W. v. Q., played this and
next Saturday, will finish this season’s football. N.S.W.
won both matches played in Sydney, but Q. should do
better at Brisbane. The visiting team is a strong one,
its back division being nearly all from the 'Varsity team.
As usual,'the country representation is minute.
• • « ■
’Varsity-Glebe match, last Saturday, prac-
tically decided the destination of this year’s
N.S.W. Metropolitan Rugby premiership. The struggle
was superb, and 11,000 onlookers were wildly
excited. Half time saw no score and sides appar-
ently equal. Well through the second half neither
had an advantage, till 'Varsity notched a try then
a goal from a mark shoved Glebe forward. “ Uni’s ”
dash and force were splendid. Glebe fended and fought
well, and the public outside the railings vented its
feelings in a sustained roar. ’Varsity, attacking hard
with rare combination, at last got an opening a
few moments off cease play, and the full time whistle
followed close upon their kick for goal. It was a great
and sensational struggle, won right on the verge of van-
quishment by 6 points to 4. Last year same two clubs
shared supremacs'. First round of this season’s compe-
tition saw Glebe inflict a heavy and unexpected defeat on
their latest conquerors, but previous week’s results
brought them level-pegging, Western Suburbs being next
—1 point to the bad.
• « • *
One-time Australian heavy champion bruiser,
Joe Goddard, said to have been shot in an election rough-
up at Punsanken (U.S.A.), was born in N.S.W. 41 years
ago, and v/as prominent as a pug. since ’B9, when he
squelched big Ned Ryan in three rounds. Prior to then,
Goddard figured occasionally at Saturday-night shows in
Foley’s old White Horse Gym., then situated just where
the George-st. entrance to the Strand (Sydney) Arcade is
now. Billed as the “ Illawarra Navvy,” he essayed to
stand before Peter Jackson for four rounds, but the big
black crushed him. Foley having retired, and Jackson
and Paddy Slavin gone abroad, he attracted attention as
a possible champion of the continent by smashing smiling
Charlie Dunn and Owen Sullivan, at Broken Hill, thus
earning the title of “ Barrier Champion.” Afterwards,
Goddard fought all through Australia, and then to
America, but came back badly wrecked after being
knocked out in 18 rounds by Yankee Ed. Smith. In ’94,
Maori Harry Lang (since dead) bested Goddard, at Mel-
bourne, in 12 rounds, which proved that the Barrier
champion had deteriorated considerably. Drifting
to S’Africa in the boxing boom, he made a pile
and then returned to America, where he never
showed anything like his old form. He sank down
to making matches with any and everyone any-
where, provided they “cut” the purse or “gate.” He
also tried beer-pumping, but that apparently didn’t
pay, as, less than a year since, he was heard of
figuring in a big six days’ go-as-you-please worry in
Philadelphia.
• * * •
Twelve years ago Goddard was 6ft. and 14st.
Absolutely devoid of skill, he relied mainly upon brute
force, grit and marvellous insensibility to punishment.
He could endure thumps like mule-kicks and still come in
on top of his man, which was how he beat the skilled
fighters. ’Murkan Choynski, among the heaviest hitters in
the biz., in Sydney 11 years ago, landed a fearful right on
Goddard’s jaw, between the ear and the chin, which lifted
him clean off his feet and sent him skidding along the
boards like a log ; it was the most terrific punch the
oldest inhabitant had heard of, yet Goddard came again
and stood further fearful battering, till nearing the end
of the third round the pair were fought to a standstill;
neither had a slug in him. Goddard won, Choynski,
limp and lame, crying enough at the close of the
fourth round. But Goddard’s most ferocious scrap
was with “ Mick ” Dooley, in June, 1900, at Sydney. For
21 rounds he roughed and mauled the very life out of his
2st. lighter opponent, who banged that beautiful left of
his home until the Barrier champion’s face—with eyes all
but closed and cheeks bruised and lumpy—resembled a
battered liver. But for the referee tolerating foul after
foul Goddard would undoubtedly have been beaten that
night. Writer remembers Joe offering to bet, and putting
up the money, that he would remain inert with hands at
his sides, and no man living could knock him out with
one punch on the point. No one undertook the task
under those conditions, but Joe lived to be knocked out—
with both hands up, too—by Denver Ed. Smith years
afterwards.
* * * •
Joe Goddard, the “rushing” pugilist, whose
death at the hands of a colored policeman in America is
reported, leaves a widow and family in South Melbourne.
Jack Marshall, the best known of Vic. trainers, says God-
dard was the strongest pug. that ever stood in a ring in
Australia, and believed that if his hands had not gone
back on him he would have come out a world’s champion.
Vic. papers credit Goddard with a victory over Frank
Slavin, but it. was Slavin’s inferior relation, Jack, whom
Joe beat at Yarra Glen, when Joe was a comparatively
green amateur. Goddard’s best exhibition was his battle
with Peter Jackson, ending in a draw after 20 rounds.
The referee (Prof. Miller) said that he declared a draw out
of regard for Jackson’s position—a rather curious verdict,
as Goddard was certainly points ahead. Goddard beat
Peter Maher, then pretty well up top in the States ; but
his hands broke up on Peter’s thick head, and were never
good for much after. One of Goddard’s tricks of strength
was throwing a cow over a three-railed fence at Fisher-
man’s Bend. He got his head under the animal, and,
taking a fore and hind leg in each hand, he hoisted and
over she went. Joseph earned a lot of money at the
game, but “did it in” like the rest of “the fancy."
Among his victims were Dooley, Owen Sullivan, Choyn-
ski, and Lees.
• » * •
On Monday night, 18th inst., “Young” Hig-
gins, brother of “Tot,” the Port Melb. pug, put ’em up
to Corbet, a little-known local with a flying right and an
able left, at the Lyceum Hall, and, although the house
was full of emptiness, the two fought one of the brightest
battles seen in the building. They were scrapping for
next to nothing, less expenses, but made it rabid from the
jump. Corbet proved himself an audacious fighter, with
cunning elbows and heaps of business. He is a baker by
trade, and trains on dough. Higgins stood over him, and
had a large natural surplus in the matter of weight, but
the smaller man was making a big reserve of points,
although Higgins looked good for another month, when
with the eighth round the fight was ended. No decision
was given. The two should draw a ship-load when they
meet again.
♦ ♦ ♦
Negro Tom Mitchell and white ex-waterman,
George Johns, drew the biggest house seen for years to
Sydney Gaiety A.C. last week. Money came freely for
Mitchell at 6 to 4 on, and the initial round made him look
a sure winner. Johns played into his hands by fighting
out from him, and the black pounded him heavily on face
and ribs. The second round was particularly sultry, both
men raining blows till they sat down, with the white man
best and the black’s left hand fractured. The next two
rounds were just as desperate, bub the fractured hand
told, and at the end of the fourtn the Mitchell crowd ele-
vated the wipe amid cursing and gnashing of backers’
teeth. Bub for the cracked slugger, Mitchell, on his
showing, might have won, although John’s work was
quite a revelation to even hardened old-timers.
* * * * .
Two well-known nigger pugs were divorced by
their wives at Melbourne last week.
* * * ♦
Sandow, when in Adelaide, kindly started to
organise firemen and police on his “ system.” The latter
were taken on first, and presented a delightful spectacle.
Preference being given to seniority in the force, the old
and portly men had to exhibit themselves while the
young and active looked on. The seniors were asked in
so many words to keep their “stomachs in,” but the
task was altogether beyond them, and by the time they
had, face downwards, raised themselves from the floor by
toes and finger-tips, the said stomachs came in very handy
for all but one or two of them to rest upon, completely
knocked out of time.
* • * *
The biggest road event yet run in the Southern
hemisphere is to be held in Victoria next Saturday. The
course is over the fine stretch of road between Warrnam-
bool and Melbourne—a distance of 165 miles. The enor-
mous acceptance of 118 riders has been received by the
Dunlop Tyre Co.—a record for a race of this description.
Representatives from every Commonwealth State, also
from Maoriland, have arrived in Melbourne to try and
win the great Australasian blue riband road event.
Littlechild (N.S.W.), Chalmers (M.L.), Hawley (Vic.) look
to have a good chance of carrying off the race, with
Ralston for fastest time prize.*
* * • *
The latest thing in bicycles is a steam-driven
Yankee motor-cycle, which, ridden by a man wearing
what looked like a diving helmet, cut out 51 miles per
hour over one of the few scraps of decent road there are
in the U.S. If that ever reaches Sydney’s
narrow, crooked streets, people who live near street cor-
ners will become quite accustomed to see a dead cyclist
and his machine come through the wall and sprawl across
the dinner table. What will happen to the people in the
street is too horrible to think of.
* * * *
Remarkable form, in England, of swimmer
“Dick” Cavill continues. Had he arrived earlier-
barring perhaps the five-mile race on the Thames—Aus-
tralia would probably have owned all England’s premier-
ships, which are the world’s. Cavill’s latest success is
the British Quarter-Mile Championship last week in, ’tis
cabled, 5.4 4-ssec.—a hair-raising speed feat. Previous
best was world’s professional champion “ Joey ” Nuttall’s
5.35 1-5; the prior amateur fastest (5.37 2-5) belonged to
“ Dick ” Cavill himself, and was accomplished at the
Australasian Championship meet at Wanganui (M.L.)
last February. As Australian George Read was second,
only 2yds. behind, he is evidently now well up to his best.
Sydney swimming enthusiast, Mr. George
Farmer, who returned from England t’other day, said
Read’s defeat in the mile occurred through sheer funk.
Three days before he did a trial that would have won the
race easily. Cavill had landed only four days previously.
Professional champion Nuttall is a side swimmer and the
finest in the world. With a wonderful stroke, crossing
and recrossing the legs with each single movement of the
arms, his progress doesn’t appear effort at all and is
motion always. The under arm isn’t brought right
through to its fullest extent as with Australians, but
directly it gets at right angles to the shoulder it is turned
upward quickly and shot out straight again, all the time
the top arm is sent in about level with the forehead and
swept through the water quickly. No time is wasted
recovering or bringing the power to bear. Australian
swimmers are often slow sending the arm forward, for the
stroke, and slower still forcing it through the water.
Nuttall is vigor from end to end of his work. Though
only perhaps 5.5 high he scales 12st. and has a 43in. chest
with underpins a picture of symmetry and muscle.
English amateur champ. Jarvis has Nuttall for trainer
and coach, and devotes his whole time to swimming ; is
big, awkwardly built, scales perhaps 13J stone and humps
a wealth of superfluous tissue about with him. Jarvis
affects a leg stroke which is a blend of Nuttalt’s and that
known in Sydney a few years ago as “ Gormly’s kick.”
• • • *
Fred Lane and Dick Cavill were first and
second in England’s furlong championship, former
winning by the touch only, in fresh world’s record
time of 2.28 2-5. Remarkable that the same pair
should finish in exactly the same manner as when
swimming for the 100yds. blue ribbon a week or
two before, when they also smashed all previous records.
Figures obliterated by Lane’s latest performance were
2.34 4-5, created by himself and the great English sprinter
Derbyshire, when they dead-heated for the English
220yds. championship at Birmingham, two years ago.
As showing the big advancement made over this distance
within the past decade, 10 years back Australian cham-
pion Walter Gormly swam the 220yds. in 2.38 J, and this
stood good till seven years later, when Lane covered the
trip, at Farmer’s Domain baths (Sydney) in 2.38 1-5. Now
we hear of it being done in little less than lOsec. better.
* * • *
Ferguson, who appropriated Victorian cross-
country individual supremacy on Miners’ Racecourse at
Ballarat, has been running four years ; his first public
effort landed him 19th in the 10-mile championship.
Following year he finished third, and last season was only
just beaten after a severe tussle with Stillwell. This year
Ferguson has come on further and made fastest times in
“ Anderson ” five-miles team and seven miles open races.

Premiership of N.S.W. cross-country champion-
ship, which eventuated on Kensington racecourse Satur-
day last, over s£m. and 35 jumps averaging 4ft., was won
by East Sydney—l 7 points better than West and 21 ahead
of South, last year’s holders. Individual supremacy fell
to S. W. York, verging on 30 years old, who scored his
third success (last two in succession), and in ’9B. Despite
heavy going, York got home with something to spare in
28.53—lOfsec. better than his time for ’Ol on same
ground.
• * • •
There is a possibility that, except for track-
racing, the pneumatic tyre is in its last days. The great
danger arising from liability to puncture has always been
a thorn in the side of the cyclist who goes forth in the
spring to kill himself before the advent of winter. Now
the inevitable Scotchman has come along with a simple
spiral-spring arrangement that can be punctured till the
tyre looks like a fish-skeleton and still run on serenely.
The new device consists of a number of spiral springs
placed between a solid-rubber tyre and a patent rim, and
has all the advantages of the pneumatic without any of
its numerous corresponding disadvantages.

Dear Bulletin.— lt is the fashion to insist
on a local Appeal Court and thus say good-bye to
the dear old Privy Council. Who would consti-
tute that Court ? The present leaders of the Bar,
or some of them, and if that be so, the following
record of P.C. decisions on appeals from N.S.W.
Supreme Court since 1895 is instructive
Gould v. Stewart, affirmed ; Attorney-General v. Rennie,
affirmed; Hunter Water Board v. Newcastle Council,
affirmed; Bank of N.S.W. v. Piper, overruled ; Hill v,
Dalgety, overruled; Attorney-General v. Walters, over-
ruled ; Garnsey v. Flood, affirmed ; Attorney-General v.
Love, affirmed; Colles v. Minister for Lands, affirmed ;
v Minister for Lands, overruled ; Croudace v. Yoebel,
affirmed ; Taxation Commissioner v. Teeee, affirmed ;
Broughton v. Commissioner for Stamps, affirmed ; Palmer
v. Moore, affirmed; Williams v. Papworth, affirmed;
Taxation Commissioner v. Kirk, overruled ; King v. Hen-
derson, affirmed ; Minister for Lands v. Wilson, over-
ruled ; Cook v. Ricketson, overruled ; Falkner v. M'Kin-
nery, overruled; Humble v. Humphreys, affirmed;
Young v. Waller, affirmed ; Edgar v. Plomley, overruled ;
William v. Scott, overruled ; Wentworth v. Wentworth,
overruled ; Taxation Com. v. Antill, overruled ; A.J.S.
Bank v. Bailey, overruled; Williams v. Railway Com.,
overruled ; Simpson v. Bank of N.Z., overruled ; Falk v.
Williams, affirmed ; Taxation Com. v. Municipality of
Broken Hill, overruled.
In 31 appeals N.S.W. Full Court was wrong 16
times and right 15 times —no one knows how often
it has been wrong in the cases in which the loser
did not appeal. In the very latest case, Taxation
Com. v. Antill (June, 1902), all three Judges were
“ quite clear ” and ‘ ‘ plain ” in deciding for Antill
—the Privy Council says that they are all wrong
—that the reputed case on which they based their
judgment had nothing to do with the point in
discussion, and that there was no analogy
between our Income Tax Act and the Eng-
lish. Falkner v. M'Kinnery is another very
instructive case where the Privy Council wiped
the floor with the Full Court. So is Hill v. Dal-
gety. Good old Privy Council! —Yours, &e., H.
[But why this Hinglish presumption that the
Privy Council is always right? The Bulletin
dares to think that, possibly, in some of
these cases in which that body “ wiped the floor ”
with Australian judges, it was wrong and Austra-
lian jurists right.]
At Poverty Point.
The preliminary advts. of the Mount Kembla benefit
concert at Sydney Town Hall didn’t please Amy Castles a
little bit. They represented that the “ Princess of Song ”
would appear by “ kind permission ” of J. C. Williamson,
whereas the lady was about to complete her engagement
with J.C.W. when she sang in Brisbane. The ads. and
paragraphs should have said that Princess Amy would be
graciously pleased to give her services to the good cause
on her royal own.
Melba has been getting “ fits ” from the critics for her
death-scene in “La Traviata.” The heroine dies of a
lingering consumption, and Patti used to play it, as a
peaceful end, in an invalid-chair. Melba prefers to take
it standing, drawing herself up to full height, and giving
the slashing “ back-fall ” of melodrama.
Dear Bulletin.— Re par. about Players’ Club being
mostly ex-professional. Outside Lytton, who was with
Brough and Hawtrey, the players in “ Weaker Sex ” were
purely amateur, nine out of 14 making their initial bow.
—L.
“ S.T.S.” : “ Edson ” claims (B. 28/6/’O2) that to give
any of Shakespeare’s big soliloquies means to drop the
actor for the elocutionist. True enough as a rule, but
the Waldorf-plus- 11 Ben Hur” crowd gave a pleasant ex-
ception (and to one of “ Edson’s ” most notable instances)
in “As You Like It.” H. H. Vincent, as Jaques, gave
the “ Seven Ages” speech sitting close to one wing, side-
ways to the audience, and looking at his fellow-players.
He spoke thoughtfully, as one who had all his ideas clear
enough, but had to arrange them in due order. And the
banished Duke listened attentively, nodding occasionally
with a smile, as who should say, “ Rather long-winded,
this, old boy ; but that was neatly put.” The gentlemen
of the suite gave a perfunctory attention, but clearly
thought the whole thing a bore ; and the faithful retainers
sat about at the back, eating apples from the lunch-table.
Everything just as it might have happened in “ real life’’
of the Forest of Arden.
Melbourne police, desirous of suppressing a peripatetic
suburban phonograph which, after wheezing out one or
two colorless selections, suddenly breaks into cheerful
profanity and cerulean stories, find themselves in a
quandary. The instrument itself is not an “ obscene pro-
duction,” and as a neuter object cannot, under present
Vic. laws, be held to have “ used obscene language,” no
prosecution has followed, and the naughty 'graph con-
tinues to do brisk biz. amongst depraved suburbanites.
The Wellington (M.L.) police recently hauled up a kine-
matograph showman for exhibiting allegedly suggestive
pictures, but the magistrate dismissed the case on the
grounds that there was very little azure about the sub-
jects, and that in any case the films were put through the
machine too rapidly to enable anybody to assemble a suf-
ficient number of sensibilities to be shocked. What
time the living-picture man was breasting the bar of
Social Gimletism, a meek and lowly showman was doing
the song-and-dance townships with no one to say him nay
or to drop Condy’s Fluid on his outfit. One of the items on
hisprogramme was a leg-show (literally) in which a number
of females (begged, borrowed, stolen or lost) ranged them-
selves along the front of the stage behind the curtain,
and lifted their skirts slightly (or considerably) while, to
the music of a clingsome waltz, the curtain was raised
about 18 inches or so. And then the showman, with a
wooden pointer (reminiscent of Zulu Thompson of blessed
memory) skipped along in front, requesting votes for
“ this pair, gents. ? ” “ And the next pair ? ” “ Now, what
do you say to these?” And so on, through the whole
gamut. Upon a show of hands the show of legs was
decided, and the happy possessor of the winning limbs
was awarded a silver watch. And all this in a country
where stockings are dried on an oven door, for fear the
baker’s boy may see them! Of course, in America it
would excite no comment. Such leg-shows are a familiar
feature of church bazaars in the land Geil comes from.
Old friend Teddy Royce, whom Australia remembers
chiefly for his Blueskin (Ri-too-ral) in “Jack Sheppard,”
was lately in danger of losing his eyesight, but an opera-
tion proved successful. Royce must be a tough little bit of
Bohemianism. When he came to Australia, 16 years ago,
he was a mere wreck of the original Royce who had
played in Gaiety burlesques with Nellie Farren and
Edward Terry prior to being laid low by paralysis. At
that time friends said of the little man that it had seemed
impossible for him to ever reappear on the stage. Such
resurrections never happen to big, beefy men.
Australian musical critics who babble pityingly of the
old Italian operas as “ sugary ” relics of an absurd
musical past, must be shocked to hear that Donizetti was
considerably on the bill at Oovent Garden this season.
“Lucia ” had a revival as usual, also “ L’Elisire D’Amore,"
one of the very sweetest of Donizetti’s operas, which has
been rarely heard in London since it was sung in English
by the Louisa Payne 00. ever so long ago.
Hilda Spong, now an actress of considerable importance
in America, is said to be prettier than ever. Some years
ago, when tall Hilda was leading lady in the Bland Holt
00., she threatened to grow too stout for heroine parts
before she was 30. But America is a great country for
girls who incline to be embonpoint. The various climates,
or the everlasting iced-water, or something must have an
anti-fatic influence.
Quite likely that George Rignold will be offered an
engagement by one of the managers when they find him
roaming at large in the theatrical world of the Big Smoke.
His family name carries weight in London, and
“ Handsome George ” retains the reputation of having
been the “star” Rignold in days of yore.
Dan. Barry, begorra ! introduces the “ latest American
cake-walk ” into the revels of the plantation slaves when
he stages “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin ” nowadays. One of these
days Dan will set the villain on a motor-car to chase
George Harris and Eliza.
Julius Knight, whose padded Superbus legs still live in
the memory of the Australian girl, has been unlucky with
his occasional London appearances. T’other week he
scored favorable press notice for his acting in a play—
“ The Queen of the Roses”—which fell flat. Miss Beryl
Faber also got praise for her share in the ill-fated show.

Colonel Tom Price is making things hum
In Brisbane military circles. Inspecting the last
Q. returned cohentingenters, he exhorted them not
to fill themselves up with cheap beer until they
imagined they were “returned heroes.” And
then, in a loud voice, he asked Lt.-Col. Chauvel
what he Was keeping the men on parade for ! The
startled colonel precipitately dismissed them.
An amusing feature of Vic. politics just
bow is the assiduous support of Irvine’s Govern-
ment by Smiler Peacock. That wily bird’s
criticism of Treasurer Shiels’ advance budget was
full of honeyed references to various real and
imaginary difficulties with which his brother, the
Raven, is confronted. At the same time, Peacock
is playing a risky game if he really doesn’t want
to be hustled out of his job as Opposition leader.
It is very well to let the other fellow get the
gruelling while pretending to assist him in the
dirty work, but many of Peacock’s supporters,
being new hands and not up to the tricks of the
political trade, already begin to growl. And now
that Outtrim is reconciled to his old party, it 1
won’t be long ere he makes capital out of such
discontent. Has the Smiler sealed his doom with
a knighthood ?
Premier Lewis’s scheme for putting him-
self and the dog in the same kennel—or for
putting the Council and the Assembly in the same
room while retaining them as separate institu-
tions—was defeated by 18 to 15. One of its
opponents, Lyne, voted against it because, he
said, there was no such thing as a Legislature of
one Chamber in the British empire, and he didn’t
want to get away from the safe paths of precedent.
The Tas. legislator, on a steady average, is
probably the most ignorant in Australasia, and
nobody made a serious effort to enlighten Lyne.
Premier Lewis now proposes to have a Leg.
Council of 12 members, and an Assembly of 24, so
he is back in the old hole. One notable feature is
that Lewis’s new ‘ ‘ Reform ” Bill says nothing
about paying any salary to members. This looks
like an attempt to destroy almost the only demo-
cratic idea Tasmania possesses, by accidental
omission.
Judge Dash wood, who was appointed to
investigate the pearl-shelling industry, reports
that in 1901 the pearl-shell landed at the two
great stations, Thursday Island and Port Dar-
win, was worth £122,571, and that the indus-
try employed 85 white people and 2420 imported
mgg'ers. It is a solemn fact that two average-
sized N.S.W. or Victorian boot-factories employ
as many white workers as all the great pearling
industry—there are 94 boot-factories in N.S.W.
employing an average of 42 people each. And if
Queensland would set to work and grow its own
wheat supply, it would save more in the way of
money paid for wheat imports than it makes out
of all the pearl-shell landed by all the 2138 niggers
who drink and curse and jabber at Thursday’s
pleasant isle.
“ Hermes ” : The young ladies who waved
the street collection-boxes for the Kembla fund
were a little too modest to be a huge financial suc-
cess, and I was considerably surprised to see. that
£1008 was raked in. Still, there were exceptions ;
some of the ladies in nursing uniform were par-
ticularly fleet. I had successfully navigated a
shoal of five, when a flowing cloak swooped down,
and, as the box rattled under my nose, I noted the
scars and moraines of glacial middle age. I pro-
duced a thrummer. “ Make it a shilling and 111
P've you a kiss,” said the lady. I hesitated—then
. kissed her for sweet Charity’s sake, and dropped
sixpence into the aperture. The occasion de-
manded the sacrifice.
From an Australian Star interview with
Premier John See (25/8/’02):
The State Government, he went on to say, would only
be able to pay back the duty to the importer —not to
remit it, because it must be paid—under an Act of Par-
liament, which would have to be passed. He did not
think a refund of the amount of duty paid would afford
any relief to the squatters. If the Federal Government
had seen its way clear to grant a remission of the duty,
he would have been very pleased ; as they had not, he
could do nothing further in that direction.
John See admits that the State Government can
refund the fodder duty (as it gets the duty from
the Federal Government at the division of the
surplus it certainly can refund it), but he flatly
refuses to part with a copper of said duty for
the benefit of any distressed pastoralists. And
—this is where the mystery comes in—John,
though he claims that it would help the squatter
if he hadn’t to pay the duty at all, alleges that it
wouldn’t help him in the least to get the duty re-
funded after he had paid it. Why that should be
so Heaven only knows, but John See says it is so.
The great Adelaide divorce case of Hall y.
Hall, with rev. preacher Wilson badly involved in
the background, came to a sudden but logical
close last Monday. It had gone on for many
days, and seemed likely to go on for many more
days. Costs were mounting up like an Alp; they
had already mounted up so high that you could
hardly see to the top of Costs. Gentleman One
was getting along beautifully; also Gentlemen
Two, Three, Four, Five and Six. And then, just
as the defence was fairly started, Judge Boucaut
asked if it had any evidence to refute certain of
the charges on the other side ; if it couldn’t refute
these particular charges, His Honor said, they
alone would settle the case. Defence admitted
that it couldn’t refute these charges. Judgment
for petitioner. Case stopped. Costs stopped.
Nothing left for a shocked lawyer to do but gather
up his bag and go.
N.S.W. public service was badly scared
one day last week, when the dailies simul-
taneously stated that the See Government con-
templated a drastic scheme of percentage reduction
on all salaries over £150. But this proved to be
premature, the Premier announcing that strict
economy was to be enforced, and that while no
salary-increases would be granted, no reductions
were yet contemplated. To show how careful the
Govt, would be in future, the Assembly next day
sanctioned the introduction of Bills deeming it
expedient to build a bridge over the Hawkesbury,
a railway line from Cobar to Wilcannia, another
from Bogan Gate to Bulbodney (wherever that
is); the Tuckian flood escape scheme (the Hunter
River is nearly dry at present), and one or two
minor matters which will throw a few more thou-
sands on to the loan votes; while at the back of
the D.T.’s issue intimating the threatened reduc-
tion appeared an elaborate design for the intended
North Shore bridge, which, as it would cost only
about £1,780,000, was hardly considered “ class
enough.
Melb. Age adds a further howl to the
Victorian “stinking-fish” cry by declaring that
the State deficit will be £800,000, instead of the
£450,000 estimated by the Treasury officers, and
the £650,000 put down by Treas. Shiels as his out-
side calculation. This persistent and well-organ-
ised declaration of public insolvency, when it
comes from such sources as the Melb. morning
journals and the Irvine-McKenzie-Robert Reid
crowd, obviously has some deep-laid motive be-
hind it, and there are weird rumors in the southern
city just now of a Flinders-lane and Collins-_st.
conspiracy to cut wages on the one hand and raise
prices on the other. Also, the 48 Rich Landlords
who veto Vic. legislation are holding back the
Factories Act, and when the time comes for
settling the matter one way or another, will point
to the general diminution of wages outside the
provisions of that measure and will insist that
“in the awful condition of the finances and the
general stagnation of trade,” it is the duty of the
luxurious tailoress and the overpaid fellmonger to
go back to the old sweating terms.

rPHE company is being formed on non-tariff lines for the purpose of carrying on Fire, Marine, and General Insurance
■*" Business (excluding Life) in New South Wales and the other States of the Commonwealth, as the Directors may
deem advisable.
Assurances have been given of substantial support, and it is confidently expected that the Company will
immediately secure a considerable business of a sound and profitable nature.
It is proposed to gradually cultivate a high-class Insurance connection on safe lines. The Management will be in
capable hands, and the best expert knowledge obtainable will be employed in launching the Company, and putting it
on a sound business footing from the outset.
The aim of the Directors in the management will be care and economy ; the latter as far as it is compatible with
efficiency and the most rigid and exacting inspection of risks.
Experience has demonstrated on average results that there is no safer or more profitable investment than the
shares of a prudently-managed Insurance Company. With first-class securities in this market at figures so high it is
difficult to see where f?bsh funds can be placed to advantage—such an advantage is now placed before the nublic in
the COMMONWEALTH MUTUAL INSURANCE COMPANY, LIMITED.
There is ample room for this Company. The plan has been well thought out, and, without doubt, the formation
of a Non-tariff Company will meet a want which, of recent years, has been growing more apparent.
In event of the Company not being formed, the application money will be returned in full.

WHAT THE WORLD’S SCIENTISTS SAY.
THAT half of the nervous affections from which people suffer arise through imperfect and insufficient nerve force. Rob the body of animal magnetism, and paralysis and dis-
order of the chief functions follow. Pluck a flower—it withers. The principle is the same. It is one which involves Death.
Physicians of the widest experience and highest attainment declare that where nervous disorders arise from structural changes in the great nerve centres, the treatment
which lends itself with direct adaptability and possessing most curative results is that of Electricity combined with medical treatment. Many eminent professional medical experts
assert this fact—that where nervous exhaustion and functional disorders are present, symptoms show themselves in sleeplessne s, great muscular debility, headache, backache, mental
incapacity, loss of appetite with dyspepsia, and constipation, weariness or a condition of weariness and unrest. The intei by and variety of these symptoms indicate how greatly
the system is affected.
Electricity adopted judiciously with restorative medicines have powerful reconstructive influences. An electric ”J. -vigorator ” worn around the loins for a few hours
nightly will effect a great and lasting change by pouring into the nerves that necessary vitality which being absent forms the nucifis of disease.

The office of Under-Secretary for Mines in
N.S.W. has now been vacant for three or four months.
It is an open .secret that there has been a battle royal in
and out of the Cabinet over the filling of the office and
that the “ envy, hatred, malice and all uncharitableness ”
of Yellow and Green animosities have been most pro-
nouncedly in evidence. If Premier See had any real
backbone he would defy priest and parson and promptly
appoint the man whom he considered best qualified.
* * * *
Report of Metropolitan Coal Co., held in
London, reminds N.S.W. that her absolutely most
valuable asset, coal, is exploited almost wholly on behalf
.of foreign capitalists. The Metropolitan co. (whose pit is
at Helensburgh, on the South Coast) earned last year
£22,172, the greater part of which goes abroad. At a
rough estimate three-fourths of N.S.W. colliery scrip is
held abroad by English and Scotch capitalists. The A. A.
Co., which besides being the biggest coal-owner in-
N.S.W., is also the biggest land monopolist, is almost
purely a British institution.
• * • •
“Jimmy 8.” : Particulars to hand of an explo-
sion at a Welsh colliery, whereby 8 men were killed,
strongly suggests an amendment to N.S.W. Coal Mines
Regulation Act, renderingcompulsory the adequate water-
ing of all dry and dusty mines before shot-firing. The
jury found that “deceased persons met with their death
from an explosion of coal-dust caused by shot-firing, the
place where the shot took place being insufficiently
watered at the time.” There is always the possibility of
a small local explosion of gas developing, through the
presence of coal-dust, into a general explosion. N.S.W.
Act clearly recognises this danger. Rule 12 section d
forbids the miner to use or have possession of any iron or
steel (liable to strike fire) pricker, tamper, stemmer or
scraper (tools used in blasting operations), or to stem a
hole with coal-dust or dry coal. One would naturally
think that watering as an explosion-deterrent would
promptly be adopted by a practical mine-manager. But
evidence, at an enquiry into a recent mining disaster in
Newcastle district, showed that the return air from a
district worked with safety lamps actually passed over a
stow-bord in which men were working with naked lights.
That forces the conclusion that many a precaution would
readily commend itself if the company were held directly
responsible for all “ accidents.” Failing that, it can
hardly be considered iniquitous to place the proprietor on
a somewhat similar footing to the miner in the rule
quoted.
* * • •
When Adelaide heard, the other day, of the
finding of a 12ft. coal-seam in the far north she felt
another flutter ; but the wet blanket, which she knows so
well, soon came in the shape of further news that the find
was almost as much a pyrites-bed as a coal-seam.
Adelaide knows that she can’t expect coal to be hauled
from up there to compete with Newcastle in the
city ; but she knows that it would help on the lon»--
dreamt-of transcontinental line if she could find that
Nature ahead and fixed up a coal depot midway.
Rutherglen (Vic.) co-operative parties are up in
arms against the enforcement of the law which provides
that working shafts must have proper ladderways. They
argue that there is no necessity for ladders—that the man
who can’t haul himself up a 150 ft. shaft hand-over-hand
on a quarter-inch wire-rope has no business to go below.
But the law says ladders are necessary to guard against
accidents, and can’t take into account the eagerness of
men, perhaps forced by poverty, to risk their lives for
their bread. At Clear Creek, near Bathurst, a field run
by small parties, the mills have been idle for some time
for want of water. Last week rain fell and all the bat-
teries started, when an inspector appeared on the scene,
and ordered the immediate cessation of work because
none of the drivers held certificates. The miners com-
plain, arid state that in Bathurst, or any other town,
there will be found drivers of mill engines, sawmills, and
factory plants without certificates. But one wrong can’t
be righted by adding another wrong. No untrained man
should be allowed to fool with a boiler, on a mine or any-
where else, even if he is readv to take the risk.
* * * *
“H.G.”: One-half the annual deficit of £365,000
in Vic. Railway Dept, is represented by the difference in
price between coal in N.S.W. and the same coal in Vic.,
resultant upon the excellent organisation of the shipping
ring. This has been going on for years, and, apparently,
neither the officers of the Dept, nor any Minister of Rail-
ways has regarded the matter as curable. A smart,
energetic, fighting Commissioner like the late Eddy, of
N.S.W., would have settled the matter within a month.
The cost of the purchase and equipment of a big collier,
run by a crew under the Department itself, would be
more than repaid in the first three months of its service.
This is saying nothing of the possibility of Vic. Govern-
ment buying up a handy N.S.W. colliery, and thus pro-
viding its own coal supply, if it can’t get it in Victoria.
* • • *
The first person to be fined under the Truck
Act of N.S.W. was Newman, M.P. for Lucknow. He runs
the local mine, and also the local store, and was fined
because he wrote to a miner: “ I cannot afford to let you
run any further (he owed £4), and if you cannot pay for
your tucker you had better get your time and find
another place.” The miner resented this intimation, and
was shortly afterwards dismissed, for, as alleged by
Newman, insubordination in declining to work in a
certain part of the mine.
• * * •'
A Bill now before Maoriland Parliament, to
have the 8-hours-day reckoned from “bank to bank”—
i.e., from the time the miner enters the mine until he
returns to the surface—is being hotly opposed by the
mining co’s, who are lobbying with much energy. The
measure, however, seems in a fair way to become law,
although certain Parliamentary rail-sitters would like to
vote both ways.
* • * *
Value of Maoriland’s gold and silver output for
last year was £1,819,041, an increase of £340,560 on pre-
vious year’s output. Though this is the largest return
since ’7l, the number of working miners steadily
declines, there being some 800 less than in the
year immediately preceding. As larger amounts of
capital are being spent on development, the miner
becomes less important.
* • * •
A Westralian Mine-owners’ Association has
been formed in London, and the executive committee of
15 includes many notorious market manipulators.
Whitaker Wright and Horatio Bottomley are conspicuous
by their absence—the list seems incomplete without
them. In a vein of unconscious humor a London journal
says: “ So influential an assembly ought to do much to
purge the Westralian field of the conspiracies and evil
machinations which have characterised it in the past."
* * • •
N.Q. (more particularly Towers’) people, not
satisfied with the good things which they have in their
own districts—many languishing for want of proper
attention —have embarked on a dredging enterprise on
the Rio D’Oro, in Brazil. They have spent £16,000 in
acquiring concessions from the Brazilian Govt, and erect-
ing a dredge. The latter is to start work next month.
60,000 shares in the co. said to be worth 21s. each in the
market. If the reports as to.the prospects of the dredg-
ing area are to be regarded in the same light as the
report as to the market value of the shares, writer
wouldn’t give much for the show. The Q. man who
would not jump at a return of £6O On £l6 on the eve of
producing operations being started must belong to a
different class from those who are associated with local
mining. The wise Queenslander knows that, as a rule,
it is a sensible thing for him to talk himself out of his
stock —if he can do so at a profit—before the battery
becins to talk. It can tell no lies.
' * • * .*
The luck of the miner. Some months ago an
explosion in the Vesuvius (O. Towers) shaft resulted in
the death of one man and severe injuries to two others.
One of the latter, W. White, during his convalescence,
occupied himself with trenching his garden on Moonstone
Flat and struck a reef. Result for 16 weeks’ work, 520 z.
smelted gold, which, with sand and sludge, gave him
something like £l2 per week.
• • • *
Lake View manager reports that “ a cheaper
system of stoping is being started, and a considerable
saving in explosives and labor may be looked for.” This
statement, writes a correspondent, is rather interesting,
considering that in some of the L.V. stopes they are in
the habit of shooting the ore down on the cyanide sands
used for filling. The shovellers dig the ore out and send
it to the surface, accompanied with a considerable portion
of sands. This is what they call a “ cheaper system of
stoping.”
The north end of Kalgoorlie field, from which
so much was expected by sanguine exploiters, is sick
unto death. Hannan’s North, Hannan’s Reward, Golden
Zone, Reefers Eureka, and many others are either defunct,
or else handed over to tributors’ bent on rooting out the
scanty chutes of pay-ore which exist in some of these
shows.
* * * *
Not satisfied with existing processes, L. Y. Con-
sols’ management is about to try the Elmore oil process
on concentration of the sulpho-telluride ores. A plant,
capable of treating 50 tons daily, is to be installed without
loss of time. This new departure is a nasty knock for the
much-vaunted Diehl process, which superseded the fur-
nace plant on the co.’s mine and was boomed by its spon-
sors, Bewick Moreingand Co., as quite irreproachable.
• * * »
The water trouble in Cosmopolitan, W.A., is
still acute, despite all denials. At No. 5 level 400,000
gals, a day are making, and it is impossible to make much
headway in consequence. Of course, the difficulty will
sooner or later be surmounted, but meanwhile mill is
rapidly overhauling developments and ore reserves are
being steadily depleted. Mine is being very efficiently
equipped, and will make a big splash once things are
properly fixed up.
• • • •
An English paper, taking for its text, “Ignor-
ance is always expensive,” cites this as a case in point:
“ For long past the N.S.W. public works have-been en-
gaged in dredging out the harbors at the mouths of the
Moruya and Shoalhaven rivers. Incidentally they have
also been employed in the expensive operation of dump-
ing thousands of pounds worth of gold into the sea. The
workmen made the discovery that the sand they were
dredging up was rich in gold, so rich, indeed, that by
running the stuff through an automatic gold-saver it is
certain that the gold recovered will more than pay all the
expense of the harbor-making operations.” The Owe’S.,
so far, has refrained from informing the public that he is
carrying out one public work which is directly remunera-
tive. Who will say now that he is not modest ?
