Cabled that recent discoveries show that the
main series of the Witwatersrand reefs extends 30 miles
to the eastward of the hitherto-known boundary. Also
that the whole area had been secured under option by a
“ powerful syndicate.” Thirty miles of it! Anyhow, the
Transvaal was well worth lying for, and (in the name of
God and Liberty) murdering other people to grab.
• * # *
Bathurst (N.S.W.) has been gloating over a
“ pile ” of water-worn nuggets, weighing 620 z., discovered
in a patch at the old Ophir diggings. The sleepy city
was further galvanised by a report that a marvellously
rich reef had been struck at Native Dog Greek—it was so
rich that the miners’ picks stuck in the gold. A faithful
scribe visited the spot, and reported that he would be a
hard-working miner who would make tucker there.
Bathurst is once more asleep.
* • • •
Briseis yearly meeting in London was a happy-
family affair. No growling. All compliments. Expecta-
tions high. Report showed that the water-race cost
£52,000, or £30,000 over the estimate. Supply of water,
1,200,000ga1. per hour. Engineer estimates, as the result
of extensive prospecting work, that the value of tin on
the property is £2,000,000 above original estimate.
“ Little ” Langford was at the meeting in all his glory.
He is a marvellous worker, who has more electrical
force in him than the average man of twice his
physical proportions. He made a magniloquent
speech, which sent all the shareholders away happy
in the _ belief that they were associated with an
enterprise which has all the essential elements for a
big success. The walls of the room were covered with
splendid photos, of the mine and works, touching which
and other matters a correspondent writes : “ Everything
being above-ground, a mine like this lends itself to be
well illustrated by photographs. lam quite sure there
was never another case where London shareholders could
so well see their mine so many thousands of miles away
as they could in this instance. It was a great idea, and
Langford deserves credit for it. The shares are likely to
go much higher before the end of the year. Despite' the
world-wide mining depreciation, these scrip have held
their own, and, but for the collapse in S’Africans and the
condition of things in Australia, they would be at least
25 per cent, higher to-day.” Any material appreciation
in the value of Briseis shares will have to come from
actual results, and these will soon have to be forthcoming,
to maintain their present prices.
The Drought.
Writes “ 11. F.” :
Thh Bulletin is doubly right re the tacit conspiracy of
the daily press to minimise the drought. Few Sydney
residents even commence to understand what is happen-
ing. London Times correspondent apparently wired
part of the truth, only to be contradicted by poli«
tical Falstaffs, Barton and Copeland. The poai*
tion in N.S.W. is this : After a series of dry
seasons, in which the subsoil hardened to rock and the
creeks dried up as never before within the memory of
living men, the just-past 12 months followed without one
soaking rain. In many places three following crops have
failed, and it is now impossible to plant a fourth. Last
summer, paddocks that had been carefully saved as a
stand-by had all the long, dry grass burnt by bush fires.
The ground is as black now after seven months as though
the fire was of yesterday. Hay, straw, and lucerne stacks,
saved by the thrifty fora possible dry time, have gone long
ago. For months past the railways have been at their
wits’ end to find trucks for the produce that is being
rushed from town to country. All this time the stock is
dying, and when water is to be had, death is slow. The
hair becomes harsh and rough ; a range of sharp hills rises
along the back while gullies and ridges form down the
flanks ; there are two empty craters over the hips. Day after
day the body contracts, till, a living mummy, it is but a
few inches thick, the head appearing wide out of all pro-
portion. The beast makes no moan or groan, but stands
about moveless, hours at a time. It is well not to look at
their eyes, for there is a look there at times that will
haunt you. The hides of starved stock are very light,
and the tanners give little for them. The poor owners
were the lucky men, their stock died nine months ago ;
the more well-to-do have been using the last shilling of
money and credit only to reach the same end after long-
drawn agony. They started hand-feeding bravely and
with light hearts—“ There must be rain soon ! ” but, one
by one, as time has passed and the price of feed risen,
they have thrown up the game like bluffed card-players.
Probably two-thirds of our stock is dead, this, in many
cases, after more than their value has been spent in feed.
Only a few more dry weeks, a little further rise in fodder,
andN.S.W., away from the coast-line, will be a desolation
without hoof or horn—a desert with only empty buildings
to mark where homesteads stood. The'average bushie in
ordinarily bad years is kept going by the storekeeper, who
is backed Jby the merchant, who is backed by the bank.
Of late this process has gone far past the limit of safety ;
and, finally, the word has passed round, falling like the
first earth on the coffin of Hope—“ No more credit! ”
“ You refuse me food for my starving family ; you ask me
for cash when you know I have not had a bean for a
twelvemonth? What shall I do?” That is the question
of thousands of cookies this day. As for the storekeepers,
they are grey and haggard ; those with the hearts of men
must soon be tramping Sydney streets. After all else are
dead, the absorbing effort is to keep the working horses
alive—the last shilling, the last bite of feed is saved for
them ; carting taken at unheard of prices if only they can
be kept alive. The bush is ranged for a chance wallaby
to fill the pot; yet they are so scarce, so weak and thin
when shot as to be hardly worth carriage. Though the
farm is idle, there is plenty to do ; five, six, and 10 miles
the dray and tank have to go daily for water, join
the file of waiting carts, and take turn at the pump.
The old bush and orchard trees are dead, only
saplings and late-planted have life, and they are wilting.
The edible scrub is gone, and the bare ground littered
with the trees cut down long ago to tempt stock to eat
them. Box trees are ring-barked by horses as high as
their teeth would reach. Such is the state of the country
40 miles from Sydney—a state described by travellers
as simply luxuriant compared with “Wayback” and
“ Furtherback.” And our fat representatives in London
say : “ The evils of the drought are grossly exaggerated.”
And this is the story of our great “ natural indus-
tries ” —the ones the Freetrade party bids us stick
to and rely upon, instead of going in for manu-
factures vvhich don’t collapse every dry year. The
hand-fed iron and steel foundry looks more pro-
mising than the hand-fed sheep, and the bonused
woollen mill or hat factory seems much more
hopeful than the bonused jumbuck.

PEARS
Atlas.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Long since, out of high Olympus,
Through gleaming gulfs of air,
Like a wild, white star shot downward,
Hermes, the Messenger.
As he flew, the skies around him,
Like a flag of stars, were furled—
Till he came to where strong Atlas
Upheld the heavy World.
The great, broad-shouldered giant
Strode darkly on his road,
But ever to Zeus made outcry
To rid him of his load.
Quoth Hermes, laughing lightly :
“Thou soon shalt take thine ease—
The Gods’ Fool I have-brought thee,
The Strong Man Hercules !
“ And he shall bear thy burden,
And he thy yoke shall wear;
And that he throw off neither
Henceforth shall be thy care.’*
Then Hercules the Worker,
With lip of pride upcurled,
Took, smiling, on his shoulders
The burden of the world.
But Atlas sprang upon it,
And, with triumphant air,
Cried “Fool of the Gods, ’tis written
That me too thou shalt bear ! ”
From Pole to Pole his body
Lay stretched at godlike ease ;
His arms clasped the Equator,
His feet were in the seas.
He laughed a laugh Titanic—
“ O Hermes, I have won
A sturdy Beast of Burden
To bear me round the sun 1
“ O Hermes, I shall ride him
With iron bit and rein—
And lest he should prove restive
I will chain him with a chain.”
And Hercules the Worker
With groans the burden bore ;
But the more he groaned and murmured
Proud Atlas laughed the more.
And, if he shook down Empires
In throes of angry pain,
With bolt and rivet, Atlas
Straight fastened them again.
And round the sun for scons,
The world, with all its zones,
Rolled to a dreadful music
Of laughter and of groans.
Then came a small sweet spirit,
To Hercules said she—
“ Take heart again, sad Giant,
My name is Liberty.”
And age by age she labored,
By days and nights she wrought
With rasp and file of Knowledge,
And acid keen of Thought.
“ Thy chains are nearly severed,
The day draws nigh,” said she,
“ When, with one wrench convulsive,
Thou shalt once more be free.” ;
The day came—and the God’s Fool
With eyes far-shining trod
Upon the earth—his burden
Of old—a demigod.
Ye say this is a Fable—
Too long the Hour doth wait —
Ye fools and blind—this moment
’Tis knocking at the gate.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
The Burial of Mrs. Moggs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Impressive? Oh Yes! Mister, funerals at sea,
is very. I was a mourner at one, and this is how
it came about.
“We was taking out two passengers;
Mr. Moggs, an old gent, as had made a pile
of stuff in the colonies, and an old girl as he said
was his mother. He was a bachelor and had come
home, a’ purpose to fetch her out to keep house
for him. Both of ’em was religious. Mr. Moggs,
as he told us himself, was a reformed character.
He promised all hands hell-fire an’ damnation
every other day, as if he reckoned on running that
part of hereafter on his own. But the old woman
was the two ends and the bight of a tangled
nuisance—always paddling away for’ed to the
galley, for hot water or something; she’d bump
up agen a chap just as he’d be letting go a cuss-
word, when she’d open out on him, something
awful. Besides she used to hang her nasty little
bits of washing all over the after-end of us—always
taking the running-gear for it too. We’d be
puttin’ the ship about, maybe, 1 Mainsail aul an
the hands a-bursting of theirselves on the weather-
braces— a-pully-hauling their arms out, without
getting an inch—one of Mrs. Moggs’s flannel
petticoats bein’ jammed hard and fast in a block to
leeward. it weren t petticoats it was
stockin’s or some such female parcellin. She
was a regular Jonahess; we’d nothing but foul
winds and greasy calms as long as she was aboard
of us. , ,
“We’d been beaclmed for a week, somewhere
down about the Line. A blue shark—the biggest
as ever I’ve seen—had been bangin’ round under
the counter for days. Mrs. Moggs wouldn’t hear
of the old man trying to catch him, as she said it
was sinful to destroy one of God s critturs, and
that afternoon she was a decoratin’ of us up as
usual, when she over-reached herself and jumped
into the water just ahead of the shark. You may
bet there was no ‘beg pardons’ or ‘by your leave,
mum!’ with him— he just rolled over and
swallowed her in two bites. The skipper an her
son was on the poop at the time, and Mr. Moggs
was terrible put-about, to think that his mother
should go so sudden-like, after her paying her
passage and although what troubled him more,
he said, was her having such an unchristian
burial. So him and the Cap’en makes it up to
catch that there shark.
“The hook was baited with a junk o’ pork and
hung over the stein, an’ Mr. Moggs he spent all
that afternoon praying hard and chuckin’ small
lumps of salt-pig astern to entice the brute; but,
as the mate said, ‘ the old lady, like enough, was
powerful hard to digest, an’ the shark wasn’t
hungry yet.’ The third day the Cap’en has him
on tne hook; we gets a bowline round him and
he’s yanked inboard. Tho cook opens him up —
careful—but, though there was evidence of Mrs.
Moggs having been there, there wasn’t much of
her left.
“The old man ordered all that was there to
be put in a bucket, along of a cuttle-fish or two, a
couple of beef-bones, and a condensed-milk tin,
which was so mixed up together that we couldn’t
well sort ’em out. All hands cleaned theirselves,
and when the bell for’ed was tolled, laid aft on to
the half-deck, standin’ solemn and sorrowful,
whilst the Cap’en read tho burial service. One
young Cockney-chap among us starts a grin, but
when he catches the chief mourner a-eyein’ him he
tops the snigger off with a sob—the most of us
sobs then, and Mr. Moggs seemed powerful
pleased. The bosun he sat on tho t’ gallant-rail
with the bucket, under the Union Jack, and,
when the old man gives him the wink, drops
the remains and other things over the sido.
Mr. Moggs hung over the rail for a good spell,
watching the milk-tin and beef-bones zig-zag
down out of sight, for fear another shark might
come along and bury his mother again. And ho
gets grog served out to us, plentiful, that day, for
he said ‘ the amount of sympathy shown by tho
crew ’ did us credit and ‘was most gratifying to
his feelings. ’ And from that out we mado a record
passage ; so you see, mister, she must' have been
a Jonahess ! ”
m. l.
F. MARRYAT NORHIS.
Tales of The Penance Track.
A RUN OF BAD LUCK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
It was a run of bad luck for No. 23. He and
his brother had got two years each for horse-
stealing. The brother was delicate, and was
received into gaol hospital, from which he sent
word asking 23 to send him a “ chew.” No. 23 was
a painter, and his workshop looked out on the
back-gate of the hospital, so, after “muster,” he
dexterously avoided the eye of Warder Lanigan,
and slipped “a quarter” under the gate, knowing
that the tobacco-hungry consumptive would be
waiting on the other side. This done, he cheer-
fully proceeded with his business. But Warder
Goodman, from the balcony of the workshops
overlooking the lane, had seen the “move.” No.
23 was promptly loeked-up, awaiting the arrival of
the visiting “beak.”
The spirit of generosity is promptly strangled in
gaol. A prisoner caught in the act of distributing
any surplus “indulgence” of tobacco,
4ozs. of tea, and lib. of brown sugar, which
he receives weekly, after a year’s servitude)
is placed in “lock-up cells” and charged with
“trafficking.” The fact of receiving no return
does nothing to invalidate the charge. .Money
value for unused “indulgence” will be given by
the Government, but philanthropy is a crime,
punishable under a special rule.
The day prior to No. 23’s trial for breach of
prison discipline, all the poultry belonging to the
visiting magistrate died sudden and violent deaths
from poison. So 23 faced a soured magistrate and
got seven days dark cells.
A medical student once “doing time” promul-
gated a theory that occupation for the mind during
dark-cell treatment could always be obtained by
tossing a button into the black, inky darkness,
and then getting down on hands and knees and
searching for it. The device has served to pre-
serve the mental balance of many who otherwise
might have gone “queer” under the treatment.
No. 23 thus spent six days busily employed.
Hundreds of times the button was found and tossed
up again and again, the hunter joyfully finding
after groping on hands and knees over the cell.
On the morning of the seventh day, the button ,
after being thrown up, did not return again.
Thirty-six times the hunter crawled over the
floor—no button. Now, imagination lies in the
pit of the stomach, and bread and water are hardly
a sufficient weight to put upon it, so No. 23
started to think things. He rushed the cell door
and kicked, but two doors were between him and
Warder Williamson (who was a trifle deaf), so no
good resulted. Then someone laughed in the
corner of the dark cell, and it was all up. No. 23
kicked the door, upset the water-pot, and
shrieked ; the waves of curse-encrusted darkness
welled up from the corners and smothered him.
Invisible hands, clammy with horror-sweat,
grasped at his throat and choked him. When
Warder Williamson called him into the corridor
for Governor’s inspection it was too late.
* » * *
Months afterwards, when an enlightened prison
official suggested the abolition of dark cells as a
punishment, and they were converted into light
punishment cells, a sweeper found in “No. 17
Dark Cell” a trouser-button, securely fastened
in a cobweb near the ceiling. In the criminal
ward, Kenmore Lunatic Asylum, a prisoner is
still hunting for that button.

THE WASP.
William Naismith, author of “ A Young
Draper’s Guide to Success,” recently published in
London, is a fine sample of the young men in the
socks department who sell off job-lots from highly
moral motives. Says William :
I know no-better employment than that of a draper for
training a young man in social and commercial accom-
plishments. He comes into contact with refinement, to
which instinctively and officially he must pay respect,
that (sic) unconsciously, he absorbs some of the essence
of refinement; and may by a wise selection choose that
form of it which his judgment and expediency suggest as
the most useful to him.
In late hours hides almost every serpent of malignant
fang, and the peril is that the glistening evils are mis-
taken for the innocuous glow of permissible recreations.
Cosmos is incarnate punctuality. ,
It is not too much to affirm that the daily obligation of
buying and selling, especially selling 1 , can be developed
into an art of the most exquisite potency and pleasure,
and ought to be studied to that end, of course with the
The few who never experience the thrill and fascina-
tion of ambition, of the witchery that encircles a young
draper’s future with alluring haloes, cannot unagme the
exhilaration —I had almost said the rapture— which those
feel whose purpose and hope are nourished by the
judicious hand of ambition.
Verily, there are no humorists in the rag-trade.
